 Charles Dickens
Hard Times
BOOK the FIRST - sowing
CHAPTER I - the one thing NEEDFUL
'now, what I want be, fact. teach these boy and girl nothing but fact. fact alone be want in life. plant nothing else, and root out everything else. you can only form the mind of reasoning animal upon fact: nothing else will ever be of any service to they. this be the principle on which I bring up my own child, and this be the principle on which I bring up these child. Stick to Facts, sir!'
the scene be a plain, bare, monotonous vault of a school-room, and the speaker's square forefinger emphasize his observation by underscore every sentence with a line on the schoolmaster's sleeve. the emphasis be help by the speaker's square wall of a forehead, which have his eyebrow for its base, while his eye find commodious cellarage in two dark cave, overshadow by the wall. the emphasis be help by the speaker's mouth, which be wide, thin, and hard set. the emphasis be help by the speaker's voice, which be inflexible, dry, and dictatorial. the emphasis be help by the speaker's hair, which bristle on the skirt of his bald head, a plantation of fir to keep the wind from its shin surface, all cover with knob, like the crust of a plum pie, as if the head have scarcely warehouse-room for the hard fact store inside. the speaker's obstinate carriage, square coat, square leg, square shoulder, - nay, his very neckcloth, train to take he by the throat with an unaccommodating grasp, like a stubborn fact, as it be, - all help the emphasis.
'in this life, we want nothing but fact, sir; nothing but fact!'
the speaker, and the schoolmaster, and the third grown person present, all back a little, and sweep with their eye the incline plane of little vessel then and there arrange in order, ready to have imperial gallon of fact pour into they until they be full to the brim.
CHAPTER II - murder the innocent
THOMAS GRADGRIND, sir. a man of reality. a man of fact and calculation. a man who proceed upon the principle that two and two be four, and nothing over, and who be not to be talk into allow for anything over. Thomas Gradgrind, sir - peremptorily Thomas - Thomas Gradgrind. with a rule and a pair of scale, and the multiplication table always in his pocket, sir, ready to weigh and measure any parcel of human nature, and tell you exactly what it come to. it be a mere question of figure, a case of simple arithmetic. you might hope to get some other nonsensical belief into the head of George Gradgrind, or Augustus Gradgrind, or John Gradgrind, or Joseph Gradgrind (all supposititious, non-existent person), but into the head of Thomas Gradgrind - no, sir!
in such term Mr. Gradgrind always mentally introduce himself, whether to his private circle of acquaintance, or to the public in general. in such term, no doubt, substitute the word 'boy and girl,' for 'sir,' Thomas Gradgrind now present Thomas Gradgrind to the little pitcher before he, who be to be fill so full of fact.
indeed, as he eagerly sparkle at they from the cellarage before mention, he seem a kind of cannon load to the muzzle with fact, and prepare to blow they clean out of the region of childhood at one discharge. he seem a galvanize apparatus, too, charge with a grim mechanical substitute for the tender young imagination that be to be storm away.
'girl number twenty,' say Mr. Gradgrind, squarely point with his square forefinger, 'I donot know that girl. who be that girl?'
'Sissy Jupe, sir,' explain number twenty, blushing, stand up, and curtsey.
'Sissy be not a name,' say Mr. Gradgrind. 'donot call yourself Sissy. call yourself Cecilia.'
'itbe father as call I Sissy, sir,' return the young girl in a trembling voice, and with another curtsey.
'then he have no business to do it,' say Mr. Gradgrind. 'tell he he mustnot. Cecilia Jupe. let I see. what be your father?'
'he belong to the horse-riding, if you please, sir.'
Mr. Gradgrind frown, and wave off the objectionable calling with his hand.
'we donot want to know anything about that, here. you mustnot tell we about that, here. your father break horse, donot he?'
'if you please, sir, when they can get any to break, they do break horse in the ring, sir.'
'you mustnot tell we about the ring, here. very well, then. describe your father as a horsebreaker. he doctor sick horse, I dare say?'
'oh yes, sir.'
'very well, then. he be a veterinary surgeon, a farrier, and horsebreaker. give I your definition of a horse.'
(Sissy Jupe throw into the great alarm by this demand.)
'girl number twenty unable to define a horse!' say Mr. Gradgrind, for the general behoof of all the little pitcher. 'girl number twenty possess of no fact, in reference to one of the commonest of animal! some boy's definition of a horse. Bitzer, your.'
the square finger, move here and there, light suddenly on Bitzer, perhaps because he chance to sit in the same ray of sunlight which, dart in at one of the bare window of the intensely white-washed room, irradiate Sissy. for, the boy and girl sit on the face of the incline plane in two compact body, divide up the centre by a narrow interval; and Sissy, be at the corner of a row on the sunny side, come in for the beginning of a sunbeam, of which Bitzer, be at the corner of a row on the other side, a few row in advance, catch the end. but, whereas the girl be so dark-eyed and dark-haired, that she seem to receive a deep and more lustrous colour from the sun, when it shine upon she, the boy be so light-eyed and light-haired that the self-same ray appear to draw out of he what little colour he ever possess. his cold eye would hardly have be eye, but for the short end of lash which, by bring they into immediate contrast with something pale than themselves, express their form. his short-cropped hair might have be a mere continuation of the sandy freckle on his forehead and face. his skin be so unwholesomely deficient in the natural tinge, that he look as though, if he be cut, he would bleed white.
'Bitzer,' say Thomas Gradgrind. 'your definition of a horse.'
'quadrupe. Graminivorous. forty tooth, namely twenty-four grinder, four eye-tooth, and twelve incisive. shed coat in the spring; in marshy country, shed hoof, too. hoof hard, but require to be shod with iron. age know by mark in mouth.' thus (and much more) Bitzer.
'now girl number twenty,' say Mr. Gradgrind. 'you know what a horse be.'
she curtsey again, and would have blush deeply, if she could have blush deep than she have blush all this time. Bitzer, after rapidly blink at Thomas Gradgrind with both eye at once, and so catch the light upon his quivering end of lash that they look like the antennae of busy insect, put his knuckle to his freckled forehead, and sit down again.
the third gentleman now step forth. a mighty man at cut and dry, he be; a government officer; in his way (and in most other people's too), a professed pugilist; always in training, always with a system to force down the general throat like a bolus, always to be hear of at the bar of his little public-office, ready to fight all England. to continue in fistic phraseology, he have a genius for come up to the scratch, wherever and whatever it be, and prove himself an ugly customer. he would go in and damage any subject whatever with his right, follow up with his left, stop, exchange, counter, bear his opponent (he always fight all England) to the rope, and fall upon he neatly. he be certain to knock the wind out of common sense, and render that unlucky adversary deaf to the call of time. and he have it in charge from high authority to bring about the great public-office Millennium, when commissioner should reign upon earth.
'very well,' say this gentleman, briskly smile, and fold his arm. 'thatbe a horse. now, let I ask you girl and boy, would you paper a room with representation of horse?'
after a pause, one half of the child cry in chorus, 'yes, sir!' upon which the other half, see in the gentleman's face that yes be wrong, cry out in chorus, 'no, sir!' - as the custom be, in these examination.
'of course, no. why wouldnot you?'
a pause. one corpulent slow boy, with a wheezy manner of breathing, venture the answer, because he wouldnot paper a room at all, but would paint it.
'you must paper it,' say the gentleman, rather warmly.
'you must paper it,' say Thomas Gradgrind, 'whether you like it or not. donot tell we you wouldnot paper it. what do you mean, boy?'
'Iwill explain to you, then,' say the gentleman, after another and a dismal pause, 'why you wouldnot paper a room with representation of horse. do you ever see horse walk up and down the side of room in reality - in fact? do you?'
'yes, sir!' from one half. 'no, sir!' from the other.
'of course no,' say the gentleman, with an indignant look at the wrong half. 'why, then, you be not to see anywhere, what you donot see in fact; you be not to have anywhere, what you donot have in fact. what be call Taste, be only another name for Fact.' Thomas Gradgrind nod his approbation.
'this be a new principle, a discovery, a great discovery,' say the gentleman. 'now, Iwill try you again. suppose you be go to carpet a room. would you use a carpet have a representation of flower upon it?'
there be a general conviction by this time that 'no, sir!' be always the right answer to this gentleman, the chorus of NO be very strong. only a few feeble straggler say yes: among they Sissy Jupe.
'girl number twenty,' say the gentleman, smile in the calm strength of knowledge.
Sissy blush, and stand up.
'so you would carpet your room - or your husband's room, if you be a grown woman, and have a husband - with representation of flower, would you?' say the gentleman. 'why would you?'
'if you please, sir, I be very fond of flower,' return the girl.
'and be that why you would put table and chair upon they, and have people walk over they with heavy boot?'
'it wouldnot hurt they, sir. they wouldnot crush and wither, if you please, sir. they would be the picture of what be very pretty and pleasant, and I would fancy - '
'ay, ay, ay! but you mustnot fancy,' cry the gentleman, quite elated by come so happily to his point. 'thatbe it! you be never to fancy.'
'you be not, Cecilia Jupe,' Thomas Gradgrind solemnly repeat, 'to do anything of that kind.'
'fact, fact, fact!' say the gentleman. and 'fact, fact, fact!' repeat Thomas Gradgrind.
'you be to be in all thing regulate and govern,' say the gentleman, 'by fact. we hope to have, before long, a board of fact, compose of commissioner of fact, who will force the people to be a people of fact, and of nothing but fact. you must discard the word Fancy altogether. you have nothing to do with it. you be not to have, in any object of use or ornament, what would be a contradiction in fact. you donot walk upon flower in fact; you cannot be allow to walk upon flower in carpet. you donot find that foreign bird and butterfly come and perch upon your crockery; you cannot be permit to paint foreign bird and butterfly upon your crockery. you never meet with quadruped go up and down wall; you must not have quadruped represent upon wall. you must use,' say the gentleman, 'for all these purpose, combination and modification (in primary colour) of mathematical figure which be susceptible of proof and demonstration. this be the new discovery. this be fact. this be taste.'
the girl curtsey, and sit down. she be very young, and she look as if she be frighten by the matter-of-fact prospect the world afford.
'now, if Mr. M'Choakumchild,' say the gentleman, 'will proceed to give his first lesson here, Mr. Gradgrind, I shall be happy, at your request, to observe his mode of procedure.'
Mr. Gradgrind be much oblige. 'Mr. M'Choakumchild, we only wait for you.' so, Mr. M'Choakumchild begin in his good manner. he and some one hundred and forty other schoolmaster, have be lately turn at the same time, in the same factory, on the same principle, like so many pianoforte leg. he have be put through an immense variety of pace, and have answer volume of head-break question. Orthography, etymology, syntax, and prosody, biography, astronomy, geography, and general cosmography, the science of compound proportion, algebra, land-surveying and levelling, vocal music, and draw from model, be all at the end of his ten chill finger. he have work his stony way into her Majesty's most honourable Privy Council's Schedule B, and have take the bloom off the high branch of mathematic and physical science, French, German, Latin, and Greek. he know all about all the Water Sheds of all the world (whatever they be), and all the history of all the people, and all the name of all the river and mountain, and all the production, manner, and custom of all the country, and all their boundary and bearing on the two and thirty point of the compass. ah, rather overdone, M'Choakumchild. if he have only learn a little less, how infinitely well he might have teach much more!
he go to work in this preparatory lesson, not unlike Morgiana in the Forty Thieves: look into all the vessel range before he, one after another, to see what they contain. Say, good M'Choakumchild. when from thy boiling store, thou shalt fill each jar brim full by-and-by, dost thou think that thou wilt always kill outright the robber Fancy lurking within - or sometimes only maim he and distort he!
CHAPTER iii - A LOOPHOLE
MR. GRADGRIND walk homeward from the school, in a state of considerable satisfaction. it be his school, and he intend it to be a model. he intend every child in it to be a model - just as the young Gradgrinds be all model.
there be five young Gradgrinds, and they be model every one. they have be lecture at, from their tender year; course, like little hare. almost as soon as they could run alone, they have be make to run to the lecture-room. the first object with which they have an association, or of which they have a remembrance, be a large black board with a dry Ogre chalk ghastly white figure on it.
not that they know, by name or nature, anything about an Ogre fact forbid! I only use the word to express a monster in a lecturing castle, with Heaven know how many head manipulate into one, take childhood captive, and drag it into gloomy statistical den by the hair.
no little Gradgrind have ever see a face in the moon; it be up in the moon before it could speak distinctly. no little Gradgrind have ever learn the silly jingle, Twinkle, twinkle, little star; how I wonder what you be! no little Gradgrind have ever know wonder on the subject, each little Gradgrind have at five year old dissect the Great Bear like a Professor Owen, and drive Charles's Wain like a locomotive engine-driver. no little Gradgrind have ever associate a cow in a field with that famous cow with the crumpled horn who toss the dog who worry the cat who kill the rat who eat the malt, or with that yet more famous cow who swallow Tom Thumb: it have never hear of those celebrity, and have only be introduce to a cow as a graminivorous ruminating quadrupe with several stomach.
to his matter-of-fact home, which be call Stone Lodge, Mr. Gradgrind direct his step. he have virtually retire from the wholesale hardware trade before he build Stone Lodge, and be now look about for a suitable opportunity of make an arithmetical figure in Parliament. Stone Lodge be situate on a moor within a mile or two of a great town - call Coketown in the present faithful guide-book.
a very regular feature on the face of the country, Stone Lodge be. not the least disguise tone down or shade off that uncompromise fact in the landscape. a great square house, with a heavy portico darken the principal window, as its master's heavy brow overshadow his eye. a calculate, cast up, balanced, and prove house. six window on this side of the door, six on that side; a total of twelve in this wing, a total of twelve in the other wing; four-and-twenty carry over to the back wing. a lawn and garden and an infant avenue, all rule straight like a botanical account- book. gas and ventilation, drainage and water-service, all of the prime quality. iron clamp and girder, fire-proof from top to bottom; mechanical lift for the housemaid, with all their brush and broom; everything that heart could desire.
everything? well, I suppose so. the little Gradgrinds have cabinet in various department of science too. they have a little conchological cabinet, and a little metallurgical cabinet, and a little mineralogical cabinet; and the specimen be all arrange and label, and the bit of stone and ore look as though they might have be break from the parent substance by those tremendously hard instrument their own name; and, to paraphrase the idle legend of Peter Piper, who have never find his way into their nursery, if the greedy little Gradgrinds grasp at more than this, what be it for good gracious goodness' sake, that the greedy little Gradgrinds grasp it!
their father walk on in a hopeful and satisfied frame of mind. he be an affectionate father, after his manner; but he would probably have describe himself (if he have be put, like Sissy Jupe, upon a definition) as 'an eminently practical' father. he have a particular pride in the phrase eminently practical, which be consider to have a special application to he. Whatsoever the public meeting hold in Coketown, and whatsoever the subject of such meeting, some Coketowner be sure to seize the occasion of allude to his eminently practical friend Gradgrind. this always please the eminently practical friend. he know it to be his due, but his due be acceptable.
he have reach the neutral ground upon the outskirt of the town, which be neither town nor country, and yet be either spoil, when his ear be invade by the sound of music. the clash and bang band attach to the horse-rid establishment, which have there set up its rest in a wooden pavilion, be in full bray. a flag, float from the summit of the temple, proclaim to mankind that it be 'Sleary's horse-riding' which claim their suffrage. Sleary himself, a stout modern statue with a money-box at its elbow, in an ecclesiastical niche of early gothic architecture, take the money. Miss Josephine Sleary, as some very long and very narrow strip of print bill announce, be then inaugurate the entertainment with her graceful equestrian Tyrolean flower-act. among the other pleasing but always strictly moral wonder which must be see to be believe, Signor Jupe be that afternoon to 'elucidate the divert accomplishment of his highly train perform dog Merrylegs.' he be also to exhibit 'his astounding feat of throw seventy-five hundred-weight in rapid succession backhand over his head, thus form a fountain of solid iron in mid-air, a feat never before attempt in this or any other country, and which have elicit such rapturous plaudit from enthusiastic throng it cannot be withdraw.' the same Signor Jupe be to 'enliven the varied performance at frequent interval with his chaste Shaksperean quip and retort.' lastly, he be to wind they up by appear in his favourite character of Mr. William Button, of Tooley Street, in 'the highly novel and laughable hippo- comedietta of the Tailor's Journey to Brentford.'
Thomas Gradgrind take no heed of these triviality of course, but pass on as a practical man ought to pass on, either brush the noisy insect from his thought, or consign they to the House of Correction. but, the turning of the road take he by the back of the booth, and at the back of the booth a number of child be congregate in a number of stealthy attitude, strive to peep in at the hide glory of the place.
this bring he to a stop. 'now, to think of these vagabond,' say he, 'attract the young rabble from a model school.'
a space of stunt grass and dry rubbish be between he and the young rabble, he take his eyeglass out of his waistcoat to look for any child he know by name, and might order off. Phenomenon almost incredible though distinctly see, what do he then behold but his own metallurgical Louisa, peep with all her might through a hole in a deal board, and his own mathematical Thomas abase himself on the ground to catch but a hoof of the graceful equestrian Tyrolean flower-act!
dumb with amazement, Mr. Gradgrind cross to the spot where his family be thus disgrace, lay his hand upon each err child, and say:
'Louisa!! Thomas!!'
both rise, red and disconcert. but, Louisa look at her father with more boldness than Thomas do. indeed, Thomas do not look at he, but give himself up to be take home like a machine.
'in the name of wonder, idleness, and folly!' say Mr. Gradgrind, lead each away by a hand; 'what do you do here?'
'want to see what it be like,' return Louisa, shortly.
'what it be like?'
'yes, father.'
there be an air of jade sullenness in they both, and particularly in the girl: yet, struggle through the dissatisfaction of her face, there be a light with nothing to rest upon, a fire with nothing to burn, a starve imagination keep life in itself somehow, which brighten its expression. not with the brightness natural to cheerful youth, but with uncertain, eager, doubtful flash, which have something painful in they, analogous to the change on a blind face grope its way.
she be a child now, of fifteen or sixteen; but at no distant day would seem to become a woman all at once. her father think so as he look at she. she be pretty. would have be self-willed (he think in his eminently practical way) but for her bringing-up.
'Thomas, though I have the fact before I, I find it difficult to believe that you, with your education and resource, should have bring your sister to a scene like this.'
'I bring he, father,' say Louisa, quickly. 'I ask he to come.'
'I be sorry to hear it. I be very sorry indeed to hear it. it make Thomas no well, and it make you bad, Louisa.'
she look at her father again, but no tear fall down her cheek.
'you! Thomas and you, to whom the circle of the science be open; Thomas and you, who may be say to be replete with fact; Thomas and you, who have be train to mathematical exactness; Thomas and you, here!' cry Mr. Gradgrind. 'in this degraded position! I be amazed.'
'I be tired, father. I have be tired a long time,' say Louisa.
'tired? of what?' ask the astonished father.
'I donot know of what - of everything, I think.'
'say not another word,' return Mr. Gradgrind. 'you be childish. I will hear no more.' he do not speak again until they have walk some half-a-mile in silence, when he gravely break out with: 'what would your good friend say, Louisa? do you attach no value to their good opinion? what would Mr. Bounderby say?' at the mention of this name, his daughter steal a look at he, remarkable for its intense and search character. he see nothing of it, for before he look at she, she have again cast down her eye!
'what,' he repeat presently, 'would Mr. Bounderby say?' all the way to Stone Lodge, as with grave indignation he lead the two delinquent home, he repeat at interval 'what would Mr. Bounderby say?' - as if Mr. Bounderby have be Mrs. Grundy.
CHAPTER IV - MR. BOUNDERBY
not be Mrs. Grundy, who be Mr. Bounderby?
why, Mr. Bounderby be as near be Mr. Gradgrind's bosom friend, as a man perfectly devoid of sentiment can approach that spiritual relationship towards another man perfectly devoid of sentiment. so near be Mr. Bounderby - or, if the reader should prefer it, so far off.
he be a rich man: banker, merchant, manufacturer, and what not. a big, loud man, with a stare, and a metallic laugh. a man make out of a coarse material, which seem to have be stretch to make so much of he. a man with a great puff head and forehead, swell vein in his temple, and such a strained skin to his face that it seem to hold his eye open, and lift his eyebrow up. a man with a pervade appearance on he of be inflate like a balloon, and ready to start. a man who could never sufficiently vaunt himself a self-make man. a man who be always proclaim, through that brassy speaking-trumpet of a voice of his, his old ignorance and his old poverty. a man who be the Bully of humility.
a year or two young than his eminently practical friend, Mr. Bounderby look old; his seven or eight and forty might have have the seven or eight add to it again, without surprising anybody. he have not much hair. one might have fancy he have talk it off; and that what be leave, all stand up in disorder, be in that condition from be constantly blow about by his windy boastfulness.
in the formal drawing-room of Stone Lodge, stand on the hearthrug, warm himself before the fire, Mr. Bounderby deliver some observation to Mrs. Gradgrind on the circumstance of its be his birthday. he stand before the fire, partly because it be a cool spring afternoon, though the sun shine; partly because the shade of Stone Lodge be always haunt by the ghost of damp mortar; partly because he thus take up a commanding position, from which to subdue Mrs. Gradgrind.
'I havenot a shoe to my foot. as to a stocking, I donot know such a thing by name. I pass the day in a ditch, and the night in a pigsty. thatbe the way I spend my tenth birthday. not that a ditch be new to I, for I be bear in a ditch.'
Mrs. Gradgrind, a little, thin, white, pink-eyed bundle of shawl, of surpass feebleness, mental and bodily; who be always take physic without any effect, and who, whenever she show a symptom of come to life, be invariably stun by some weighty piece of fact tumble on she; Mrs. Gradgrind hop it be a dry ditch?
'no! as wet as a sop. a foot of water in it,' say Mr. Bounderby.
'enough to give a baby cold,' Mrs. Gradgrind consider.
'Cold? I be bear with inflammation of the lung, and of everything else, I believe, that be capable of inflammation,' return Mr. Bounderby. 'for year, ma'am, I be one of the most miserable little wretch ever see. I be so sickly, that I be always moan and groaning. I be so ragged and dirty, that you wouldnot have touch I with a pair of tong.'
Mrs. Gradgrind faintly look at the tong, as the most appropriate thing her imbecility could think of do.
'how I fight through it, I donot know,' say Bounderby. 'I be determine, I suppose. I have be a determined character in later life, and I suppose I be then. here I be, Mrs. Gradgrind, anyhow, and nobody to thank for my be here, but myself.'
Mrs. Gradgrind meekly and weakly hop that his mother -
'my mother? Bolted, ma'am!' say Bounderby.
Mrs. Gradgrind, stun as usual, collapse and give it up.
'my mother leave I to my grandmother,' say Bounderby; 'and, accord to the good of my remembrance, my grandmother be the wicked and the bad old woman that ever live. if I get a little pair of shoe by any chance, she would take them off and sell them for drink. why, I have know that grandmother of mine lie in her bed and drink her four-teen glass of liquor before breakfast!'
Mrs. Gradgrind, weakly smiling, and give no other sign of vitality, look (as she always do) like an indifferently execute transparency of a small female figure, without enough light behind it.
'she keep a chandler's shop,' pursue Bounderby, 'and keep I in an egg-box. that be the cot of my infancy; an old egg-box. as soon as I be big enough to run away, of course I run away. then I become a young vagabond; and instead of one old woman knock I about and starve I, everybody of all age knock I about and starve I. they be right; they have no business to do anything else. I be a nuisance, an incumbrance, and a pest. I know that very well.'
his pride in have at any time of his life achieve such a great social distinction as to be a nuisance, an incumbrance, and a pest, be only to be satisfied by three sonorous repetition of the boast.
'I be to pull through it, I suppose, Mrs. Gradgrind. whether I be to do it or not, ma'am, I do it. I pull through it, though nobody throw I out a rope. Vagabond, errand-boy, vagabond, labourer, porter, clerk, chief manager, small partner, Josiah Bounderby of Coketown. those be the antecedent, and the culmination. Josiah Bounderby of Coketown learn his letter from the outside of the shop, Mrs. Gradgrind, and be first able to tell the time upon a dial-plate, from study the steeple clock of St. Giles's Church, London, under the direction of a drunken cripple, who be a convict thief, and an incorrigible vagrant. tell Josiah Bounderby of Coketown, of your district school and your model school, and your training school, and your whole kettle-of-fish of school; and Josiah Bounderby of Coketown, tell you plainly, all right, all correct - he havenot such advantage - but let we have hard-head, solid-fiste people - the education that make he willnot do for everybody, he know well - such and such his education be, however, and you may force he to swallow boiling fat, but you shall never force he to suppress the fact of his life.'
be heat when he arrive at this climax, Josiah Bounderby of Coketown stop. he stop just as his eminently practical friend, still accompany by the two young culprit, enter the room. his eminently practical friend, on see he, stop also, and give Louisa a reproachful look that plainly say, 'behold your Bounderby!'
'well!' bluster Mr. Bounderby, 'whatbe the matter? what be young Thomas in the dump about?'
he speak of young Thomas, but he look at Louisa.
'we be peep at the circus,' muttered Louisa, haughtily, without lift up her eye, 'and father catch we.'
'and, Mrs. Gradgrind,' say her husband in a lofty manner, 'I should as soon have expect to find my child read poetry.'
'dear I,' whimper Mrs. Gradgrind. 'how can you, Louisa and Thomas! I wonder at you. I declare yoube enough to make one regret ever have have a family at all. I have a great mind to say I wish I havenot. then what would you have do, I should like to know?'
Mr. Gradgrind do not seem favourably impress by these cogent remark. he frown impatiently.
'as if, with my head in its present throbbing state, you couldnot go and look at the shell and mineral and thing provide for you, instead of circus!' say Mrs. Gradgrind. 'you know, as well as I do, no young people have circus master, or keep circus in cabinet, or attend lecture about circus. what can you possibly want to know of circus then? I be sure you have enough to do, if thatbe what you want. with my head in its present state, I couldnot remember the mere name of half the fact you have get to attend to.'
'thatbe the reason!' pout Louisa.
'donot tell I thatbe the reason, because it cannot be nothing of the sort,' say Mrs. Gradgrind. 'go and be somethingological directly.' Mrs. Gradgrind be not a scientific character, and usually dismiss her child to their study with this general injunction to choose their pursuit.
in truth, Mrs. Gradgrind's stock of fact in general be woefully defective; but Mr. Gradgrind in raise she to her high matrimonial position, have be influence by two reason. firstly, she be most satisfactory as a question of figure; and, secondly, she have 'no nonsense' about she. by nonsense he mean fancy; and truly it be probable she be as free from any alloy of that nature, as any human be not arrive at the perfection of an absolute idiot, ever be.
the simple circumstance of be leave alone with her husband and Mr. Bounderby, be sufficient to stun this admirable lady again without collision between herself and any other fact. so, she once more die away, and nobody mind she.
'Bounderby,' say Mr. Gradgrind, draw a chair to the fireside, 'you be always so interested in my young people - particularly in Louisa - that I make no apology for say to you, I be very much vex by this discovery. I have systematically devote myself (as you know) to the education of the reason of my family. the reason be (as you know) the only faculty to which education should be address. 'and yet, Bounderby, it would appear from this unexpected circumstance of to-day, though in itself a trifling one, as if something have creep into Thomas's and Louisa's mind which be - or rather, which be not - I donot know that I can express myself well than by say - which have never be intend to be develop, and in which their reason have no part.'

CHAPTER V - THE KEYNOTE
COKETOWN, to which Messrs. Bounderby and Gradgrind now walk, be a triumph of fact; it have no great taint of fancy in it than Mrs. Gradgrind herself. let we strike the key-note, Coketown, before pursue our tune.
it be a town of red brick, or of brick that would have be red if the smoke and ash have allow it; but as matter stand, it be a town of unnatural red and black like the paint face of a savage. it be a town of machinery and tall chimney, out of which interminable serpent of smoke trail themselves for ever and ever, and never get uncoil. it have a black canal in it, and a river that run purple with ill-smell dye, and vast pile of build full of window where there be a rattle and a trembling all day long, and where the piston of the steam-engine work monotonously up and down, like the head of an elephant in a state of melancholy madness. it contain several large street all very like one another, and many small street still more like one another, inhabit by people equally like one another, who all go in and out at the same hour, with the same sound upon the same pavement, to do the same work, and to whom every day be the same as yesterday and to-morrow, and every year the counterpart of the last and the next.
these attribute of Coketown be in the main inseparable from the work by which it be sustain; against they be to be set off, comfort of life which find their way all over the world, and elegancie of life which make, we will not ask how much of the fine lady, who could scarcely bear to hear the place mention. the rest of its feature be voluntary, and they be these.
you see nothing in Coketown but what be severely workful. if the member of a religious persuasion build a chapel there - as the member of eighteen religious persuasion have do - they make it a pious warehouse of red brick, with sometimes (but this be only in highly ornamental example) a bell in a birdcage on the top of it. the solitary exception be the New Church; a stuccoed edifice with a square steeple over the door, terminate in four short pinnacle like florid wooden leg. all the public inscription in the town be paint alike, in severe character of black and white. the jail might have be the infirmary, the infirmary might have be the jail, the town-hall might have be either, or both, or anything else, for anything that appear to the contrary in the grace of their construction. fact, fact, fact, everywhere in the material aspect of the town; fact, fact, fact, everywhere in the immaterial. the M'Choakumchild school be all fact, and the school of design be all fact, and the relation between master and man be all fact, and everything be fact between the lie-in hospital and the cemetery, and what you couldnot state in figure, or show to be purchaseable in the cheap market and saleable in the dearest, be not, and never should be, world without end, amen.
a town so sacred to fact, and so triumphant in its assertion, of course get on well? why no, not quite well. no? dear I!
no. Coketown do not come out of its own furnace, in all respect like gold that have stand the fire. first, the perplexing mystery of the place be, who belong to the eighteen denomination? because, whoever do, the labour people do not. it be very strange to walk through the street on a Sunday morning, and note how few of they the barbarous jangling of bell that be drive the sick and nervous mad, call away from their own quarter, from their own close room, from the corner of their own street, where they lounge listlessly, gaze at all the church and chapel going, as at a thing with which they have no manner of concern. nor be it merely the stranger who notice this, because there be a native organization in Coketown itself, whose member be to be hear of in the House of Commons every session, indignantly petition for act of parliament that should make these people religious by main force. then come the Teetotal Society, who complain that these same people would get drunk, and show in tabular statement that they do get drunk, and prove at tea party that no inducement, human or Divine (except a medal), would induce they to forego their custom of get drunk. then come the chemist and druggist, with other tabular statement, show that when they donot get drunk, they take opium. then come the experienced chaplain of the jail, with more tabular statement, outdo all the previous tabular statement, and show that the same people would resort to low haunt, hide from the public eye, where they hear low singing and see low dancing, and mayhap join in it; and where A. B., age twenty-four next birthday, and commit for eighteen month' solitary, have himself say (not that he have ever show himself particularly worthy of belief) his ruin begin, as he be perfectly sure and confident that otherwise he would have be a tip-top moral speciman. then come Mr. Gradgrind and Mr. Bounderby, the two gentleman at this present moment walk through Coketown, and both eminently practical, who could, on occasion, furnish more tabular statement derive from their own personal experience, and illustrate by case they have know and see, from which it clearly appear - in short, it be the only clear thing in the case - that these same people be a bad lot altogether, gentleman; that do what you would for they they be never thankful for it, gentleman; that they be restless, gentleman; that they never know what they want; that they live upon the good, and buy fresh butter; and insist on Mocha coffee, and reject all but prime part of meat, and yet be eternally dissatisfied and unmanageable. in short, it be the moral of the old nursery fable:
there be an old woman, and what do you think? she live upon nothing but victual and drink; victual and drink be the whole of her diet, and yet this old woman would never be quiet.
be it possible, I wonder, that there be any analogy between the case of the Coketown population and the case of the little Gradgrinds? surely, none of we in our sober sens and acquaint with figure, be to be tell at this time of day, that one of the foremost element in the existence of the Coketown working-people have be for score of year, deliberately set at nought? that there be any Fancy in they demand to be bring into healthy existence instead of struggle on in convulsion? that exactly in the ratio as they work long and monotonously, the craving grow within they for some physical relief - some relaxation, encourage good humour and good spirit, and give they a vent - some recognize holiday, though it be but for an honest dance to a stirring band of music - some occasional light pie in which even M'Choakumchild have no finger - which craving must and would be satisfied aright, or must and would inevitably go wrong, until the law of the Creation be repeal?

CHAPTER VII - MRS. SPARSIT

for, Mrs. Sparsit have not only see different day, but be highly connected. she have a great aunt live in these very time call Lady Scadgers. CHAPTER VIII - NEVER WONDER
LET we strike the key-note again, before pursue the tune.

Herein lie the spring of the mechanical art and mystery of educate the reason without stoop to the cultivation of the sentiment and affection. never wonder. by mean of addition, subtraction, multiplication, and division, settle everything somehow, and never wonder. bring to I, say M'Choakumchild, yonder baby just able to walk, and I will engage that it shall never wonder.
now, besides very many baby just able to walk, there happen to be in Coketown a considerable population of baby who have be walk against time towards the infinite world, twenty, thirty, forty, fifty year and more. these portentous infant be alarm creature to stalk about in any human society, the eighteen denomination incessantly scratch one another's face and pull one another's hair by way of agree on the step to be take for their improvement - which they never do; a surprising circumstance, when the happy adaptation of the mean to the end be consider. still, although they differ in every other particular, conceivable and inconceivable (especially inconceivable), they be pretty well united on the point that these unlucky infant be never to wonder. body number one, say they must take everything on trust. body number two, say they must take everything on political economy. body number three, write leaden little book for they, show how the good grow-up baby invariably get to the Savings-bank, and the bad grow-up baby invariably get transport. body number four, under dreary pretence of be droll (when it be very melancholy indeed), make the shallow pretence of conceal pitfall of knowledge, into which it be the duty of these baby to be smuggle and inveigle. but, all the body agree that they be never to wonder.
there be a library in Coketown, to which general access be easy. 'I be sick of my life, Loo. I, hate it altogether, and I hate everybody except you,' say the unnatural young Thomas Gradgrind in the hair-cut chamber at twilight.

I donot know what this - jolly old - jaundice jail,' Tom have pause to find a sufficiently complimentary and expressive name for the parental roof, and seem to relieve his mind for a moment by the strong alliteration of this one, 'would be without you.'
'indeed, Tom? do you really and truly say so?'
'why, of course I do. whatbe the use of talk about it!' return Tom, chafe his face on his coat-sleeve, as if to mortify his flesh, and have it in unison with his spirit.
'because, Tom,' say his sister, after silently watch the spark awhile, 'as I get old, and nearer grow up, I often sit wonder here, and think how unfortunate it be for I that I cannot reconcile you to home well than I be able to do. I donot know what other girl know. I cannot play to you, or sing to you. I cannot talk to you so as to lighten your mind, for I never see any amusing sight or read any amusing book that it would be a pleasure or a relief to you to talk about, when you be tired.'
'well, no more do I. I be as bad as you in that respect; and I be a Mule too, which yoube not. if father be determined to make I either a Prig or a Mule, and I be not a Prig, why, it stand to reason, I must be a Mule. and so I be,' say Tom, desperately.
'itbe a great pity,' say Louisa, after another pause, and speak thoughtfully out of her dark corner: 'itbe a great pity, Tom. itbe very unfortunate for both of we.'
'oh! you,' say Tom; 'you be a girl, Loo, and a girl come out of it well than a boy do. I donot miss anything in you. you be the only pleasure I have - you can brighten even this place - and you can always lead I as you like.'
'you be a dear brother, Tom; and while you think I can do such thing, I donot so much mind know well. though I do know well, Tom, and be very sorry for it.' she come and kiss he, and go back into her corner again.
'I wish I could collect all the fact we hear so much about,' say Tom, spitefully set his tooth, 'and all the figure, and all the people who find they out: and I wish I could put a thousand barrel of gunpowder under they, and blow they all up together! however, when I go to live with old Bounderby, Iwill have my revenge.'
'your revenge, Tom?'
'I mean, Iwill enjoy myself a little, and go about and see something, and hear something. Iwill recompense myself for the way in which I have be bring up.'
'but donot disappoint yourself beforehand, Tom. Mr. Bounderby think as father think, and be a great deal rougher, and not half so kind.'
'oh!' say Tom, laugh; 'I donot mind that. I shall very well know how to manage and smooth old Bounderby!'
their shadow be define upon the wall, but those of the high press in the room be all blend together on the wall and on the ceiling, as if the brother and sister be overhang by a dark cavern. or, a fanciful imagination - if such treason could have be there - might have make it out to be the shadow of their subject, and of its lower association with their future.
'what be your great mode of smooth and managing, Tom? be it a secret?'
'oh!' say Tom, 'if it be a secret, itbe not far off. itbe you. you be his little pet, you be his favourite; hewill do anything for you. when he say to I what I donot like, I shall say to he, "my sister Loo will be hurt and disappoint, Mr. Bounderby. she always use to tell I she be sure you would be easy with I than this." thatwill bring he about, or nothing will.'
after wait for some answer remark, and get none, Tom wearily relapse into the present time, and twin himself yawn round and about the rail of his chair, and rumple his head more and more, until he suddenly look up, and ask:
'have you go to sleep, Loo?'
'no, Tom. I be look at the fire.'
'you seem to find more to look at in it than ever I could find,' say Tom. 'another of the advantage, I suppose, of be a girl.'
'Tom,' enquire his sister, slowly, and in a curious tone, as if she be read what she ask in the fire, and it be not quite plainly write there, 'do you look forward with any satisfaction to this change to Mr. Bounderby's?'
'why, therebe one thing to be say of it,' return Tom, push his chair from he, and stand up; 'it will be get away from home.'
'there be one thing to be say of it,' louisa repeat in her former curious tone; 'it will be get away from home. yes.'
'not but what I shall be very unwilling, both to leave you, Loo, and to leave you here. but I must go, you know, whether I like it or not; and I have well go where I can take with I some advantage of your influence, than where I should lose it altogether. donot you see?'
'yes, Tom.'
the answer be so long in come, though there be no indecision in it, that Tom go and lean on the back of her chair, to contemplate the fire which so engross she, from her point of view, and see what he could make of it.
'except that it be a fire,' say Tom, 'it look to I as stupid and blank as everything else look. what do you see in it? not a circus?'
'I donot see anything in it, Tom, particularly. but since I have be look at it, I have be wonder about you and I, grow up.'
'wonder again!' say Tom.

'then I beg of you, Louisa,' say Mrs. Gradgrind, who have open the door without be hear, 'to do nothing of that description, for goodness' sake, you inconsiderate girl, or I shall never hear the last of it from your father. and, Thomas, it be really shameful, with my poor head continually wear I out, that a boy bring up as you have be, and whose education have cost what yours have, should be find encourage his sister to wonder, when he know his father have expressly say that she be not to do it.'

'nonsense!' say Mrs. Gradgrind, render almost energetic. 'nonsense! donot stand there and tell I such stuff, Louisa, to my face, when you know very well that if it be ever to reach your father's ear I should never hear the last of it. after all the trouble that have be take with you! after the lecture you have attend, and the experiment you have see! after I have hear you myself, when the whole of my right side have be benumb, go on with your master about combustion, and calcination, and calorification, and I may say every kind of ation that could drive a poor invalid distract, to hear you talk in this absurd way about spark and ash! I wish,' whimper Mrs. Gradgrind, take a chair, and discharge her strong point before succumb under these mere shadow of fact, 'yes, I really do wish that I have never have a family, and then you would have know what it be to do without I!'
CHAPTER ix - SISSY's PROGRESS
SISSY JUPE have not an easy time of it, between Mr. M'Choakumchild and Mrs. Gradgrind, and be not without strong impulse, in the first month of her probation, to run away. it hail fact all day long so very hard, and life in general be open to she as such a closely rule cipher-book, that assuredly she would have run away, but for only one restraint.
it be lamentable to think of; but this restraint be the result of no arithmetical process, be self-impose in defiance of all calculation, and go dead against any table of probability that any Actuary would have draw up from the premise. the girl believe that her father have not desert she; she live in the hope that he would come back, and in the faith that he would be make the happy by she remain where she be.
the wretched ignorance with which Jupe cling to this consolation, reject the superior comfort of know, on a sound arithmetical basis, that her father be an unnatural vagabond, fill Mr. Gradgrind with pity. yet, what be to be do? M'Choakumchild report that she have a very dense head for figure; that, once possess with a general idea of the globe, she take the smallest conceivable interest in its exact measurement; that she be extremely slow in the acquisition of date, unless some pitiful incident happen to be connect therewith; that she would burst into tear on be require (by the mental process) immediately to name the cost of two hundred and forty-seven muslin cap at fourteen-pence halfpenny; that she be as low down, in the school, as low could be; that after eight week of induction into the element of Political Economy, she have only yesterday be set right by a prattler three foot high, for return to the question, 'what be the first principle of this science?' the absurd answer, 'to do unto other as I would that they should do unto I.'
Mr. Gradgrind observe, shake his head, that all this be very bad; that it show the necessity of infinite grind at the mill of knowledge, as per system, schedule, blue book, report, and tabular statement a to Z; and that Jupe 'must be keep to it.' so Jupe be keep to it, and become low-spirited, but no wiser.
'it would be a fine thing to be you, Miss Louisa!' she say, one night, when Louisa have endeavour to make her perplexity for next day something clear to she.
'do you think so?'
'I should know so much, Miss Louisa. all that be difficult to I now, would be so easy then.'
'you might not be the well for it, Sissy.'
Sissy submit, after a little hesitation, 'I should not be the bad, Miss Louisa.' to which Miss Louisa answer, 'I donot know that.'
there have be so little communication between these two - both because life at Stone Lodge go monotonously round like a piece of machinery which discourage human interference, and because of the prohibition relative to Sissy's past career - that they be still almost stranger. Sissy, with her dark eye wonderingly direct to Louisa's face, be uncertain whether to say more or to remain silent.
'you be more useful to my mother, and more pleasant with she than I can ever be,' Louisa resume. 'you be pleasanter to yourself, than I be to myself.'
'but, if you please, Miss Louisa,' Sissy plead, 'I be - o so stupid!'
Louisa, with a bright laugh than usual, tell she she would be wise by-and-by.
'you donot know,' say Sissy, half cry, 'what a stupid girl I be. all through school hour I make mistake. Mr. and Mrs. M'Choakumchild call I up, over and over again, regularly to make mistake. I cannot help they. they seem to come natural to I.'
'Mr. and Mrs. M'Choakumchild never make any mistake themselves, I suppose, Sissy?'
'o no!' she eagerly return. 'they know everything.'
'tell I some of your mistake.'
'I be almost ashamed,' say Sissy, with reluctance. 'but to-day, for instance, Mr. M'Choakumchild be explain to we about Natural Prosperity.'
'National, I think it must have be,' observed Louisa.
'yes, it be. - but benot it the same?' she timidly ask.
'you have well say, National, as he say so,' return Louisa, with her dry reserve.
'National Prosperity. and he say, now, this schoolroom be a nation. and in this nation, there be fifty million of money. benot this a prosperous nation? girl number twenty, benot this a prosperous nation, and a'n't you in a thriving state?'
'what do you say?' ask Louisa.
'Miss Louisa, I say I donot know. I think I couldnot know whether it be a prosperous nation or not, and whether I be in a thriving state or not, unless I know who have get the money, and whether any of it be mine. but that have nothing to do with it. it be not in the figure at all,' say Sissy, wipe her eye.
'that be a great mistake of your,' observed Louisa.
'yes, Miss Louisa, I know it be, now. then Mr. M'Choakumchild say he would try I again. and he say, this schoolroom be an immense town, and in it there be a million of inhabitant, and only five-and-twenty be starve to death in the street, in the course of a year. what be your remark on that proportion? and my remark be - for I couldnot think of a well one - that I think it must be just as hard upon those who be starve, whether the other be a million, or a million million. and that be wrong, too.'
'of course it be.'
'then Mr. M'Choakumchild say he would try I once more. and he say, here be the stuttering - '
'statistic,' say Louisa.
'yes, Miss Louisa - they always remind I of stuttering, and thatbe another of my mistake - of accident upon the sea. and I find (Mr. M'Choakumchild say) that in a give time a hundred thousand person go to sea on long voyage, and only five hundred of they be drown or burn to death. what be the percentage? and I say, Miss;' here Sissy fairly sob as confess with extreme contrition to her great error; 'I say it be nothing.'
'nothing, Sissy?'

Sissy hesitate before reply, and so plainly show her sense that they be enter on forbid ground, that Louisa add, 'no one hear we; and if any one do, I be sure no harm could be find in such an innocent question.'

'yet he leave you now, Sissy?'
'only for my good. nobody understand he as I do; nobody know he as I do. when he leave I for my good - he never would have leave I for his own - I know he be almost break-hearted with the trial. he will not be happy for a single minute, till he come back.'
'tell I more about he,' say Louisa, 'I will never ask you again. where do you live?'
'we travel about the country, and have no fix place to live in. Father's a;' Sissy whisper the awful word, 'a clown.'
'to make the people laugh?' say Louisa, with a nod of intelligence.

Father's not like most. those who donot know he as well as I do, and donot love he as dearly as I do, might believe he be not quite right. sometimes they play trick upon he; but they never know how he feel they, and shrink up, when he be alone with I. he be far, far timid than they think!'
'and you be his comfort through everything?'
she nod, with the tear roll down her face. 'and your father be always kind? to the last?' ask Louisa contravene the great principle, and wonder very much.

'why be he angry with the dog?' Louisa demand.
'Father, soon after they come home from perform, tell Merrylegs to jump up on the back of the two chair and stand across they - which be one of his trick. he look at father, and donot do it at once. everything of father's have go wrong that night, and he havenot please the public at all. he cry out that the very dog know he be fail, and have no compassion on he. then he beat the dog, and I be frightened, and say, "Father, father! pray donot hurt the creature who be so fond of you! o Heaven forgive you, father, stop!" and he stop, and the dog be bloody, and father lie down cry on the floor with the dog in his arm, and the dog lick his face.'
Louisa see that she be sob; and go to she, kiss she, take her hand, and sit down beside she.
'finish by tell I how your father leave you, Sissy. now that I have ask you so much, tell I the end. the blame, if there be any blame, be mine, not your.'

(as he do sometimes, like they all do), and he say, "a little, my darling." and when I come to stoop down and look up at his face, I see that he be cry. the more I speak to he, the more he hide his face; and at first he shake all over, and say nothing but "my darling;" and "my love!"'

'Iwill come directly.'
'Iwill wait for you,' say Tom, 'to make sure.'

'I say! look sharp for old Bounderby, Loo!' Tom remonstrate.

I keep the nine oil ready for he, and I know he will come back. every letter that I see in Mr. Gradgrind's hand take my breath away and blind my eye, for I think it come from father, or from Mr. Sleary about father. Mr. Sleary promise to write as soon as ever father should be hear of, and I trust to he to keep his word.'
'do look sharp for old Bounderby, Loo!' say Tom, with an impatient whistle. 'hewill be off if you donot look sharp!'
after this, whenever Sissy drop a curtsey to Mr. Gradgrind in the presence of his family, and say in a falter way, 'I beg your pardon, sir, for be troublesome - but - have you have any letter yet about I?' Louisa would suspend the occupation of the moment, whatever it be, and look for the reply as earnestly as Sissy do. and when Mr. Gradgrind regularly answer, 'no, Jupe, nothing of the sort,' the trembling of Sissy's lip would be repeat in Louisa's face, and her eye would follow Sissy with compassion to the door.
