this observation must be limit exclusively to his daughter. as to Tom, he be become that not unprecedented triumph of calculation which be usually at work on number one. as to Mrs. Gradgrind, if she say anything on the subject, she would come a little way out of her wrapper, like a feminine dormouse, and say:
'good gracious bless I, how my poor head be vex and worry by that girl Jupe's so perseveringly ask, over and over again, about her tiresome letter! upon my word and honour I seem to be fat, and destine, and ordain, to live in the midst of thing that I be never to hear the last of. it really be a most extraordinary circumstance that it appear as if I never be to hear the last of anything!'
at about this point, Mr. Gradgrind's eye would fall upon she; and under the influence of that wintry piece of fact, she would become torpid again.
CHAPTER X - STEPHEN BLACKPOOL
I entertain a weak idea that the english people be as hard-work as any people upon whom the sun shin. I acknowledge to this ridiculous idiosyncrasy, as a reason why I would give they a little more play.
in the hard working part of Coketown; in the innermost fortification of that ugly citadel, where Nature be as strongly bricke out as kill air and gas be bricke in; at the heart of the labyrinth of narrow court upon court, and close street upon street, which have come into existence piecemeal, every piece in a violent hurry for some one man's purpose, and the whole an unnatural family, shouldering, and trampling, and press one another to death; in the last close nook of this great exhausted receiver, where the chimney, for want of air to make a draught, be build in an immense variety of stunted and crooked shape, as though every house put out a sign of the kind of people who might be expect to be bear in it; among the multitude of Coketown, generically call 'the Hands,' - a race who would have find more favour with some people, if Providence have see fit to make they only hand, or, like the low creature of the seashore, only hand and stomach - live a certain Stephen Blackpool, forty year of age.
Stephen look old, but he have have a hard life. it be say that every life have its rose and thorn; there seem, however, to have be a misadventure or mistake in Stephen's case, whereby somebody else have become possess of his rose, and he have become possessed of the same somebody else's thorn in addition to his own. he have know, to use his word, a peck of trouble. he be usually call Old Stephen, in a kind of rough homage to the fact.
a rather stoop man, with a knit brow, a ponder expression of face, and a hard-look head sufficiently capacious, on which his iron-grey hair lie long and thin, Old Stephen might have pass for a particularly intelligent man in his condition. yet he be not. he take no place among those remarkable 'hand,' who, piece together their broken interval of leisure through many year, have master difficult science, and acquire a knowledge of most unlikely thing. he hold no station among the Hands who could make speech and carry on debate. thousand of his compeer could talk much well than he, at any time. he be a good power-loom weaver, and a man of perfect integrity. what more he be, or what else he have in he, if anything, let he show for himself.
the light in the great factory, which look, when they be illuminate, like Fairy palace - or the traveller by express- train say so - be all extinguish; and the bell have ring for knock off for the night, and have cease again; and the Hands, man and woman, boy and girl, be clatter home. Old Stephen be stand in the street, with the old sensation upon he which the stoppage of the machinery always produce - the sensation of its have work and stop in his own head.
'yet I donot see Rachael, still!' say he.
it be a wet night, and many group of young woman pass he, with their shawl draw over their bare head and hold close under their chin to keep the rain out. he know Rachael well, for a glance at any one of these group be sufficient to show he that she be not there. at last, there be no more to come; and then he turn away, say in a tone of disappointment, 'why, then, ha' miss she!'
but, he have not go the length of three street, when he see another of the shawled figure in advance of he, at which he look so keenly that perhaps its mere shadow indistinctly reflect on the wet pavement - if he could have see it without the figure itself move along from lamp to lamp, brighten and fade as it go - would have be enough to tell he who be there. make his pace at once much quick and much soft, he dart on until he be very near this figure, then fall into his former walk, and call 'Rachael!'
she turn, be then in the brightness of a lamp; and raise her hood a little, show a quiet oval face, dark and rather delicate, irradiate by a pair of very gentle eye, and far set off by the perfect order of her shin black hair. it be not a face in its first bloom; she be a woman five and thirty year of age.
'ah, lad! 'tis thou?' when she have say this, with a smile which would have be quite express, though nothing of she have be see but her pleasant eye, she replace her hood again, and they go on together.
'I think thou wast ahind I, Rachael?'
'no.'
'early t'night, lass?'
''Times Ibe a little early, Stephen! 'time a little late. Ibe never to be count on, go home.'
'nor go t'other way, neither, 't seem to I, Rachael?'
'no, Stephen.'
he look at she with some disappointment in his face, but with a respectful and patient conviction that she must be right in whatever she do. the expression be not lose upon she; she lay her hand lightly on his arm a moment as if to thank he for it.
'we be such true friend, lad, and such old friend, and get to be such old folk, now.'
'no, Rachael, thou'rt as young as ever thou wast.'
'one of we would be puzzle how to get old, Stephen, without 't other get so too, both be alive,' she answer, laugh; 'but, anyways, webe such old friend, and t' hide a word of honest truth fro' one another would be a sin and a pity. 'tis well not to walk too much together. 'time, yes! 'twould be hard, indeed, if 'twa not to be at all,' she say, with a cheerfulness she seek to communicate to he.
''tis hard, anyways, Rachael.'
'try to think not; and 'twill seem well.'
'Ihave try a long time, and 'ta'nt get well. but thou'rt right; 't might mak fok talk, even of thee. Thou hast be that to I, Rachael, through so many year: thou hast do I so much good, and hearten of I in that cheering way, that thy word be a law to I. ah, lass, and a bright good law! well than some real one.'
'never fret about they, Stephen,' she answer quickly, and not without an anxious glance at his face. 'let the law be.'
'yes,' he say, with a slow nod or two. 'let them be. let everything be. let all sort alone. 'tis a muddle, and thatbe aw.'
'always a muddle?' say Rachael, with another gentle touch upon his arm, as if to recall he out of the thoughtfulness, in which he be bite the long end of his loose neckerchief as he walk along. the touch have its instantaneous effect. he let they fall, turn a smile face upon she, and say, as he break into a good-humour laugh, 'ay, Rachael, lass, awlus a muddle. thatbe where I stick. I come to the muddle many time and agen, and I never get beyond it.'
they have walk some distance, and be near their own home. the woman's be the first reach. it be in one of the many small street for which the favourite undertaker (who turn a handsome sum out of the one poor ghastly pomp of the neighbourhood) keep a black ladder, in order that those who have do their daily groping up and down the narrow stair might slide out of this work world by the window. she stop at the corner, and put her hand in his, wish he good night.
'good night, dear lass; good night!'
she go, with her neat figure and her sober womanly step, down the dark street, and he stand look after she until she turn into one of the small house. there be not a flutter of her coarse shawl, perhaps, but have its interest in this man's eye; not a tone of her voice but have its echo in his innermost heart.
when she be lose to his view, he pursue his homeward way, glance up sometimes at the sky, where the cloud be sail fast and wildly. but, they be break now, and the rain have cease, and the moon shine, - look down the high chimney of Coketown on the deep furnace below, and cast titanic shadow of the steam-engine at rest, upon the wall where they be lodge. the man seem to have brighten with the night, as he go on.
his home, in such another street as the first, save that it be narrow, be over a little shop. how it come to pass that any people find it worth their while to sell or buy the wretched little toy, mix up in its window with cheap newspaper and pork (there be a leg to be raffle for to-morrow-night), matter not here. he take his end of candle from a shelf, light it at another end of candle on the counter, without disturb the mistress of the shop who be asleep in her little room, and go upstairs into his lodging.
it be a room, not unacquainted with the black ladder under various tenant; but as neat, at present, as such a room could be. a few book and writing be on an old bureau in a corner, the furniture be decent and sufficient, and, though the atmosphere be taint, the room be clean.
go to the hearth to set the candle down upon a round three- legged table stand there, he stumble against something. as he recoil, look down at it, it raise itself up into the form of a woman in a sit attitude.
'Heaven's mercy, woman!' he cry, fall far off from the figure. 'hast thou come back again!'
such a woman! a disabled, drunken creature, barely able to preserve her sit posture by steady herself with one begrimed hand on the floor, while the other be so purposeless in try to push away her tangle hair from her face, that it only blind she the more with the dirt upon it. a creature so foul to look at, in her tatter, stain and splash, but so much fouler than that in her moral infamy, that it be a shameful thing even to see she.
after an impatient oath or two, and some stupid clawing of herself with the hand not necessary to her support, she get her hair away from her eye sufficiently to obtain a sight of he. then she sit sway her body to and fro, and make gesture with her unnerved arm, which seem intend as the accompaniment to a fit of laughter, though her face be stolid and drowsy.
'eigh, lad? what, yo'r there?' some hoarse sound mean for this, come mockingly out of she at last; and her head drop forward on her breast.
'back agen?' she screech, after some minute, as if he have that moment say it. 'yes! and back agen. back agen ever and ever so often. back? yes, back. why not?'
rouse by the unmeaning violence with which she cry it out, she scramble up, and stand support herself with her shoulder against the wall; dangle in one hand by the string, a dunghill- fragment of a bonnet, and try to look scornfully at he.
'Iwill sell thee off again, and Iwill sell thee off again, and Iwill sell thee off a score of time!' she cry, with something between a furious menace and an effort at a defiant dance. 'come awa' from th' bed!' he be sit on the side of it, with his face hide in his hand. 'come awa! from 't. 'Tis mine, and Ihave a right to t'!'
as she stagger to it, he avoid she with a shudder, and pass - his face still hide - to the opposite end of the room. she throw herself upon the bed heavily, and soon be snore hard. he sink into a chair, and move but once all that night. it be to throw a covering over she; as if his hand be not enough to hide she, even in the darkness.
CHAPTER XI - no WAY OUT
the Fairy palace burst into illumination, before pale morning show the monstrous serpent of smoke trail themselves over Coketown. a clattering of clogs upon the pavement; a rapid ringing of bell; and all the melancholy mad elephant, polished and oil up for the day's monotony, be at their heavy exercise again.
Stephen bend over his loom, quiet, watchful, and steady. a special contrast, as every man be in the forest of loom where Stephen work, to the crashing, smash, tear piece of mechanism at which he labour. never fear, good people of an anxious turn of mind, that art will consign Nature to oblivion. Set anywhere, side by side, the work of GOD and the work of man; and the former, even though it be a troop of Hands of very small account, will gain in dignity from the comparison.
so many hundred Hands in this Mill; so many hundred horse Steam Power. it be know, to the force of a single pound weight, what the engine will do; but, not all the calculator of the National Debt can tell I the capacity for good or evil, for love or hatred, for patriotism or discontent, for the decomposition of virtue into vice, or the reverse, at any single moment in the soul of one of these its quiet servant, with the compose face and the regulated action. there be no mystery in it; there be an unfathomable mystery in the mean of they, for ever. - supposing we be to reverse our arithmetic for material object, and to govern these awful unknown quantity by other mean!
the day grow strong, and show itself outside, even against the flame light within. the light be turn out, and the work go on. the rain fall, and the smoke-serpent, submissive to the curse of all that tribe, trail themselves upon the earth. in the waste-yard outside, the steam from the escape pipe, the litter of barrel and old iron, the shin heap of coal, the ash everywhere, be shroud in a veil of mist and rain.
the work go on, until the noon-bell ring. more clattering upon the pavement. the loom, and wheel, and hand all out of gear for an hour.
Stephen come out of the hot mill into the damp wind and cold wet street, haggard and worn. he turn from his own class and his own quarter, take nothing but a little bread as he walk along, towards the hill on which his principal employer live, in a red house with black outside shutter, green inside blind, a black street door, up two white step, BOUNDERBY (in letter very like himself) upon a brazen plate, and a round brazen door-handle underneath it, like a brazen full-stop.
Mr. Bounderby be at his lunch. so Stephen have expect. would his servant say that one of the Hands beg leave to speak to he? message in return, require name of such Hand. Stephen Blackpool. there be nothing troublesome against Stephen Blackpool; yes, he might come in.
Stephen Blackpool in the parlour. Mr. Bounderby (whom he just know by sight), at lunch on chop and sherry. Mrs. Sparsit net at the fireside, in a side-saddle attitude, with one foot in a cotton stirrup. it be a part, at once of Mrs. Sparsit's dignity and service, not to lunch. she supervise the meal officially, but imply that in her own stately person she consider lunch a weakness.
'now, Stephen,' say Mr. Bounderby, 'whatbe the matter with you?'
Stephen make a bow. not a servile one - these Hands will never do that! Lord bless you, sir, youwill never catch they at that, if they have be with you twenty year! - and, as a complimentary toilet for Mrs. Sparsit, tuck his neckerchief end into his waistcoat.
'now, you know,' say Mr. Bounderby, take some sherry, 'we have never have any difficulty with you, and you have never be one of the unreasonable one. you donot expect to be set up in a coach and six, and to be feed on turtle soup and venison, with a gold spoon, as a good many of them do!' Mr. Bounderby always represent this to be the sole, immediate, and direct object of any Hand who be not entirely satisfied; 'and therefore I know already that you have not come here to make a complaint. now, you know, I be certain of that, beforehand.'
'no, sir, sure I ha' not coom for nowt o' th' kind.'
Mr. Bounderby seem agreeably surprised, notwithstanding his previous strong conviction. 'very well,' he return. 'yoube a steady hand, and I be not mistaken. now, let I hear what itbe all about. as itbe not that, let I hear what it be. what have you get to say? out with it, lad!'
Stephen happen to glance towards Mrs. Sparsit. 'I can go, Mr. Bounderby, if you wish it,' say that self-sacrifice lady, make a feint of take her foot out of the stirrup.
Mr. Bounderby stay she, by hold a mouthful of chop in suspension before swallow it, and put out his left hand. then, withdraw his hand and swallow his mouthful of chop, he say to Stephen:
'now you know, this good lady be a bear lady, a high lady. you be not to suppose because she keep my house for I, that she havenot be very high up the tree - ah, up at the top of the tree! now, if you have get anything to say that cannot be say before a bear lady, this lady will leave the room. if what you have get to say can be say before a bear lady, this lady will stay where she be.'
'Sir, I hope I never have nowt to say, not fitten for a bear lady to year, sin' I be bear mysen',' be the reply, accompany with a slight flush.
'very well,' say Mr. Bounderby, push away his plate, and lean back. 'fire away!'
'I ha' coom,' Stephen begin, raise his eye from the floor, after a moment's consideration, 'to ask yo yor advice. I need 't overmuch. I be married on Eas'r Monday nineteen year sin, long and dree. she be a young lass - pretty enow - wi' good account of herseln. well! she go bad - soon. not along of I. Gonnows I be not a unkind husband to she.'
'I have hear all this before,' say Mr. Bounderby. 'she take to drinking, leave off work, sell the furniture, pawn the clothe, and play old Gooseberry.'
'I be patient wi' she.'
('the more fool you, I think,' say Mr. Bounderby, in confidence to his wine-glass.)
'I be very patient wi' she. I try to wean her fra 't ower and ower agen. I try this, I try that, I try t'other. I ha' go home, manybe the time, and find all vanish as I have in the world, and she without a sense leave to bless herseln lie on bare ground. I ha' dun 't not once, not twice - twenty time!'
every line in his face deepen as he say it, and put in its affect evidence of the suffering he have undergo.
'from bad to bad, from bad to worsen. she leave I. she disgrace herseln everyway, bitter and bad. she coom back, she coom back, she coom back. what could I do t' hinder she? I ha' walk the street night long, ere ever Iwould go home. I ha' go t' th' brigg, minded to fle myseln ower, and ha' no more on't. I ha' bore that much, that I be owd when I be young.'
Mrs. Sparsit, easily amble along with her netting-needle, raise the coriolanian eyebrow and shake her head, as much as to say, 'the great know trouble as well as the small. please to turn your humble eye in my direction.'
'I ha' pay she to keep awa' fra' I. these five year I ha' pay she. I ha' get decent fewtril about I agen. I ha' live hard and sad, but not ashamed and fearfo' a' the minnit o' my life. last night, I go home. there she lie upon my har-stone! there she be!'
in the strength of his misfortune, and the energy of his distress, he fire for the moment like a proud man. in another moment, he stand as he have stand all the time - his usual stoop upon he; his ponder face address to Mr. Bounderby, with a curious expression on it, half shrewd, half perplexed, as if his mind be set upon unravel something very difficult; his hat hold tight in his left hand, which rest on his hip; his right arm, with a rugged propriety and force of action, very earnestly emphasize what he say: not least so when it always pause, a little bent, but not withdraw, as he pause.
'I be acquaint with all this, you know,' say Mr. Bounderby, 'except the last clause, long ago. itbe a bad job; thatbe what it be. you have well have be satisfied as you be, and not have get marry. however, itbe too late to say that.'
'be it an unequal marriage, sir, in point of year?' ask Mrs. Sparsit.
'you hear what this lady ask. be it an unequal marriage in point of year, this unlucky job of your?' say Mr. Bounderby.
'not e'en so. I be one-and-twenty myseln; she be twenty nighbut.'
'indeed, sir?' say Mrs. Sparsit to her Chief, with great placidity. 'I infer, from its be so miserable a marriage, that it be probably an unequal one in point of year.'
Mr. Bounderby look very hard at the good lady in a side-long way that have an odd sheepishness about it. he fortify himself with a little more sherry.
'well? why donot you go on?' he then ask, turn rather irritably on Stephen Blackpool.
'I ha' coom to ask yo, sir, how I be to be rid o' this woman.' Stephen infuse a yet deep gravity into the mixed expression of his attentive face. Mrs. Sparsit utter a gentle ejaculation, as have receive a moral shock.
'what do you mean?' say Bounderby, get up to lean his back against the chimney-piece. 'what be you talk about? you take she for well for bad.'
'I mun' be ride o' she. I cannot bear 't nommore. I ha' live under 't so long, for that I ha' had'n the pity and comfort word o' th' good lass living or dead. haply, but for she, I should ha' go batter mad.'
'he wish to be free, to marry the female of whom he speak, I fear, sir,' observe Mrs. Sparsit in an undertone, and much deject by the immorality of the people.
'I do. the lady say whatbe right. I do. I be a come to 't. I ha' read I' th' paper that great folk (fair faw them a'! I wish them no hurt!) be not bond together for well for bad so fast, but that they can be set free fro' their misfortnet marriage, an' marry ower agen. when they dunnot agree, for that their temper be ill-sorted, they have room o' one kind an' another in their house, above a bit, and they can live asunder. we fok ha' only one room, and we cannot. when that willnot do, they ha' gowd an' other cash, an' they can say "this for yo' an' that for I," an' they can go their separate way. we cannot. spite o' all that, they can be set free for small wrong than mine. so, I mun be ride o' this woman, and I want t' know how?'
'no how,' return Mr. Bounderby.
'if I do she any hurt, sir, therebe a law to punish I?'
'of course there be.'
'if I flee from she, therebe a law to punish I?'
'of course there be.'
'if I marry t'oother dear lass, therebe a law to punish I?'
'of course there be.'
'if I be to live wi' her an' not marry she - say such a thing could be, which it never could or would, an' her so good - therebe a law to punish I, in every innocent child belong to I?'
'of course there be.'
'now, a' God's name,' say Stephen Blackpool, 'show I the law to help I!'
'Hem! therebe a sanctity in this relation of life,' say Mr. Bounderby, 'and - and - it must be keep up.'
'no no, dunnot say that, sir. 'tan't kep' up that way. not that way. 'Tis kep' down that way. Ibe a weaver, I be in a fact'ry when a chilt, but I ha' get een to see wi' and eern to year wi'. I read in th' paper every 'Sizes, every Sessions - and you read too - I know it! - with dismay - how th' suppose unpossibility o' ever get unchained from one another, at any price, on any term, bring blood upon this land, and bring many common married fok to battle, murder, and sudden death. let we ha' this, right understood. minebe a grievous case, an' I want - if yo will be so good - t' know the law that help I.'
'now, I tell you what!' say Mr. Bounderby, put his hand in his pocket. 'there be such a law.'
Stephen, subside into his quiet manner, and never wander in his attention, give a nod.
'but itbe not for you at all. it cost money. it cost a mint of money.'
'how much might that be?' Stephen calmly ask.
'why, youwould have to go to doctor' Commons with a suit, and youwould have to go to a court of Common Law with a suit, and youwould have to go to the House of Lords with a suit, and youwould have to get an Act of Parliament to enable you to marry again, and it would cost you (if it be a case of very plain sailing), I suppose from a thousand to fifteen hundred pound,' say Mr. Bounderby. 'perhaps twice the money.'
'therebe no other law?'
'certainly not.'
'why then, sir,' say Stephen, turn white, and motion with that right hand of his, as if he give everything to the four wind, ''tis a muddle. 'tis just a muddle a'toogether, an' the soon I be dead, the well.'
(Mrs. Sparsit again deject by the impiety of the people.)
'Pooh, pooh! donot you talk nonsense, my good fellow,' say Mr. Bounderby, 'about thing you donot understand; and donot you call the institution of your country a muddle, or youwill get yourself into a real muddle one of these fine morning. the institution of your country be not your piece-work, and the only thing you have get to do, be, to mind your piece-work. you donot take your wife for fast and for loose; but for well for bad. if she have turn out bad - why, all we have get to say be, she might have turn out well.'
''tis a muddle,' say Stephen, shake his head as he move to the door. ''tis a' a muddle!'
'now, Iwill tell you what!' Mr. Bounderby resume, as a valedictory address. 'with what I shall call your unhallowed opinion, you have be quite shocking this lady: who, as I have already tell you, be a bear lady, and who, as I have not already tell you, have have her own marriage misfortune to the tune of ten of thousand of pound - ten of thousand of Pounds!' (he repeat it with great relish). 'now, you have always be a steady Hand hitherto; but my opinion be, and so I tell you plainly, that you be turn into the wrong road. you have be listen to some mischievous stranger or other - theybe always about - and the good thing you can do be, to come out of that. now you know;' here his countenance express marvellous acuteness; 'I can see as far into a grindstone as another man; far than a good many, perhaps, because I have my nose well keep to it when I be young. I see trace of the turtle soup, and venison, and gold spoon in this. yes, I do!' cry Mr. Bounderby, shake his head with obstinate cunning. 'by the Lord Harry, I do!'
with a very different shake of the head and deep sigh, Stephen say, 'thank you, sir, I wish you good day.' so he leave Mr. Bounderby swell at his own portrait on the wall, as if he be go to explode himself into it; and Mrs. Sparsit still amble on with her foot in her stirrup, look quite cast down by the popular vice.
CHAPTER XII - the OLD woman
OLD STEPHEN descend the two white step, shut the black door with the brazen door-plate, by the aid of the brazen full-stop, to which he give a parting polish with the sleeve of his coat, observe that his hot hand cloud it. he cross the street with his eye bent upon the ground, and thus be walk sorrowfully away, when he feel a touch upon his arm.
it be not the touch he need most at such a moment - the touch that could calm the wild water of his soul, as the uplifted hand of the sublime love and patience could abate the raging of the sea - yet it be a woman's hand too. it be an old woman, tall and shapely still, though wither by time, on whom his eye fall when he stop and turn. she be very cleanly and plainly dress, have country mud upon her shoe, and be newly come from a journey. the flutter of her manner, in the unwonted noise of the street; the spare shawl, carry unfold on her arm; the heavy umbrella, and little basket; the loose long-finger glove, to which her hand be unused; all bespoke an old woman from the country, in her plain holiday clothe, come into Coketown on an expedition of rare occurrence. remark this at a glance, with the quick observation of his class, Stephen Blackpool bend his attentive face - his face, which, like the face of many of his order, by dint of long work with eye and hand in the midst of a prodigious noise, have acquire the concentrated look with which we be familiar in the countenance of the deaf - the well to hear what she ask he.
'pray, sir,' say the old woman, 'donot I see you come out of that gentleman's house?' point back to Mr. Bounderby's. 'I believe it be you, unless I have have the bad luck to mistake the person in follow?'
'yes, missus,' return Stephen, 'it be I.'
'have you - youwill excuse an old woman's curiosity - have you see the gentleman?'
'yes, missus.'
'and how do he look, sir? be he portly, bold, outspoken, and hearty?' as she straighten her own figure, and hold up her head in adapt her action to her word, the idea cross Stephen that he have see this old woman before, and have not quite like she.
'o yes,' he return, observe she more attentively, 'he be all that.'
'and healthy,' say the old woman, 'as the fresh wind?'
'yes,' return Stephen. 'he be ett'n and drinking - as large and as loud as a Hummobee.'
'thank you!' say the old woman, with infinite content. 'thank you!'
he certainly never have see this old woman before. yet there be a vague remembrance in his mind, as if he have more than once dream of some old woman like she.
she walk along at his side, and, gently accommodate himself to her humour, he say Coketown be a busy place, be it not? to which she answer 'eigh sure! dreadful busy!' then he say, she come from the country, he see? to which she answer in the affirmative.
'by Parliamentary, this morning. I come forty mile by Parliamentary this morning, and Ibe go back the same forty mile this afternoon. I walk nine mile to the station this morning, and if I find nobody on the road to give I a lift, I shall walk the nine mile back to-night. thatbe pretty well, sir, at my age!' say the chatty old woman, her eye brightening with exultation.
''deed 'tis. donot do't too often, missus.'
'no, no. once a year,' she answer, shake her head. 'I spend my saving so, once every year. I come regular, to tramp about the street, and see the gentleman.'
'only to see them?' return Stephen.
'thatbe enough for I,' she reply, with great earnestness and interest of manner. 'I ask no more! I have be stand about, on this side of the way, to see that gentleman,' turn her head back towards Mr. Bounderby's again, 'come out. but, hebe late this year, and I have not see he. you come out instead. now, if I be oblige to go back without a glimpse of he - I only want a glimpse - well! I have see you, and you have see he, and I must make that do.' say this, she look at Stephen as if to fix his feature in her mind, and her eye be not so bright as it have be.
with a large allowance for difference of taste, and with all submission to the patrician of Coketown, this seem so extraordinary a source of interest to take so much trouble about, that it perplex he. but they be pass the church now, and as his eye catch the clock, he quicken his pace.
he be go to his work? the old woman say, quicken her, too, quite easily. yes, time be nearly out. on his tell she where he work, the old woman become a more singular old woman than before.
'an't you happy?' she ask he.
'why - therebe awmost nobbody but have their trouble, missus.' he answer evasively, because the old woman appear to take it for grant that he would be very happy indeed, and he have not the heart to disappoint she. he know that there be trouble enough in the world; and if the old woman have live so long, and could count upon his have so little, why so much the well for she, and none the bad for he.
'ay, ay! you have your trouble at home, you mean?' she say.
'time. just now and then,' he answer, slightly.
'but, work under such a gentleman, they donot follow you to the Factory?'
no, no; they donot follow he there, say Stephen. all correct there. everything accordant there. (he do not go so far as to say, for her pleasure, that there be a sort of Divine right there; but, I have hear claim almost as magnificent of late year.)
they be now in the black by-road near the place, and the Hands be crowd in. the bell be ring, and the Serpent be a Serpent of many coil, and the Elephant be get ready. the strange old woman be delighted with the very bell. it be the beautifull bell she have ever hear, she say, and sound grand!
she ask he, when he stop good-naturedly to shake hand with she before go in, how long he have work there?
'a dozen year,' he tell she.
'I must kiss the hand,' say she, 'that have work in this fine factory for a dozen year!' and she lift it, though he would have prevent she, and put it to her lip. what harmony, besides her age and her simplicity, surround she, he do not know, but even in this fantastic action there be a something neither out of time nor place: a something which it seem as if nobody else could have make as serious, or do with such a natural and touching air.
he have be at his loom full half an hour, think about this old woman, when, have occasion to move round the loom for its adjustment, he glance through a window which be in his corner, and see she still look up at the pile of building, lose in admiration. heedless of the smoke and mud and wet, and of her two long journey, she be gaze at it, as if the heavy thrum that issue from its many story be proud music to she.
she be go by and by, and the day go after she, and the light spring up again, and the Express whirl in full sight of the Fairy Palace over the arch near: little feel amid the jarring of the machinery, and scarcely hear above its crash and rattle. long before then his thought have go back to the dreary room above the little shop, and to the shameful figure heavy on the bed, but heavy on his heart.
Machinery slacken; throb feebly like a faint pulse; stop. the bell again; the glare of light and heat dispel; the factory, loom heavy in the black wet night - their tall chimney rise up into the air like compete tower of Babel.
he have speak to Rachael only last night, it be true, and have walk with she a little way; but he have his new misfortune on he, in which no one else could give he a moment's relief, and, for the sake of it, and because he know himself to want that soften of his anger which no voice but hers could effect, he feel he might so far disregard what she have say as to wait for she again. he wait, but she have elude he. she be go. on no other night in the year could he so ill have spar her patient face.
o! well to have no home in which to lay his head, than to have a home and dread to go to it, through such a cause. he eat and drank, for he be exhausted - but he little know or care what; and he wander about in the chill rain, think and thinking, and brood and brooding.
no word of a new marriage have ever pass between they; but Rachael have take great pity on he year ago, and to she alone he have open his closed heart all this time, on the subject of his misery; and he know very well that if he be free to ask she, she would take he. he think of the home he might at that moment have be seek with pleasure and pride; of the different man he might have be that night; of the lightness then in his now heavy- laden breast; of the then restore honour, self-respect, and tranquillity all tear to piece. he think of the waste of the good part of his life, of the change it make in his character for the bad every day, of the dreadful nature of his existence, bind hand and foot, to a dead woman, and torment by a demon in her shape. he think of Rachael, how young when they be first bring together in these circumstance, how mature now, how soon to grow old. he think of the number of girl and woman she have see marry, how many home with child in they she have see grow up around she, how she have contentedly pursue her own lone quiet path - for he - and how he have sometimes see a shade of melancholy on her blessed face, that smite he with remorse and despair. he set the picture of she up, beside the infamous image of last night; and think, could it be, that the whole earthly course of one so gentle, good, and self-deny, be subjugate to such a wretch as that!
fill with these thought - so fill that he have an unwholesome sense of grow large, of be place in some new and diseased relation towards the object among which he pass, of see the iris round every misty light turn red - he go home for shelter.
CHAPTER XIII - RACHAEL
a candle faintly burn in the window, to which the black ladder have often be raise for the slide away of all that be most precious in this world to a strive wife and a brood of hungry baby; and Stephen add to his other thought the stern reflection, that of all the casualty of this existence upon earth, not one be deal out with so unequal a hand as death. the inequality of Birth be nothing to it. for, say that the child of a King and the child of a Weaver be bear to-night in the same moment, what be that disparity, to the death of any human creature who be serviceable to, or belove by, another, while this abandon woman live on!
from the outside of his home he gloomily pass to the inside, with suspend breath and with a slow footstep. he go up to his door, open it, and so into the room.
quiet and peace be there. Rachael be there, sit by the bed.
she turn her head, and the light of her face shine in upon the midnight of his mind. she sit by the bed, watch and tend his wife. that be to say, he see that some one lie there, and he know too well it must be she; but Rachael's hand have put a curtain up, so that she be screen from his eye. her disgraceful garment be remove, and some of Rachael's be in the room. everything be in its place and order as he have always keep it, the little fire be newly trim, and the hearth be freshly sweep. it appear to he that he see all this in Rachael's face, and look at nothing besides. while look at it, it be shut out from his view by the soften tear that fill his eye; but not before he have see how earnestly she look at he, and how her own eye be fill too.
she turn again towards the bed, and satisfy herself that all be quiet there, speak in a low, calm, cheerful voice.
'I be glad you have come at last, Stephen. you be very late.'
'I ha' be walk up an' down.'
'I think so. but 'tis too bad a night for that. the rain fall very heavy, and the wind have rise.'
the wind? true. it be blow hard. Hark to the thundering in the chimney, and the surge noise! to have be out in such a wind, and not to have know it be blow!
'I have be here once before, to-day, Stephen. Landlady come round for I at dinner-time. there be some one here that need look to, she say. and 'deed she be right. all wander and lose, Stephen. wound too, and bruise.'
he slowly move to a chair and sit down, droop his head before she.
'I come to do what little I could, Stephen; first, for that she work with I when we be girl both, and for that you court she and marry she when I be her friend - '
he lay his furrowed forehead on his hand, with a low groan.
'and next, for that I know your heart, and be right sure and certain that 'tis far too merciful to let she die, or even so much as suffer, for want of aid. Thou know who say, "let he who be without sin among you cast the first stone at she!" there have be plenty to do that. thou art not the man to cast the last stone, Stephen, when she be bring so low.'
'o Rachael, Rachael!'
'Thou hast be a cruel sufferer, Heaven reward thee!' she say, in compassionate accent. 'I be thy poor friend, with all my heart and mind.'
the wound of which she have speak, seem to be about the neck of the self-make outcast. she dress they now, still without show she. she steep a piece of linen in a basin, into which she pour some liquid from a bottle, and lay it with a gentle hand upon the sore. the three-legged table have be draw close to the bedside, and on it there be two bottle. this be one.
it be not so far off, but that Stephen, follow her hand with his eye, could read what be print on it in large letter. he turn of a deadly hue, and a sudden horror seem to fall upon he.

CHAPTER XIV - the great MANUFACTURER

nobody present have any suggestion to offer in the remarkable emergency, Mrs. Gradgrind depart this life for the time be, after deliver the follow codicil to her remark already execute:

when Mr. Gradgrind have present Mrs. Bounderby, Sissy have suddenly turn her head, and look, in wonder, in pity, in sorrow, in doubt, in a multitude of emotion, towards Louisa. CHAPTER XVI - HUSBAND and WIFE
MR. BOUNDERBY's first disquietude on hearing of his happiness, be occasion by the necessity of impart it to Mrs. Sparsit. he could not make up his mind how to do that, or what the consequence of the step might be. whether she would instantly depart, bag and baggage, to Lady Scadgers, or would positively refuse to budge from the premise; whether she would be plaintive or abusive, tearful or tearing; whether she would break her heart, or break the looking- glass; Mr. Bounderby could not all foresee. however, as it must be do, he have no choice but to do it; so, after attempt several letter, and fail in they all, he resolve to do it by word of mouth.

nothing move Mrs. Sparsit from that position any more. it be in vain for Bounderby to bluster or to assert himself in any of his explosive way; Mrs. Sparsit be resolve to have compassion on he, as a Victim. she be polite, obliging, cheerful, hopeful; but, the more polite, the more obliging, the more cheerful, the more hopeful, the more exemplary altogether, she; the forlorner Sacrifice and Victim, he. she have that tenderness for his melancholy fate, that his great red countenance use to break out into cold perspiration when she look at he.

CHAPTER i - EFFECTS in THE BANK
a sunny midsummer day. there be such a thing sometimes, even in Coketown.
see from a distance in such weather, Coketown lie shroud in a haze of its own, which appear impervious to the sun's ray. you only know the town be there, because you know there could have be no such sulky blotch upon the prospect without a town. a blur of soot and smoke, now confusedly tend this way, now that way, now aspire to the vault of Heaven, now murkily creep along the earth, as the wind rise and fall, or change its quarter: a dense formless jumble, with sheet of cross light in it, that show nothing but mass of darkness:- Coketown in the distance be suggestive of itself, though not a brick of it could be see.
the wonder be, it be there at all. it have be ruin so often, that it be amazing how it have bear so many shock. surely there never be such fragile china-ware as that of which the miller of Coketown be make. handle they never so lightly, and they fall to piece with such ease that you might suspect they of have be flawed before. they be ruin, when they be require to send labour child to school; they be ruin when inspector be appoint to look into their work; they be ruin, when such inspector consider it doubtful whether they be quite justified in chop people up with their machinery; they be utterly undo, when it be hint that perhaps they need not always make quite so much smoke. besides Mr. Bounderby's gold spoon which be generally receive in Coketown, another prevalent fiction be very popular there. it take the form of a threat. whenever a Coketowner feel he be ill-use - that be to say, whenever he be not leave entirely alone, and it be propose to hold he accountable for the consequence of any of his act - he be sure to come out with the awful menace, that he would 'soon pitch his property into the Atlantic.' this have terrify the Home Secretary within an inch of his life, on several occasion.
however, the Coketowners be so patriotic after all, that they never have pitch their property into the Atlantic yet, but, on the contrary, have be kind enough to take mighty good care of it. so there it be, in the haze yonder; and it increase and multiply.
the street be hot and dusty on the summer day, and the sun be so bright that it even shine through the heavy vapour droop over Coketown, and could not be look at steadily. stoker emerge from low underground doorway into factory yard, and sit on step, and post, and paling, wipe their swarthy visage, and contemplate coal. the whole town seem to be fry in oil. there be a stifling smell of hot oil everywhere. the steam- engine shine with it, the dress of the Hands be soil with it, the mill throughout their many story ooze and trickle it. the atmosphere of those Fairy palace be like the breath of the simoom: and their inhabitant, waste with heat, toil languidly in the desert. but no temperature make the melancholy mad elephant more mad or more sane. their wearisome head go up and down at the same rate, in hot weather and cold, wet weather and dry, fair weather and foul. the measured motion of their shadow on the wall, be the substitute Coketown have to show for the shadow of rustle wood; while, for the summer hum of insect, it could offer, all the year round, from the dawn of Monday to the night of Saturday, the whirr of shaft and wheel.
Drowsily they whir all through this sunny day, make the passenger more sleepy and more hot as he pass the hum wall of the mill. Sun-blind, and sprinkling of water, a little cool the main street and the shop; but the mill, and the court and alley, bake at a fierce heat. down upon the river that be black and thick with dye, some Coketown boy who be at large - a rare sight there - row a crazy boat, which make a spumous track upon the water as it jog along, while every dip of an oar stir up vile smell. but the sun itself, however beneficent, generally, be less kind to Coketown than hard frost, and rarely look intently into any of its close region without engender more death than life. so do the eye of Heaven itself become an evil eye, when incapable or sordid hand be interpose between it and the thing it look upon to bless.
Mrs. Sparsit sit in her afternoon apartment at the Bank, on the shady side of the fry street. office-hour be over: and at that period of the day, in warm weather, she usually embellish with her genteel presence, a managerial board-room over the public office. her own private sitting-room be a story high, at the window of which post of observation she be ready, every morning, to greet Mr. Bounderby, as he come across the road, with the sympathize recognition appropriate to a Victim. he have be marry now a year; and Mrs. Sparsit have never release he from her determined pity a moment.
the Bank offer no violence to the wholesome monotony of the town. it be another red brick house, with black outside shutter, green inside blind, a black street-door up two white step, a brazen door-plate, and a brazen door-handle full stop. it be a size large than Mr. Bounderby's house, as other house be from a size to half-a-dozen size small; in all other particular, it be strictly accord to pattern.
Mrs. Sparsit be conscious that by come in the evening-tide among the desk and write implement, she shed a feminine, not to say also aristocratic, grace upon the office. seat, with her needlework or netting apparatus, at the window, she have a self- laudatory sense of correct, by her ladylike deportment, the rude business aspect of the place. with this impression of her interesting character upon she, Mrs. Sparsit consider herself, in some sort, the Bank Fairy. the townspeople who, in their passing and repasse, see she there, regard she as the Bank Dragon keep watch over the treasure of the mine.
what those treasure be, Mrs. Sparsit know as little as they do. gold and silver coin, precious paper, secret that if divulge would bring vague destruction upon vague person (generally, however, people whom she dislike), be the chief item in her ideal catalogue thereof. for the rest, she know that after office- hour, she reign supreme over all the office furniture, and over a lock-up iron room with three lock, against the door of which strong chamber the light porter lay his head every night, on a truckle bed, that disappear at cockcrow. far, she be lady paramount over certain vault in the basement, sharply spike off from communication with the predatory world; and over the relic of the current day's work, consist of blot of ink, wear-out pen, fragment of wafer, and scrap of paper tear so small, that nothing interesting could ever be decipher on they when Mrs. Sparsit try. lastly, she be guardian over a little armoury of cutlass and carbine, array in vengeful order above one of the official chimney-piece; and over that respectable tradition never to be separate from a place of business claim to be wealthy - a row of fire-bucket - vessel calculate to be of no physical utility on any occasion, but observe to exercise a fine moral influence, almost equal to bullion, on most beholder.

he hold the respectable office of general spy and informer in the establishment, for which volunteer service he receive a present at Christmas, over and above his weekly wage. he have grow into an extremely clear-head, cautious, prudent young man, who be safe to rise in the world. his mind be so exactly regulated, that he have no affection or passion. all his proceeding be the result of the nice and cold calculation; and it be not without cause that Mrs. Sparsit habitually observe of he, that he be a young man of the steady principle she have ever know. have satisfied himself, on his father's death, that his mother have a right of settlement in Coketown, this excellent young economist have assert that right for she with such a steadfast adherence to the principle of the case, that she have be shut up in the workhouse ever since. it must be admit that he allow she half a pound of tea a year, which be weak in he: first, because all gift have an inevitable tendency to pauperise the recipient, and secondly, because his only reasonable transaction in that commodity would have be to buy it for as little as he could possibly give, and sell it for as much as he could possibly get; it have be clearly ascertain by philosopher that in this be comprise the whole duty of man - not a part of man's duty, but the whole.
