'my dear fellow,' return the other, 'what be I bind to suppose, when I find two married people live in harmony and happiness?'
Tom have by this time get both his leg on the sofa. if his second leg have not be already there when he be call a dear fellow, he would have put it up at that great stage of the conversation. feel it necessary to do something then, he stretch himself out at great length, and, recline with the back of his head on the end of the sofa, and smoke with an infinite assumption of negligence, turn his common face, and not too sober eye, towards the face look down upon he so carelessly yet so potently.
'you know our governor, Mr. Harthouse,' say Tom, 'and therefore, you neednot be surprised that Loo marry old Bounderby. she never have a lover, and the governor propose old Bounderby, and she take he.'
'very dutiful in your interesting sister,' say Mr. James Harthouse.
'yes, but she wouldnot have be as dutiful, and it would not have come off as easily,' return the whelp, 'if it havenot be for I.'
the tempter merely lift his eyebrow; but the whelp be oblige to go on.
'I persuade she,' he say, with an edify air of superiority. 'I be stick into old Bounderby's bank (where I never want to be), and I know I should get into scrape there, if she put old Bounderby's pipe out; so I tell she my wish, and she come into they. she would do anything for I. it be very game of she, benot it?'
'it be charming, Tom!'
'not that it be altogether so important to she as it be to I,' continue Tom coolly, 'because my liberty and comfort, and perhaps my get on, depend on it; and she have no other lover, and stay at home be like stay in jail - especially when I be go. it benot as if she give up another lover for old Bounderby; but still it be a good thing in she.'
'perfectly delightful. and she get on so placidly.'
'oh,' return Tom, with contemptuous patronage, 'shebe a regular girl. a girl can get on anywhere. she have settle down to the life, and she donot mind. it do just as well as another. besides, though Loo be a girl, shebe not a common sort of girl. she can shut herself up within herself, and think - as I have often know her sit and watch the fire - for an hour at a stretch.'
'ay, ay? have resource of her own,' say Harthouse, smoke quietly.
'not so much of that as you may suppose,' return Tom; 'for our governor have she cram with all sort of dry bone and sawdust. itbe his system.'
'form his daughter on his own model?' suggest Harthouse.
'his daughter? ah! and everybody else. why, he form I that way!' say Tom.
'impossible!'
'he do, though,' say Tom, shake his head. 'I mean to say, Mr. Harthouse, that when I first leave home and go to old Bounderby's, I be as flat as a warming-pan, and know no more about life, than any oyster do.'
'come, Tom! I can hardly believe that. a joke's a joke.'
'upon my soul!' say the whelp. 'I be serious; I be indeed!' he smoke with great gravity and dignity for a little while, and then add, in a highly complacent tone, 'oh! I have pick up a little since. I donot deny that. but I have do it myself; no thank to the governor.'
'and your intelligent sister?'
'my intelligent sister be about where she be. she use to complain to I that she have nothing to fall back upon, that girl usually fall back upon; and I donot see how she be to have get over that since. but she donot mind,' he sagaciously add, puff at his cigar again. 'girl can always get on, somehow.'
'call at the Bank yesterday evening, for Mr. Bounderby's address, I find an ancient lady there, who seem to entertain great admiration for your sister,' observe Mr. James Harthouse, throw away the last small remnant of the cigar he have now smoke out.
'Mother Sparsit!' say Tom. 'what! you have see she already, have you?'
his friend nod. Tom take his cigar out of his mouth, to shut up his eye (which have grow rather unmanageable) with the great expression, and to tap his nose several time with his finger.
'Mother Sparsit's feeling for Loo be more than admiration, I should think,' say Tom. 'say affection and devotion. Mother Sparsit never set her cap at Bounderby when he be a bachelor. oh no!'
these be the last word speak by the whelp, before a giddy drowsiness come upon he, follow by complete oblivion. he be rouse from the latter state by an uneasy dream of be stir up with a boot, and also of a voice say: 'come, itbe late. be off!'
'well!' he say, scramble from the sofa. 'I must take my leave of you though. I say. your be very good tobacco. but itbe too mild.'
'yes, itbe too mild,' return his entertainer.
'itbe - itbe ridiculously mild,' say Tom. 'wherebe the door! good night!'
'he have another odd dream of be take by a waiter through a mist, which, after give he some trouble and difficulty, resolve itself into the main street, in which he stand alone. he then walk home pretty easily, though not yet free from an impression of the presence and influence of his new friend - as if he be lounge somewhere in the air, in the same negligent attitude, regard he with the same look.
the whelp go home, and go to bed. if he have have any sense of what he have do that night, and have be less of a whelp and more of a brother, he might have turn short on the road, might have go down to the ill-smell river that be dye black, might have go to bed in it for good and all, and have curtain his head for ever with its filthy water.
CHAPTER IV - MEN and brother
'OH, my friend, the down-trodden operative of Coketown! oh, my friend and fellow-countryman, the slave of an iron-handed and a grind despotism! oh, my friend and fellow-sufferer, and fellow-workman, and fellow-man! I tell you that the hour be come, when we must rally round one another as one united power, and crumble into dust the oppressor that too long have batten upon the plunder of our family, upon the sweat of our brow, upon the labour of our hand, upon the strength of our sinew, upon the God- create glorious right of Humanity, and upon the holy and eternal privilege of Brotherhood!'
'good!' 'hear, hear, hear!' 'hurrah!' and other cry, arise in many voice from various part of the densely crowded and suffocatingly close Hall, in which the orator, perch on a stage, deliver himself of this and what other froth and fume he have in he. he have declaim himself into a violent heat, and be as hoarse as he be hot. by dint of roaring at the top of his voice under a flaring gaslight, clench his fist, knit his brow, set his tooth, and pound with his arm, he have take so much out of himself by this time, that he be bring to a stop, and call for a glass of water.
as he stand there, try to quench his fiery face with his drink of water, the comparison between the orator and the crowd of attentive face turn towards he, be extremely to his disadvantage. judge he by Nature's evidence, he be above the mass in very little but the stage on which he stand. in many great respect he be essentially below they. he be not so honest, he be not so manly, he be not so good-humour; he substitute cunning for their simplicity, and passion for their safe solid sense. an ill-make, high-shoulder man, with lower brow, and his feature crush into an habitually sour expression, he contrast most unfavourably, even in his mongrel dress, with the great body of his hearer in their plain work clothe. strange as it always be to consider any assembly in the act of submissively resign itself to the dreariness of some complacent person, lord or commoner, whom three-fourth of it could, by no human mean, raise out of the slough of inanity to their own intellectual level, it be particularly strange, and it be even particularly affect, to see this crowd of earnest face, whose honesty in the main no competent observer free from bias could doubt, so agitated by such a leader. good! hear, hear! Hurrah! the eagerness both of attention and intention, exhibit in all the countenance, make they a most impressive sight. there be no carelessness, no languor, no idle curiosity; none of the many shade of indifference to be see in all other assembly, visible for one moment there. that every man feel his condition to be, somehow or other, bad than it might be; that every man consider it incumbent on he to join the rest, towards the making of it well; that every man feel his only hope to be in his allying himself to the comrade by whom he be surround; and that in this belief, right or wrong (unhappily wrong then), the whole of that crowd be gravely, deeply, faithfully in earnest; must have be as plain to any one who choose to see what be there, as the bare beam of the roof and the whitened brick wall. nor could any such spectator fail to know in his own breast, that these man, through their very delusion, show great quality, susceptible of be turn to the happy and good account; and that to pretend (on the strength of sweeping axiom, howsoever cut and dry) that they go astray wholly without cause, and of their own irrational will, be to pretend that there could be smoke without fire, death without birth, harvest without seed, anything or everything produce from nothing.
the orator have refresh himself, wipe his corrugated forehead from left to right several time with his handkerchief fold into a pad, and concentrate all his revive force, in a sneer of great disdain and bitterness.
'but oh, my friend and brother! oh, man and Englishmen, the down-trodden operative of Coketown! what shall we say of that man - that working-man, that I should find it necessary so to libel the glorious name - who, be practically and well acquainted with the grievance and wrong of you, the injure pith and marrow of this land, and have hear you, with a noble and majestic unanimity that will make tyrant tremble, resolve for to subscribe to the fund of the United Aggregate Tribunal, and to abide by the injunction issue by that body for your benefit, whatever they may be - what, I ask you, will you say of that working-man, since such I must acknowledge he to be, who, at such a time, desert his post, and sell his flag; who, at such a time, turn a traitor and a craven and a recreant, who, at such a time, be not ashamed to make to you the dastardly and humiliating avowal that he will hold himself aloof, and will not be one of those associate in the gallant stand for Freedom and for right?'
the assembly be divide at this point. there be some groan and hiss, but the general sense of honour be much too strong for the condemnation of a man unheard. 'be sure yoube right, Slackbridge!' 'put he up!' 'letus hear he!' such thing be say on many side. finally, one strong voice call out, 'be the man heer? if the man's heer, Slackbridge, letus hear the man himseln, 'stead o' yo.' which be receive with a round of applause.
Slackbridge, the orator, look about he with a wither smile; and, hold out his right hand at arm's length (as the manner of all Slackbridges be), to still the thunder sea, wait until there be a profound silence.
'oh, my friend and fellow-man!' say Slackbridge then, shake his head with violent scorn, 'I do not wonder that you, the prostrate son of labour, be incredulous of the existence of such a man. but he who sell his birthright for a mess of pottage exist, and Judas Iscariot exist, and Castlereagh exist, and this man exist!'
here, a brief press and confusion near the stage, end in the man himself stand at the orator's side before the concourse. he be pale and a little move in the face - his lip especially show it; but he stand quiet, with his left hand at his chin, wait to be hear. there be a chairman to regulate the proceeding, and this functionary now take the case into his own hand.
'my friend,' say he, 'by virtue o' my office as your president, I ask o' our friend Slackbridge, who may be a little over hetter in this business, to take his seat, while this man Stephen Blackpool be heern. you all know this man Stephen Blackpool. you know he awlung o' his misfort'n, and his good name.'
with that, the chairman shake he frankly by the hand, and sit down again. Slackbridge likewise sit down, wipe his hot forehead - always from left to right, and never the reverse way.
'my friend,' Stephen begin, in the midst of a dead calm; 'I ha' hed whatbe be spok'n o' I, and 'tis lickly that I shallnot mend it. but Iwould liefer youwould hearn the truth concernin myseln, fro my lip than fro onny other man's, though I never cud'n speak afore so monny, wi'out bein moydert and muddle.'
Slackbridge shake his head as if he would shake it off, in his bitterness.
'Ibe th' one single Hand in Bounderby's mill, o' a' the man theer, as donot coom in wi' th' propose reg'lation. I canna coom in wi' them. my friend, I doubt their do yo onny good. licker theywill do yo hurt.'
Slackbridge laugh, fold his arm, and frown sarcastically.
'but 't an't sommuch for that as I stand out. if that be aw, Iwould coom in wi' th' rest. but I ha' my reason - mine, yo see - for be hinder; not on'y now, but awlus - awlus - life long!'
Slackbridge jump up and stand beside he, gnashing and tearing. 'oh, my friend, what but this do I tell you? oh, my fellow- countryman, what warning but this do I give you? and how show this recreant conduct in a man on whom unequal law be know to have fall heavy? oh, you englishman, I ask you how do this subornation show in one of yourself, who be thus consent to his own undoing and to your, and to your child's and your child's child's?'
there be some applause, and some cry of Shame upon the man; but the great part of the audience be quiet. they look at Stephen's worn face, render more pathetic by the homely emotion it evince; and, in the kindness of their nature, they be more sorry than indignant.
''tis this Delegate's trade for t' speak,' say Stephen, 'an' hebe pay for 't, an' he know his work. let he keep to 't. let he give no heed to what I ha had'n to bear. thatbe not for he. thatbe not for nobbody but I.'
there be a propriety, not to say a dignity in these word, that make the hearer yet more quiet and attentive. the same strong voice call out, 'Slackbridge, let the man be heern, and howd thee tongue!' then the place be wonderfully still.
'my brother,' say Stephen, whose low voice be distinctly hear, 'and my fellow-workman - for that yo be to I, though not, as I know on, to this delegate here - I ha but a word to sen, and I could sen nommore if I be to speak till strike o' day. I know weel, aw whatbe afore I. I know weel that yo aw resolve to ha nommore ado wi' a man who be not wi' yo in this matther. I know weel that if I be a lyin parisht I' th' road, yo'd feel it right to pass I by, as a forrenner and strange. what I ha getn, I mun mak th' well on.'
'Stephen Blackpool,' say the chairman, rise, 'think on 't agen. think on 't once agen, lad, afore thou'rt shun by aw owd friend.'
there be an universal murmur to the same effect, though no man articulate a word. every eye be fix on Stephen's face. to repent of his determination, would be to take a load from all their mind. he look around he, and know that it be so. not a grain of anger with they be in his heart; he know they, far below their surface weakness and misconception, as no one but their fellow- labourer could.
'I ha thowt on 't, above a bit, sir. I simply canna coom in. I mun go th' way as lay afore I. I mun tak my leave o' aw heer.'
he make a sort of reverence to they by hold up his arm, and stand for the moment in that attitude; not speak until they slowly drop at his side.
'Monnybe the pleasant word as soom heer have spok'n wi' I; monnybe the face I see heer, as I first see when I be yoong and light heart'n than now. I ha' never have no fratch afore, sin ever I be bear, wi' any o' my like; Gonnows I ha' none now thatbe o' my makin'. Yo'll can' I traitor and that - yo I mean t' say,' address slackbridge, 'but 'tis easy to can' than mak' out. so let be.'
he have move away a pace or two to come down from the platform, when he remember something he have not say, and return again.
'haply,' he say, turn his furrowed face slowly about, that he might as it be individually address the whole audience, those both near and distant; 'haply, when this question have be tak'n up and discoose, therewill be a threat to turn out if Ibe let to work among yo. I hope I shall die ere ever such a time coom, and I shall work solitary among yo unless it coom - truly, I mun do 't, my friend; not to brave yo, but to live. I ha nobbut work to live by; and wheerever can I go, I who ha work sin I be no heighth at aw, in Coketown heer? I mak' no complaint o' bein turn to the wa', o' bein outcasten and overlooken fro this time forrard, but hope I shall be let to work. if there be any right for I at aw, my friend, I think 'tis that.'
not a word be speak. not a sound be audible in the building, but the slight rustle of man move a little apart, all along the centre of the room, to open a mean of pass out, to the man with whom they have all bind themselves to renounce companionship. look at no one, and go his way with a lowly steadiness upon he that assert nothing and seek nothing, Old Stephen, with all his trouble on his head, leave the scene.
then Slackbridge, who have keep his oratorical arm extend during the go out, as if he be repress with infinite solicitude and by a wonderful moral power the vehement passion of the multitude, apply himself to raise their spirit. have not the Roman Brutus, oh, my british countryman, condemn his son to death; and have not the spartan mother, oh my soon to be victorious friend, drive their fly child on the point of their enemy' sword? then be it not the sacred duty of the man of Coketown, with forefather before they, an admire world in company with they, and a posterity to come after they, to hurl out traitor from the tent they have pitch in a sacred and a God-like cause? the wind of heaven answer yes; and bear Yes, east, west, north, and south. and consequently three cheer for the United Aggregate Tribunal!
Slackbridge act as fugleman, and give the time. the multitude of doubtful face (a little conscience-stricken) brighten at the sound, and take it up. private feeling must yield to the common cause. Hurrah! the roof yet vibrate with the cheering, when the assembly disperse.
thus easily do Stephen Blackpool fall into the loneliest of life, the life of solitude among a familiar crowd. the stranger in the land who look into ten thousand face for some answer look and never find it, be in cheer society as compare with he who pass ten avert face daily, that be once the countenance of friend. such experience be to be Stephen's now, in every wake moment of his life; at his work, on his way to it and from it, at his door, at his window, everywhere. by general consent, they even avoid that side of the street on which he habitually walk; and leave it, of all the work man, to he only.
he have be for many year, a quiet silent man, associate but little with other man, and use to companionship with his own thought. he have never know before the strength of the want in his heart for the frequent recognition of a nod, a look, a word; or the immense amount of relief that have be pour into it by drop through such small mean. it be even hard than he could have believe possible, to separate in his own conscience his abandonment by all his fellow from a baseless sense of shame and disgrace.
the first four day of his endurance be day so long and heavy, that he begin to be appal by the prospect before he. not only do he see no Rachael all the time, but he avoid every chance of see she; for, although he know that the prohibition do not yet formally extend to the woman work in the factory, he find that some of they with whom he be acquaint be change to he, and he fear to try other, and dread that Rachael might be even single out from the rest if she be see in his company. so, he have be quite alone during the four day, and have speak to no one, when, as he be leave his work at night, a young man of a very light complexion accost he in the street.
'your name's Blackpool, benot it?' say the young man.
Stephen colour to find himself with his hat in his hand, in his gratitude for be speak to, or in the suddenness of it, or both. he make a feint of adjust the lining, and say, 'yes.'
'you be the Hand they have send to Coventry, I mean?' say Bitzer, the very light young man in question.
Stephen answer 'yes,' again.
'I suppose so, from their all appear to keep away from you. Mr. Bounderby want to speak to you. you know his house, donot you?'
Stephen say 'yes,' again.
'then go straight up there, will you?' say Bitzer. 'yoube expect, and have only to tell the servant itbe you. I belong to the Bank; so, if you go straight up without I (I be send to fetch you), youwill save I a walk.'
Stephen, whose way have be in the contrary direction, turn about, and betook himself as in duty bind, to the red brick castle of the giant Bounderby.
CHAPTER V - MEN and master
'well, Stephen,' say Bounderby, in his windy manner, 'whatbe this I hear? what have these pest of the earth be do to you? come in, and speak up.'
it be into the drawing-room that he be thus bid. a tea-table be set out; and Mr. Bounderby's young wife, and her brother, and a great gentleman from London, be present. to whom Stephen make his obeisance, close the door and stand near it, with his hat in his hand.
'this be the man I be tell you about, Harthouse,' say Mr. Bounderby. the gentleman he address, who be talk to Mrs. Bounderby on the sofa, get up, say in an indolent way, 'oh really?' and dawdle to the hearthrug where Mr. Bounderby stand.
'now,' say Bounderby, 'speak up!'
after the four day he have pass, this address fall rudely and discordantly on Stephen's ear. besides be a rough handling of his wounded mind, it seem to assume that he really be the self- interested deserter he have be call.
'what be it, sir,' say Stephen, 'as yo be pleased to want wi' I?'
'why, I have tell you,' return Bounderby. 'speak up like a man, since you be a man, and tell we about yourself and this combination.'
'Wi' yor pardon, sir,' say Stephen Blackpool, 'I ha' nowt to sen about it.'
Mr. Bounderby, who be always more or less like a wind, find something in his way here, begin to blow at it directly.
'now, look here, Harthouse,' say he, 'herebe a speciman of them. when this man be here once before, I warn this man against the mischievous stranger who be always about - and who ought to be hang wherever they be find - and I tell this man that he be go in the wrong direction. now, would you believe it, that although they have put this mark upon he, he be such a slave to they still, that hebe afraid to open his lip about they?' 'I se as I have nowt to sen, sir; not as I be fearfo' o' openin' my lip.'
'you say! ah! I know what you say; more than that, I know what you mean, you see. not always the same thing, by the Lord Harry! quite different thing. you have well tell we at once, that that fellow Slackbridge be not in the town, stir up the people to mutiny; and that he be not a regular qualified leader of the people: that be, a most confounded scoundrel. you have well tell we so at once; you cannot deceive I. you want to tell we so. why donot you?'
'Ibe as sooary as yo, sir, when the people's leader be bad,' say Stephen, shake his head. 'they tak such as offer. haply 'tis na' the sma' o' their misfortun when they can get no well.' the wind begin to get boisterous.
'now, youwill think this pretty well, Harthouse,' say Mr. Bounderby. 'youwill think this tolerably strong. youwill say, upon my soul this be a tidy speciman of what my friend have to deal with; but this be nothing, sir! you shall hear I ask this man a question. pray, Mr. Blackpool' - wind spring up very fast - 'may I take the liberty of ask you how it happen that you refuse to be in this combination?'
'how 't happen?'
'ah!' say Mr. Bounderby, with his thumb in the arm of his coat, and jerk his head and shut his eye in confidence with the opposite wall: 'how it happen.'
'Iwould leefer not coom to 't, sir; but sin you put th' question - an' not want'n t' be ill-manner'n - Iwill answer. I ha pass a promess.'
'not to I, you know,' say Bounderby. (gusty weather with deceitful calm. one now prevail.)
'o no, sir. not to yo.'
'as for I, any consideration for I have have just nothing at all to do with it,' say Bounderby, still in confidence with the wall. 'if only Josiah Bounderby of Coketown have be in question, you would have join and make no bone about it?'
'why yes, sir. 'tis true.'
'though he know,' say Mr. Bounderby, now blow a gale, 'that there be a set of rascal and rebel whom transportation be too good for! now, Mr. Harthouse, you have be knock about in the world some time. do you ever meet with anything like that man out of this blessed country?' and Mr. Bounderby point he out for inspection, with an angry finger.
'Nay, ma'am,' say Stephen Blackpool, staunchly protest against the word that have be use, and instinctively address himself to Louisa, after glance at her face. 'not rebel, nor yet rascal. nowt o' th' kind, ma'am, nowt o' th' kind. theyhave not doon I a kindness, ma'am, as I know and feel. but therebe not a dozen man amoong them, ma'am - a dozen? not six - but what believe as he have doon his duty by the rest and by himseln. God forbid as I, that ha' know, and had'n experience o' these man aw my life - I, that ha' ett'n an' droonken wi' them, an' seet'n wi' them, and toil'n wi' them, and lov'n them, should fail fur to stan by them wi' the truth, let them ha' doon to I what they may!'
he speak with the rugged earnestness of his place and character - deepen perhaps by a proud consciousness that he be faithful to his class under all their mistrust; but he fully remember where he be, and do not even raise his voice.
'no, ma'am, no. theybe true to one another, faithfo' to one another, 'fectionate to one another, e'en to death. be poor amoong them, be sick amoong them, grieve amoong them for onny o' th' monny cause that carry grief to the poor man's door, an' theywill be tender wi' yo, gentle wi' yo, comfortable wi' yo, Chrisen wi' yo. be sure o' that, ma'am. theywould be rive to bit, ere ever theywould be different.'
'in short,' say Mr. Bounderby, 'itbe because they be so full of virtue that they have turn you adrift. go through with it while you be about it. out with it.'
'how 'tis, ma'am,' resume Stephen, appear still to find his natural refuge in Louisa's face, 'that what be good in we fok, seem to turn we most to trouble an' misfort'n an' mistake, I dunno. but 'tis so. I know 'tis, as I know the heavens be over I ahint the smoke. webe patient too, an' want in general to do right. an' I canna think the fawt be aw wi' us.'
'now, my friend,' say Mr. Bounderby, whom he could not have exasperate more, quite unconscious of it though he be, than by seem to appeal to any one else, 'if you will favour I with your attention for half a minute, I should like to have a word or two with you. you say just now, that you have nothing to tell we about this business. you be quite sure of that before we go any far.'
'Sir, I be sure on 't.'
'herebe a gentleman from London present,' Mr. Bounderby make a backhand point at Mr. James Harthouse with his thumb, 'a Parliament gentleman. I should like he to hear a short bit of dialogue between you and I, instead of take the substance of it - for I know precious well, beforehand, what it will be; nobody know well than I do, take notice! - instead of receive it on trust from my mouth.'
Stephen bend his head to the gentleman from London, and show a rather more troubled mind than usual. he turn his eye involuntarily to his former refuge, but at a look from that quarter (expressive though instantaneous) he settle they on Mr. Bounderby's face.
'now, what do you complain of?' ask Mr. Bounderby.
'I ha' not coom here, sir,' Stephen remind he, 'to complain. I coom for that I be send for.'
'what,' repeat Mr. Bounderby, fold his arm, 'do you people, in a general way, complain of?'
Stephen look at he with some little irresolution for a moment, and then seem to make up his mind.
'Sir, I be never good at showin o 't, though I ha had'n my share in feel o 't. 'Deed we be in a muddle, sir. look round town - so rich as 'tis - and see the number o' people as have be broughten into bein heer, fur to weave, an' to card, an' to piece out a livin', aw the same one way, somehow, 'twixt their cradle and their graf. look how we live, an' wheer we live, an' in what number, an' by what chance, and wi' what sameness; and look how the mill be awlu a goin, and how they never work we no nigh to ony dis'ant object - ceptin awlus, death. look how you consider of we, and write of we, and talk of we, and go up wi' yor deputation to secretary o' state 'bout we, and how yo be awlus right, and how we be awlus wrong, and never had'n no reason in we sin ever we be bear. look how this ha growen an' growen, sir, big an' big, broad an' broad, hard an' hard, fro year to year, fro generation unto generation. who can look on 't, sir, and fairly tell a man 'tis not a muddle?'
'of course,' say Mr. Bounderby. 'now perhaps youwill let the gentleman know, how you would set this muddle (as yoube so fond of call it) to right.'
'I donno, sir. I canna be expecten to 't. 'tis not I as should be looken to for that, sir. 'tis they as be put ower I, and ower aw the rest of we. what do they tak upon themseln, sir, if not to do't?'
'Iwill tell you something towards it, at any rate,' return Mr. Bounderby. 'we will make an example of half a dozen Slackbridges. wewill indict the blackguard for felony, and get them ship off to penal settlement.'
Stephen gravely shake his head.
'donot tell I we willnot, man,' say Mr. Bounderby, by this time blow a hurricane, 'because we will, I tell you!'
'Sir,' return Stephen, with the quiet confidence of absolute certainty, 'if yo be t' tak a hundred Slackbridges - aw as there be, and aw the number ten time towd - an' was t' sew them up in separate sack, an' sink them in the deep ocean as be make ere ever dry land coom to be, yo'd leave the muddle just wheer 'tis. mischeevous stranger!' say Stephen, with an anxious smile; 'when ha we not heern, I be sure, sin ever we can call to mind, o' th' mischeevous stranger! 'tis not by they the trouble's make, sir. 'tis not wi' they 't commence. I ha no favour for them - I ha no reason to favour them - but 'tis hopeless and useless to dream o' takin they fro their trade, 'stead o' takin their trade fro they! aw thatbe now about I in this room be heer afore I coom, an' will be heer when I be go. put that clock aboard a ship an' pack it off to Norfolk Island, an' the time will go on just the same. so 'tis wi' Slackbridge every bit.'
revert for a moment to his former refuge, he observe a cautionary movement of her eye towards the door. step back, he put his hand upon the lock. but he have not speak out of his own will and desire; and he feel it in his heart a noble return for his late injurious treatment to be faithful to the last to those who have repudiate he. he stay to finish what be in his mind.
'Sir, I canna, wi' my little learn an' my common way, tell the genelman what will well aw this - though some work man o' this town could, above my power - but I can tell he what I know will never do 't. the strong hand will never do 't. Vict'ry and triumph will never do 't. agree fur to mak one side unnat'rally awlus and for ever right, and toother side unnat'rally awlus and for ever wrong, will never, never do 't. nor yet lettin alone will never do 't. let thousand upon thousand alone, aw lead the like life and aw faw'en into the like muddle, and they will be as one, and yo will be as anoother, wi' a black unpassable world betwixt yo, just as long or short a time as sich-like misery can last. not drawin nigh to fok, wi' kindness and patience an' cheery way, that so draw nigh to one another in their monny trouble, and so cherish one another in their distress wi' what they need themseln - like, I humbly believe, as no people the genelman ha see in aw his travel can beat - will never do 't till th' Sun turn t' ice. Most o' aw, rat them as so much power, and reg'latin them as if they be figure in a soom, or machine: wi'out love and liken, wi'out memory and inclination, wi'out soul to weary and soul to hope - when aw go quiet, draggin on wi' them as if theywould nowt o' th' kind, and when aw go onquiet, reproachin them for their want o' sitch humanly feelin in their dealin wi' yo - this will never do 't, sir, till God's work be onmade.'
Stephen stand with the open door in his hand, wait to know if anything more be expect of he.
'just stop a moment,' say Mr. Bounderby, excessively red in the face. 'I tell you, the last time you be here with a grievance, that you have well turn about and come out of that. and I also tell you, if you remember, that I be up to the gold spoon look- out.'
'I be not up to 't myseln, sir; I do assure yo.'
'now itbe clear to I,' say Mr. Bounderby, 'that you be one of those chap who have always get a grievance. and you go about, sow it and raise crop. thatbe the business of your life, my friend.'
Stephen shake his head, mutely protest that indeed he have other business to do for his life.
'you be such a waspish, raspish, ill-condition chap, you see,' say Mr. Bounderby, 'that even your own Union, the man who know you well, will have nothing to do with you. I never think those fellow could be right in anything; but I tell you what! I so far go along with they for a novelty, that Iwill have nothing to do with you either.'
Stephen raise his eye quickly to his face.
'you can finish off what yoube at,' say Mr. Bounderby, with a mean nod, 'and then go elsewhere.'
'Sir, yo know weel,' say Stephen expressively, 'that if I canna get work wi' yo, I canna get it elsewheer.'
the reply be, 'what I know, I know; and what you know, you know. I have no more to say about it.'
Stephen glance at Louisa again, but her eye be raise to his no more; therefore, with a sigh, and say, barely above his breath, 'heaven help we aw in this world!' he depart.
CHAPTER VI - fade AWAY
it be fall dark when Stephen come out of Mr. Bounderby's house. the shadow of night have gather so fast, that he do not look about he when he close the door, but plod straight along the street. nothing be further from his thought than the curious old woman he have encounter on his previous visit to the same house, when he hear a step behind he that he know, and turning, see she in Rachael's company.
he see Rachael first, as he have hear she only.
'ah, Rachael, my dear! Missus, thou wi' she!'

'why, I come to be with this good lass pretty much as I come to be with you,' say the old woman, cheerfully, take the reply upon herself. 'my visit time be later this year than usual, for I have be rather trouble with shortness of breath, and so put it off till the weather be fine and warm. for the same reason I donot make all my journey in one day, but divide it into two day, and get a bed to-night at the Travellers' Coffee House down by the railroad (a nice clean house), and go back parliamentary, at six in the morning. well, but what have this to do with this good lass, say you? Ibe go to tell you. I have hear of Mr. Bounderby be marry. I read it in the paper, where it look grand - oh, it look fine!' the old woman dwelt on it with strange enthusiasm: 'and I want to see his wife. I have never see she yet. now, if youwill believe I, she havenot come out of that house since noon to- day. so not to give she up too easily, I be wait about, a little last bit more, when I pass close to this good lass two or three time; and her face be so friendly I speak to she, and she speak to I. there!' say the old woman to Stephen, 'you can make all the rest out for yourself now, a deal short than I can, I dare say!'
once again, Stephen have to conquer an instinctive propensity to dislike this old woman, though her manner be as honest and simple as a manner possibly could be. with a gentleness that be as natural to he as he know it to be to Rachael, he pursue the subject that interest she in her old age.
'well, missus,' say he, 'I ha see the lady, and she be young and hansom. Wi' fine dark thinkin eye, and a still way, Rachael, as I ha never see the like on.'

'I donno t'night,' say he, lift off his hat, and smooth his thin hair with the flat of his hand. 'but Ibe not goin t'night, Rachael, nor yet t'morrow. 'tan't easy overmuch t' know wheer t' turn, but a good heart will coom to I.'
Herein, too, the sense of even think unselfishly aid he. before he have so much as close Mr. Bounderby's door, he have reflect that at least his be oblige to go away be good for she, as it would save she from the chance of be bring into question for not withdraw from he. though it would cost he a hard pang to leave she, and though he could think of no similar place in which his condemnation would not pursue he, perhaps it be almost a relief to be force away from the endurance of the last four day, even to unknown difficulty and distress.

Age, especially when it strive to be self-reliant and cheerful, find much consideration among the poor. the old woman be so decent and content, and make so light of her infirmity, though they have increase upon she since her former interview with Stephen, that they both take an interest in she. she be too sprightly to allow of their walking at a slow pace on her account, but she be very grateful to be talk to, and very willing to talk to any extent: so, when they come to their part of the town, she be more brisk and vivacious than ever.

'I ha never thowt yet, missus,' say Stephen, 'o' askin thy name.'
the old lady announce herself as 'Mrs. Pegler.'
'a widder, I think?' say Stephen.
'oh, many long year!' Mrs. Pegler's husband (one of the good on record) be already dead, by Mrs. Pegler's calculation, when Stephen be bear.
''twere a bad job, too, to lose so good a one,' say Stephen. 'Onny child?'

'dead, Stephen,' Rachael softly hint.

for the first time in her life Louisa have come into one of the dwelling of the Coketown Hands; for the first time in her life she be face to face with anything like individuality in connection with they. she know of their existence by hundred and by thousand. she know what result in work a give number of they would produce in a give space of time. she know they in crowd pass to and from their nest, like ant or beetle. but she know from her read infinitely more of the way of toil insect than of these toil man and woman.
something to be work so much and pay so much, and there end; something to be infallibly settle by law of supply and demand; something that blunder against those law, and flounder into difficulty; something that be a little pinched when wheat be dear, and over-eat itself when wheat be cheap; something that increase at such a rate of percentage, and yield such another percentage of crime, and such another percentage of pauperism; something wholesale, of which vast fortune be make; something that occasionally rise like a sea, and do some harm and waste (chiefly to itself), and fall again; this she know the Coketown Hands to be. but, she have scarcely think more of separate they into unit, than of separate the sea itself into its component drop.

Tom have sit upon the bed, swing one leg and suck his walking- stick with sufficient unconcern, until the visit have attain this stage. see his sister ready to depart, he get up, rather hurriedly, and put in a word.

it be but a hurried parting in a common street, yet it be a sacred remembrance to these two common people. utilitarian economist, skeleton of schoolmaster, Commissioners of Fact, genteel and use-up infidel, gabbler of many little dog'-eare creed, the poor you will have always with you. cultivate in they, while there be yet time, the utmost grace of the fancy and affection, to adorn their life so much in need of ornament; or, in the day of your triumph, when romance be utterly drive out of their soul, and they and a bare existence stand face to face, reality will take a wolfish turn, and make an end of you.
Stephen work the next day, and the next, uncheered by a word from any one, and shun in all his coming and going as before. at the end of the second day, he see land; at the end of the third, his loom stand empty.
he have overstay his hour in the street outside the Bank, on each of the two first evening; and nothing have happen there, good or bad. that he might not be remiss in his part of the engagement, he resolve to wait full two hour, on this third and last night.

he have only to take leave of his landlady, and lie down on his temporary bed upon the floor; for his bundle be make up for to- morrow, and all be arrange for his departure. he mean to be clear of the town very early; before the Hands be in the street.

by the place where Rachael live, though it be not in his way; by the red brick street; by the great silent factory, not tremble yet; by the railway, where the danger-light be wan in the strengthening day; by the railway's crazy neighbourhood, half pull down and half build up; by scatter red brick villa, where the besmoke evergreen be sprinkle with a dirty powder, like untidy snuff-taker; by coal-dust path and many variety of ugliness; Stephen get to the top of the hill, and look back.

so strange to turn from the chimney to the bird. so strange, to have the road-dust on his foot instead of the coal-grit. so strange to have live to his time of life, and yet to be begin like a boy this summer morning! with these musing in his mind, and his bundle under his arm, Stephen take his attentive face along the high road. and the tree arch over he, whisper that he leave a true and love heart behind.
CHAPTER VII - GUNPOWDER
MR. JAMES HARTHOUSE, 'go in' for his adopt party, soon begin to score. with the aid of a little more coach for the political sage, a little more genteel listlessness for the general society, and a tolerable management of the assumed honesty in dishonesty, most effective and most patronize of the polite deadly sin, he speedily come to be consider of much promise. the not be trouble with earnestness be a grand point in his favour, enable he to take to the hard fact fellow with as good a grace as if he have be bear one of the tribe, and to throw all other tribe overboard, as conscious hypocrite.
'whom none of we believe, my dear Mrs. Bounderby, and who do not believe themselves. the only difference between we and the professor of virtue or benevolence, or philanthropy - never mind the name - be, that we know it be all meaningless, and say so; while they know it equally and will never say so.'
why should she be shock or warn by this reiteration? it be not so unlike her father's principle, and her early training, that it need startle she. where be the great difference between the two school, when each chain she down to material reality, and inspire she with no faith in anything else? what be there in her soul for James Harthouse to destroy, which Thomas Gradgrind have nurture there in its state of innocence!
it be even the bad for she at this pass, that in her mind - implant there before her eminently practical father begin to form it - a struggle disposition to believe in a wide and nobler humanity than she have ever hear of, constantly strove with doubt and resentment. with doubt, because the aspiration have be so lay waste in her youth. with resentment, because of the wrong that have be do she, if it be indeed a whisper of the truth. upon a nature long accustom to self-suppression, thus tear and divide, the Harthouse philosophy come as a relief and justification. everything be hollow and worthless, she have miss nothing and sacrifice nothing. what do it matter, she have say to her father, when he propose her husband. what do it matter, she say still. with a scornful self-reliance, she ask herself, what do anything matter - and go on.
towards what? step by step, onward and downward, towards some end, yet so gradually, that she believe herself to remain motionless. as to Mr. Harthouse, whither he tend, he neither consider nor care. he have no particular design or plan before he: no energetic wickedness ruffle his lassitude. he be as much amuse and interested, at present, as it become so fine a gentleman to be; perhaps even more than it would have be consistent with his reputation to confess. soon after his arrival he languidly write to his brother, the honourable and jocular member, that the bounderby be 'great fun;' and far, that the female Bounderby, instead of be the Gorgon he have expect, be young, and remarkably pretty. after that, he write no more about they, and devote his leisure chiefly to their house. he be very often in their house, in his flitting and visiting about the Coketown district; and be much encourage by Mr. Bounderby. it be quite in Mr. Bounderby's gusty way to boast to all his world that he donot care about your highly connected people, but that if his wife Tom Gradgrind's daughter do, she be welcome to their company.
Mr. James Harthouse begin to think it would be a new sensation, if the face which change so beautifully for the whelp, would change for he.
he be quick enough to observe; he have a good memory, and do not forget a word of the brother's revelation. he interwove they with everything he see of the sister, and he begin to understand she. to be sure, the well and profounder part of her character be not within his scope of perception; for in nature, as in sea, depth answer unto depth; but he soon begin to read the rest with a student's eye.
Mr. Bounderby have take possession of a house and ground, about fifteen mile from the town, and accessible within a mile or two, by a railway stride on many arch over a wild country, undermine by desert coal-shafts, and spot at night by fire and black shape of stationary engine at pit' mouth. this country, gradually soften towards the neighbourhood of Mr. Bounderby's retreat, there mellow into a rustic landscape, golden with heath, and snowy with hawthorn in the spring of the year, and tremulous with leaf and their shadow all the summer time. the bank have foreclose a mortgage effect on the property thus pleasantly situate, by one of the Coketown magnate, who, in his determination to make a short cut than usual to an enormous fortune, overspeculate himself by about two hundred thousand pound. these accident do sometimes happen in the well regulated family of Coketown, but the bankrupt have no connexion whatever with the improvident class.
it afford Mr. Bounderby supreme satisfaction to instal himself in this snug little estate, and with demonstrative humility to grow cabbage in the flower-garden. he delight to live, barrack- fashion, among the elegant furniture, and he bully the very picture with his origin. 'why, sir,' he would say to a visitor, 'I be tell that nickit,' the late owner, 'give seven hundred pound for that Seabeach. now, to be plain with you, if I ever, in the whole course of my life, take seven look at it, at a hundred pound a look, it will be as much as I shall do. no, by George! I donot forget that I be Josiah Bounderby of Coketown. for year upon year, the only picture in my possession, or that I could have get into my possession, by any mean, unless I steal them, be the engraving of a man shave himself in a boot, on the black bottle that I be overjoyed to use in clean boot with, and that I sell when they be empty for a farthing a-piece, and glad to get it!'
then he would address Mr. Harthouse in the same style.
'Harthouse, you have a couple of horse down here. bring half a dozen more if you like, and wewill find room for them. therebe stable in this place for a dozen horse; and unless Nickits be belie, he keep the full number. a round dozen of them, sir. when that man be a boy, he go to Westminster School. go to Westminster School as a King's Scholar, when I be principally live on garbage, and sleep in market basket. why, if I want to keep a dozen horse - which I donot, for one's enough for I - I couldnot bear to see them in their stall here, and think what my own lodging use to be. I couldnot look at them, sir, and not order them out. yet so thing come round. you see this place; you know what sort of a place it be; you be aware that therebe not a completer place of its size in this kingdom or elsewhere - I donot care where - and here, get into the middle of it, like a maggot into a nut, be Josiah Bounderby. while nickit (as a man come into my office, and tell I yesterday), Nickits, who use to act in Latin, in the Westminster School play, with the chief- justice and nobility of this country applaud he till they be black in the face, be drivel at this minute - drivelling, sir! - in a fifth floor, up a narrow dark back street in Antwerp.'
it be among the leafy shadow of this retirement, in the long sultry summer day, that Mr. Harthouse begin to prove the face which have set he wonder when he first see it, and to try if it would change for he.
'Mrs. Bounderby, I esteem it a most fortunate accident that I find you alone here. I have for some time have a particular wish to speak to you.'

he sit down beside she, with a glance at her face.
'your brother. my young friend Tom - '

she have make the slight action possible, as if she would have rise in a hurry and go away. he have turn the course of what he say at that instant, and she remain.

'yes.'
'allow I to be frank. do you think he game at all?'
'I think he make bet.' Mr. Harthouse wait, as if that be not her whole answer, she add, 'I know he do.'
'of course he lose?'
'yes.'
'everybody do lose who bet. may I hint at the probability of your sometimes supply he with money for these purpose?'
she sit, look down; but, at this question, raise her eye searchingly and a little resentfully.
'acquit I of impertinent curiosity, my dear Mrs. Bounderby. I think Tom may be gradually fall into trouble, and I wish to stretch out a help hand to he from the depth of my wicked experience. - shall I say again, for his sake? be that necessary?'
she seem to try to answer, but nothing come of it.

'I do not,' say Louisa, flush with her own great remembrance in that wise, 'think it likely.'

I would never complain of anything, and what I have do I do not in the least regret.'
'so spirited, too!' think James Harthouse.
'when I marry, I find that my brother be even at that time heavily in debt. heavily for he, I mean. heavily enough to oblige I to sell some trinket. they be no sacrifice. I sell they very willingly. I attach no value to they. they, be quite worthless to I.'
either she see in his face that he know, or she only fear in her conscience that he know, that she speak of some of her husband's gift. she stop, and redden again. if he have not know it before, he would have know it then, though he have be a much dull man than he be.
'since then, I have give my brother, at various time, what money I could spare: in short, what money I have have. confide in you at all, on the faith of the interest you profess for he, I will not do so by half. since you have be in the habit of visit here, he have want in one sum as much as a hundred pound. I have hold no confidence with any one, because - you anticipate my reason just now.' she abruptly break off.
he be a ready man, and he see, and seize, an opportunity here of present her own image to she, slightly disguise as her brother.
'Mrs. Bounderby, though a graceless person, of the world worldly, I feel the utmost interest, I assure you, in what you tell I. I cannot possibly be hard upon your brother. the wood float before she, for her eye be suffuse with tear. they rise from a deep well, long conceal, and her heart be fill with acute pain that find no relief in they.

he assist she to rise, and she take his arm, and they advance to meet the whelp. he be idly beat the branch as he lounge along: or he stoop viciously to rip the moss from the tree with his stick. he be startle when they come upon he while he be engage in this latter pastime, and his colour change.

'you have a suspicious appearance of inscribe some fair creature's on the bark, Tom.'
'not much of that, Mr. Harthouse, unless some fair creature with a slash fortune at her own disposal would take a fancy to I. or she might be as ugly as she be rich, without any fear of lose I. Iwould carve her name as often as she like.'
'I be afraid you be mercenary, Tom.'
'mercenary,' repeat Tom. 'who be not mercenary? ask my sister.'

they walk on to the house, where Louisa quit her visitor's arm and go in. he stand look after she, as she ascend the step, and pass into the shadow of the door; then put his hand upon her brother's shoulder again, and invite he with a confidential nod to a walk in the garden.
'Tom, my fine fellow, I want to have a word with you.'
they have stop among a disorder of rose - it be part of Mr. Bounderby's humility to keep Nickits's rose on a reduce scale - and Tom sit down on a terrace-parapet, pluck bud and pick they to piece; while his powerful Familiar stand over he, with a foot upon the parapet, and his figure easily rest on the arm support by that knee. they be just visible from her window. perhaps she see they.

'my good fellow, so be I.'

he take to bite the rosebud now, and tear they away from his tooth with a hand that tremble like an infirm old man's. after one exceedingly observant look at he, his companion relapse into his light air.

he be almost cry, and scatter the bud about by dozen. Mr. Harthouse take he persuasively by the coat.

I donot know what you may call this, but I call it unnatural conduct.'
there be a piece of ornamental water immediately below the parapet, on the other side, into which Mr. James Harthouse have a very strong inclination to pitch Mr. Thomas Gradgrind junior, as the injured man of Coketown threaten to pitch their property into the Atlantic. but he preserve his easy attitude; and nothing more solid go over the stone balustrades than the accumulate rosebud now float about, a little surface-island.
'my dear Tom,' say Harthouse, 'let I try to be your banker.'

Mr. Harthouse, as a thoroughly well-breed man, accustom to the good society, be not to be surprised - he could as soon have be affect - but he raise his eyelid a little more, as if they be lift by a feeble touch of wonder. albeit it be as much against the precept of his school to wonder, as it be against the doctrine of the Gradgrind College.

a true friend! 'Whelp, whelp!' think Mr. Harthouse, lazily; 'what an ass you be!'
'and I take your offer as a great kindness,' say Tom, grasp his hand. 'as a great kindness, Mr. Harthouse.'
'well,' return the other, 'it may be of more use by and by. and, my good fellow, if you will open your bedevilment to I when they come thick upon you, I may show you well way out of they than you can find for yourself.'

'now, you see, Tom,' say Mr. Harthouse in conclusion, himself toss over a rose or two, as a contribution to the island, which be always drift to the wall as if it want to become a part of the mainland: 'every man be selfish in everything he do, and I be exactly like the rest of my fellow-creature. 'I will be, Mr. Harthouse.'
'no time like the present, Tom. begin at once.'
'certainly I will. and my sister Loo shall say so.'

when Tom appear before dinner, though his mind seem heavy enough, his body be on the alert; and he appear before Mr. Bounderby come in. 'I donot mean to be cross, Loo,' he say, give she his hand, and kiss she. 'I know you be fond of I, and you know I be fond of you.'
after this, there be a smile upon Louisa's face that day, for some one else. alas, for some one else!
'so much the less be the whelp the only creature that she care for,' think James Harthouse, reverse the reflection of his first day's knowledge of her pretty face. 'so much the less, so much the less.'
CHAPTER VIII - EXPLOSION
the next morning be too bright a morning for sleep, and James Harthouse rise early, and sit in the pleasant bay window of his dressing-room, smoke the rare tobacco that have have so wholesome an influence on his young friend. repose in the sunlight, with the fragrance of his eastern pipe about he, and the dreamy smoke vanish into the air, so rich and soft with summer odour, he reckon up his advantage as an idle winner might count his gain. he be not at all bored for the time, and could give his mind to it.
he have establish a confidence with she, from which her husband be exclude. he have establish a confidence with she, that absolutely turn upon her indifference towards her husband, and the absence, now and at all time, of any congeniality between they. he have artfully, but plainly, assure she that he know her heart in its last most delicate recess; he have come so near to she through its tender sentiment; he have associate himself with that feeling; and the barrier behind which she live, have melt away. all very odd, and very satisfactory!
and yet he have not, even now, any earnest wickedness of purpose in he. publicly and privately, it be much well for the age in which he live, that he and the legion of whom he be one be designedly bad, than indifferent and purposeless. it be the drift iceberg set with any current anywhere, that wreck the ship.
when the devil goeth about like a roar lion, he goeth about in a shape by which few but savage and hunter be attract. but, when he be trim, smooth, and varnish, accord to the mode; when he be aweary of vice, and aweary of virtue, use up as to brimstone, and use up as to bliss; then, whether he take to the serve out of red tape, or to the kindling of red fire, he be the very Devil.
so James Harthouse recline in the window, indolently smoking, and reckon up the step he have take on the road by which he happen to be travel. the end to which it lead be before he, pretty plainly; but he trouble himself with no calculation about it. what will be, will be.
as he have rather a long ride to take that day - for there be a public occasion 'to do' at some distance, which afford a tolerable opportunity of go in for the Gradgrind man - he dress early and go down to breakfast. he be anxious to see if she have relapse since the previous evening. no. he resume where he have leave off. there be a look of interest for he again.
he get through the day as much (or as little) to his own satisfaction, as be to be expect under the fatigue circumstance; and come rid back at six o'clock. there be a sweep of some half-mile between the lodge and the house, and he be rid along at a foot pace over the smooth gravel, once Nickits's, when Mr. Bounderby burst out of the shrubbery, with such violence as to make his horse shy across the road.
'Harthouse!' cry Mr. Bounderby. 'have you hear?'
'heard what?' say Harthouse, soothe his horse, and inwardly favour Mr. Bounderby with no good wish.
'then you havenot hear!'
'I have hear you, and so have this brute. I have hear nothing else.'
Mr. Bounderby, red and hot, plant himself in the centre of the path before the horse's head, to explode his bombshell with more effect.
'the Bank's rob!'
'you donot mean it!'
'rob last night, sir. rob in an extraordinary manner. rob with a false key.'
'of much?'
Mr. Bounderby, in his desire to make the most of it, really seem mortify by be oblige to reply, 'why, no; not of very much. but it might have be.'
'of how much?'
'oh! as a sum - if you stick to a sum - of not more than a hundred and fifty pound,' say Bounderby, with impatience. 'but itbe not the sum; itbe the fact. itbe the fact of the Bank be rob, thatbe the important circumstance. I be surprised you donot see it.'
'my dear Bounderby,' say James, dismounting, and give his bridle to his servant, 'I do see it; and be as overcome as you can possibly desire I to be, by the spectacle afford to my mental view. nevertheless, I may be allow, I hope, to congratulate you - which I do with all my soul, I assure you - on your not have sustain a great loss.'
'thank'ee,' reply Bounderby, in a short, ungracious manner. 'but I tell you what. it might have be twenty thousand pound.'
'I suppose it might.'
'suppose it might! by the Lord, you may suppose so. by George!' say Mr. Bounderby, with sundry menacing nod and shake of his head. 'it might have be twice twenty. therebe no knowing what it would have be, or wouldnot have be, as it be, but for the fellow' be disturb.'
Louisa have come up now, and Mrs. Sparsit, and Bitzer.
'here's Tom Gradgrind's daughter know pretty well what it might have be, if you donot,' bluster Bounderby. 'drop, sir, as if she be shoot when I tell she! never know she do such a thing before. do her credit, under the circumstance, in my opinion!'
she still look faint and pale. James Harthouse beg she to take his arm; and as they move on very slowly, ask she how the robbery have be commit.
'why, I be go to tell you,' say Bounderby, irritably give his arm to Mrs. Sparsit. 'if you havenot be so mighty particular about the sum, I should have begin to tell you before. you know this lady (for she be a lady), Mrs. Sparsit?'
'I have already have the honour - '
'very well. and this young man, Bitzer, you see he too on the same occasion?' Mr. Harthouse incline his head in assent, and Bitzer knuckle his forehead.
'very well. they live at the Bank. you know they live at the Bank, perhaps? very well. yesterday afternoon, at the close of business hour, everything be put away as usual. in the iron room that this young fellow sleep outside of, there be never mind how much. in the little safe in young Tom's closet, the safe use for petty purpose, there be a hundred and fifty odd pound.'
'a hundred and fifty-four, seven, one,' say Bitzer.
'come!' retort Bounderby, stop to wheel round upon he, 'letus have none of your interruption. itbe enough to be rob while yoube snore because yoube too comfortable, without be put right with your four seven one. I donot snore, myself, when I be your age, let I tell you. I havenot victual enough to snore. and I donot four seven one. not if I know it.'
Bitzer knuckle his forehead again, in a sneaking manner, and seem at once particularly impressed and depress by the instance last give of Mr. Bounderby's moral abstinence.
'a hundred and fifty odd pound,' resume Mr. Bounderby. 'that sum of money, young Tom lock in his safe, not a very strong safe, but thatbe no matter now. everything be leave, all right. some time in the night, while this young fellow snore - Mrs. Sparsit, ma'am, you say you have hear he snore?'
'Sir,' return Mrs. Sparsit, 'I cannot say that I have hear he precisely snore, and therefore must not make that statement. but on winter evening, when he have fall asleep at his table, I have hear he, what I should prefer to describe as partially choke. I have hear he on such occasion produce sound of a nature similar to what may be sometimes hear in dutch clock. not,' say Mrs. Sparsit, with a lofty sense of give strict evidence, 'that I would convey any imputation on his moral character. far from it. I have always consider Bitzer a young man of the most upright principle; and to that I beg to bear my testimony.'
'well!' say the exasperate Bounderby, 'while he be snore, or choke, or Dutch-clocking, or something or other - be asleep - some fellow, somehow, whether previously conceal in the house or not remain to be see, get to young Tom's safe, force it, and abstract the content. be then disturb, they make off; let themselves out at the main door, and double-lock it again (it be double-locked, and the key under Mrs. Sparsit's pillow) with a false key, which be pick up in the street near the Bank, about twelve o'clock to-day. no alarm take place, till this chap, Bitzer, turn out this morning, and begin to open and prepare the office for business. then, look at Tom's safe, he see the door ajar, and find the lock force, and the money go.'

another of the popular fiction of Coketown, which some pain have be take to disseminate - and which some people really believe.

CHAPTER IX - hear the LAST of it
MRS. SPARSIT, lie by to recover the tone of her nerve in Mr. Bounderby's retreat, keep such a sharp look-out, night and day, under her coriolanian eyebrow, that her eye, like a couple of lighthouse on an iron-bind coast, might have warn all prudent mariner from that bold rock her roman nose and the dark and craggy region in its neighbourhood, but for the placidity of her manner. although it be hard to believe that her retire for the night could be anything but a form, so severely wide awake be those classical eye of her, and so impossible do it seem that her rigid nose could yield to any relaxing influence, yet her manner of sit, smooth her uncomfortable, not to say, gritty mitten (they be construct of a cool fabric like a meat-safe), or of amble to unknown place of destination with her foot in her cotton stirrup, be so perfectly serene, that most observer would have be constrain to suppose she a dove, embody by some freak of nature, in the earthly tabernacle of a bird of the hook-beak order.
she be a most wonderful woman for prowl about the house. how she get from story to story be a mystery beyond solution. a lady so decorous in herself, and so highly connected, be not to be suspect of drop over the banister or slide down they, yet her extraordinary facility of locomotion suggest the wild idea. another noticeable circumstance in Mrs. Sparsit be, that she be never hurry. she would shoot with consummate velocity from the roof to the hall, yet would be in full possession of her breath and dignity on the moment of her arrival there. neither be she ever see by human vision to go at a great pace.

CHAPTER X - MRS. SPARSIT's STAIRCASE

CHAPTER II - VERY RIDICULOUS
MR. JAMES HARTHOUSE pass a whole night and a day in a state of so much hurry, that the World, with its good glass in his eye, would scarcely have recognize he during that insane interval, as the brother Jem of the honourable and jocular member. he be positively agitated. he several time speak with an emphasis, similar to the vulgar manner. he go in and go out in an unaccountable way, like a man without an object. he ride like a highwayman. in a word, he be so horribly bored by exist circumstance, that he forget to go in for boredom in the manner prescribe by the authority.

she be go.

Dear Jack, - all up at Coketown. bore out of the place, and go in for camel. affectionately, JEM,
he ring the bell.

the robbery at the Bank have not languish before, and do not cease to occupy a front place in the attention of the principal of that establishment now. in boastful proof of his promptitude and activity, as a remarkable man, and a self-make man, and a commercial wonder more admirable than Venus, who have rise out of the mud instead of the sea, he like to show how little his domestic affair abate his business ardour. consequently, in the first few week of his resume bachelorhood, he even advance upon his usual display of bustle, and every day make such a rout in renew his investigation into the robbery, that the officer who have it in hand almost wish it have never be commit.

''deed, I be loath,' return Rachael, dry her eye, 'that any here should see I like this; but I willnot be see so again. young lady, when I have read whatbe put in print of Stephen - and what have just as much truth in it as if it have be put in print of you - I go straight to the Bank to say I know where Stephen be, and to give a sure and certain promise that he should be here in two day. I couldnot meet wi' Mr. Bounderby then, and your brother send I away, and I try to find you, but you be not to be find, and I go back to work. soon as I come out of the Mill to-night, I hasten to hear what be say of Stephen - for I know wi' pride he will come back to shame it! - and then I go again to seek Mr. Bounderby, and I find he, and I tell he every word I know; and he believe no word I say, and bring I here.'
'so far, thatbe true enough,' assent Mr. Bounderby, with his hand in his pocket and his hat on. 'but I have know you people before to-day, youwill observe, and I know you never die for want of talk. now, I recommend you not so much to mind talk just now, as do. you have undertake to do something; all I remark upon that at present be, do it!'
'I have write to Stephen by the post that go out this afternoon, as I have write to he once before sin' he go away,' say Rachael; 'and he will be here, at furth, in two day.'
'then, Iwill tell you something. you be not aware perhaps,' retort Mr. Bounderby, 'that you yourself have be look after now and then, not be consider quite free from suspicion in this business, on account of most people be judge accord to the company they keep. the post-office havenot be forget either. what Iwill tell you be, that no letter to Stephen Blackpool have ever get into it. therefore, what have become of your, I leave you to guess. perhaps yoube mistaken, and never write any.'
'he havenot be go from here, young lady,' say Rachael, turn appealingly to Louisa, 'as much as a week, when he send I the only letter I have have from he, say that he be force to seek work in another name.'
'oh, by George!' cry Bounderby, shake his head, with a whistle, 'he change his name, do he! thatbe rather unlucky, too, for such an immaculate chap. itbe consider a little suspicious in Courts of Justice, I believe, when an Innocent happen to have many name.'
'what,' say Rachael, with the tear in her eye again, 'what, young lady, in the name of Mercy, be leave the poor lad to do! the master against he on one hand, the man against he on the other, he only wantin to work hard in peace, and do what he feel right. can a man have no soul of his own, no mind of his own? must he go wrong all through wi' this side, or must he go wrong all through wi' that, or else be hunt like a hare?'
'indeed, indeed, I pity he from my heart,' return Louisa; 'and I hope that he will clear himself.'
'you need have no fear of that, young lady. he be sure!'
'all the surer, I suppose,' say Mr. Bounderby, 'for your refuse to tell where he be? eh?'
'he shall not, through any act of mine, come back wi' the unmerited reproach of be bring back. he shall come back of his own accord to clear himself, and put all those that have injure his good character, and he not here for its defence, to shame. I have tell he what have be do against he,' say Rachael, throw off all distrust as a rock throw of the sea, 'and he will be here, at furth, in two day.'
'Notwithstanding which,' add Mr. Bounderby, 'if he can be lay hold of any soon, he shall have an early opportunity of clear himself. as to you, I have nothing against you; what you come and tell I turn out to be true, and I have give you the mean of prove it to be true, and therebe an end of it. I wish you good night all! I must be off to look a little far into this.'
Tom come out of his corner when Mr. Bounderby move, move with he, keep close to he, and go away with he. the only part salutation of which he deliver himself be a sulky 'good night, father!' with a brief speech, and a scowl at his sister, he leave the house.
since his sheet-anchor have come home, Mr. Gradgrind have be spar of speech. he still sit silent, when Louisa mildly say:
'Rachael, you will not distrust I one day, when you know I well.'
'it go against I,' Rachael answer, in a gentler manner, 'to mistrust any one; but when I be so mistrusted - when we all be - I cannot keep such thing quite out of my mind. I ask your pardon for have do you an injury. I donot think what I say now. yet I might come to think it again, wi' the poor lad so wrong.'
'do you tell he in your letter,' inquire Sissy, 'that suspicion seem to have fall upon he, because he have be see about the Bank at night? he would then know what he would have to explain on come back, and would be ready.'
'yes, dear,' she return; 'but I cannot guess what can have ever take he there. he never use to go there. it be never in his way. his way be the same as mine, and not near it.'
Sissy have already be at her side ask she where she live, and whether she might come to-morrow night, to inquire if there be news of he.
'I doubt,' say Rachael, 'if he can be here till next day.'
'then I will come next night too,' say Sissy.
when Rachael, assent to this, be go, Mr. Gradgrind lift up his head, and say to his daughter:
'Louisa, my dear, I have never, that I know of, see this man. do you believe he to be implicate?'
'I think I have believe it, father, though with great difficulty. I do not believe it now.'
'that be to say, you once persuade yourself to believe it, from know he to be suspect. his appearance and manner; be they so honest?'
'very honest.'
'and her confidence not to be shake! I ask myself,' say Mr. Gradgrind, musing, 'do the real culprit know of these accusation? where be he? who be he?'
his hair have latterly begin to change its colour. as he lean upon his hand again, look gray and old, Louisa, with a face of fear and pity, hurriedly go over to he, and sit close at his side. her eye by accident meet Sissy's at the moment. Sissy flush and start, and Louisa put her finger on her lip.
next night, when Sissy return home and tell Louisa that Stephen be not come, she tell it in a whisper. next night again, when she come home with the same account, and add that he have not be hear of, she speak in the same low frightened tone. from the moment of that interchange of look, they never utter his name, or any reference to he, aloud; nor ever pursue the subject of the robbery, when Mr. Gradgrind speak of it.
the two appoint day run out, three day and night run out, and Stephen Blackpool be not come, and remain unheard of. on the fourth day, Rachael, with unabated confidence, but consider her despatch to have miscarry, go up to the Bank, and show her letter from he with his address, at a work colony, one of many, not upon the main road, sixty mile away. messenger be send to that place, and the whole town look for Stephen to be bring in next day.
during this whole time the whelp move about with Mr. Bounderby like his shadow, assist in all the proceeding. he be greatly excited, horribly fevere, bit his nail down to the quick, speak in a hard rattle voice, and with lip that be black and burn up. at the hour when the suspect man be look for, the whelp be at the station; offer to wager that he have make off before the arrival of those who be send in quest of he, and that he would not appear.
the whelp be right. the messenger return alone. Rachael's letter have go, Rachael's letter have be deliver. Stephen Blackpool have decamp in that same hour; and no soul know more of he. the only doubt in Coketown be, whether Rachael have write in good faith, believe that he really would come back, or warn he to fly. on this point opinion be divide.
six day, seven day, far on into another week. the wretched whelp pluck up a ghastly courage, and begin to grow defiant. 'be the suspect fellow the thief? a pretty question! if not, where be the man, and why do he not come back?'
where be the man, and why do he not come back? in the dead of night the echo of his own word, which have roll Heaven know how far away in the daytime, come back instead, and abide by he until morning. 
CHAPTER V - FOUND
day and night again, day and night again. no Stephen Blackpool. where be the man, and why do he not come back?
every night, Sissy go to Rachael's lodging, and sit with she in her small neat room. all day, Rachael toil as such people must toil, whatever their anxiety. the smoke-serpent be indifferent who be lose or find, who turn out bad or good; the melancholy mad elephant, like the Hard Fact man, abate nothing of their set routine, whatever happen. day and night again, day and night again. the monotony be unbroken. even Stephen Blackpool's disappearance be fall into the general way, and become as monotonous a wonder as any piece of machinery in Coketown.
'I misdoubt,' say Rachael, 'if there be as many as twenty left in all this place, who have any trust in the poor dear lad now.'
she say it to Sissy, as they sit in her lodging, light only by the lamp at the street corner. Sissy have come there when it be already dark, to await her return from work; and they have since sit at the window where Rachael have find she, want no bright light to shine on their sorrowful talk.
'if it havenot be mercifully bring about, that I be to have you to speak to,' pursue Rachael, 'time be, when I think my mind would not have keep right. but I get hope and strength through you; and you believe that though appearance may rise against he, he will be prove clear?'
'I do believe so,' return Sissy, 'with my whole heart. I feel so certain, Rachael, that the confidence you hold in yours against all discouragement, be not like to be wrong, that I have no more doubt of he than if I have know he through as many year of trial as you have.'
'and I, my dear,' say Rachel, with a tremble in her voice, 'have know he through they all, to be, accord to his quiet way, so faithful to everything honest and good, that if he be never to be hear of more, and I be to live to be a hundred year old, I could say with my last breath, God know my heart. I have never once leave trust Stephen Blackpool!'
'we all believe, up at the Lodge, Rachael, that he will be free from suspicion, soon or later.'
'the well I know it to be so believe there, my dear,' say Rachael, 'and the kind I feel it that you come away from there, purposely to comfort I, and keep I company, and be see wi' I when I be not yet free from all suspicion myself, the more grieve I be that I should ever have speak those mistrust word to the young lady. and yet I - '
'you donot mistrust she now, Rachael?'
'now that you have bring we more together, no. but I cannot at all time keep out of my mind - '
her voice so sink into a low and slow communing with herself, that Sissy, sit by her side, be oblige to listen with attention.
'I cannot at all time keep out of my mind, mistrusting of some one. I cannot think who 'tis, I cannot think how or why it may be do, but I mistrust that some one have put Stephen out of the way. I mistrust that by his come back of his own accord, and show himself innocent before they all, some one would be confound, who - to prevent that - have stop he, and put he out of the way.'
'that be a dreadful thought,' say Sissy, turn pale.
'it be a dreadful thought to think he may be murder.'
Sissy shudder, and turn paler yet.
'when it make its way into my mind, dear,' say Rachael, 'and it will come sometimes, though I do all I can to keep it out, wi' counting on to high number as I work, and say over and over again piece that I know when I be a child - I fall into such a wild, hot hurry, that, however tired I be, I want to walk fast, mile and mile. I must get the well of this before bed-time. Iwill walk home wi' you.'
'he might fall ill upon the journey back,' say Sissy, faintly offer a wear-out scrap of hope; 'and in such a case, there be many place on the road where he might stop.'
'but he be in none of they. he have be seek for in all, and hebe not there.'
'true,' be Sissy's reluctant admission.
'hewould walk the journey in two day. if he be footsore and couldnot walk, I send he, in the letter he get, the money to ride, lest he should have none of his own to spare.'
'let we hope that to-morrow will bring something well, Rachael. come into the air!'
her gentle hand adjust Rachael's shawl upon her shin black hair in the usual manner of she wear it, and they go out. the night be fine, little knot of Hands be here and there linger at street corner; but it be supper-time with the great part of they, and there be but few people in the street.
'yoube not so hurried now, Rachael, and your hand be cool.'
'I get well, dear, if I can only walk, and breathe a little fresh. 'time when I cannot, I turn weak and confused.'
