'yes, dear.'
they be by this time in the street where Mr. Bounderby's house stand. the way to Sissy's destination lead they past the door, and they be go straight towards it. some train have newly arrive in Coketown, which have put a number of vehicle in motion, and scatter a considerable bustle about the town. several coach be rattle before they and behind they as they approach Mr. Bounderby's, and one of the latter draw up with such briskness as they be in the act of pass the house, that they look round involuntarily. the bright gaslight over Mr. Bounderby's step show they Mrs. Sparsit in the coach, in an ecstasy of excitement, struggle to open the door; Mrs. Sparsit see they at the same moment, call to they to stop.
'itbe a coincidence,' exclaim Mrs. Sparsit, as she be release by the coachman. 'itbe a providence! come out, ma'am!' then say Mrs. Sparsit, to some one inside, 'come out, or wewill have you drag out!'
Hereupon, no other than the mysterious old woman descend. whom Mrs. Sparsit incontinently collar.
'leave she alone, everybody!' cry Mrs. Sparsit, with great energy. 'let nobody touch she. she belong to I. come in, ma'am!' then say Mrs. Sparsit, reverse her former word of command. 'come in, ma'am, or wewill have you drag in!'
the spectacle of a matron of classical deportment, seize an ancient woman by the throat, and haul she into a dwelling-house, would have be under any circumstance, sufficient temptation to all true english straggler so blest as to witness it, to force a way into that dwelling-house and see the matter out. but when the phenomenon be enhance by the notoriety and mystery by this time associate all over the town with the Bank robbery, it would have lure the straggler in, with an irresistible attraction, though the roof have be expect to fall upon their head. accordingly, the chance witness on the ground, consist of the busy of the neighbour to the number of some five-and-twenty, close in after Sissy and Rachael, as they close in after Mrs. Sparsit and her prize; and the whole body make a disorderly irruption into Mr. Bounderby's dining-room, where the people behind lose not a moment's time in mount on the chair, to get the well of the people in front.
'Fetch Mr. Bounderby down!' cry Mrs. Sparsit. 'Rachael, young woman; you know who this be?'
'itbe Mrs. Pegler,' say Rachael.
'I should think it be!' cry Mrs. Sparsit, exult. 'Fetch Mr. Bounderby. stand away, everybody!' here old Mrs. Pegler, muffle herself up, and shrink from observation, whisper a word of entreaty. 'donot tell I,' say Mrs. Sparsit, aloud. 'I have tell you twenty time, come along, that I will not leave you till I have hand you over to he myself.'
Mr. Bounderby now appear, accompany by Mr. Gradgrind and the whelp, with whom he have be hold conference up-stair. Mr. Bounderby look more astonished than hospitable, at sight of this uninvited party in his dining-room.
'why, whatbe the matter now!' say he. 'Mrs. Sparsit, ma'am?'

here Mrs. Sparsit cease; for Mr. Bounderby's visage exhibit an extraordinary combination of all possible colour and expression of discomfiture, as old Mrs. Pegler be disclose to his view.

'Sir!' exclaim Mrs. Sparsit, faintly.

this allusion to her favourite feature overpower Mrs. Sparsit. she sit down stiffly in a chair, as if she be frozen; and with a fix stare at Mr. Bounderby, slowly grate her mitten against one another, as if they be freeze too.
'my dear Josiah!' cry Mrs. Pegler, tremble. 'my darling boy! I be not to blame. itbe not my fault, Josiah. I tell this lady over and over again, that I know she be do what would not be agreeable to you, but she would do it.'

'my own boy! she threaten I that if I resist she, I should be bring by constable, and it be well to come quietly than make that stir in such a' - Mrs. Pegler glance timidly but proudly round the wall - 'such a fine house as this. indeed, indeed, it be not my fault! my dear, noble, stately boy! I have always live quiet, and secret, Josiah, my dear. I have never break the condition once. I have never say I be your mother. I have admire you at a distance; and if I have come to town sometimes, with long time between, to take a proud peep at you, I have do it unbeknown, my love, and go away again.'
Mr. Bounderby, with his hand in his pocket, walk in impatient mortification up and down at the side of the long dining-table, while the spectator greedily take in every syllable of Mrs. Pegler's appeal, and at each succeed syllable become more and more round-eye. Mr. Bounderby still walk up and down when Mrs. Pegler have do, Mr. Gradgrind address that malign old lady:

'I unnatural!' cry poor old Mrs. Pegler. 'I inhuman! to my dear boy?'
'dear!' repeat Mr. Gradgrind. 'yes; dear in his self-make prosperity, madam, I dare say. not very dear, however, when you desert he in his infancy, and leave he to the brutality of a drunken grandmother.'
'I desert my Josiah!' cry Mrs. Pegler, clasp her hand. 'now, Lord forgive you, sir, for your wicked imagination, and for your scandal against the memory of my poor mother, who die in my arm before Josiah be bear. may you repent of it, sir, and live to know well!'
she be so very earnest and injure, that Mr. Gradgrind, shock by the possibility which dawn upon he, say in a gentle tone:

'and my dear boy know, and will give you to know, sir, that after his beloved father die, when he be eight year old, his mother, too, could pinch a bit, as it be her duty and her pleasure and her pride to do it, to help he out in life, and put he 'prentice. and a steady lad he be, and a kind master he have to lend he a hand, and well he work his own way forward to be rich and thriving. and Iwill give you to know, sir - for this my dear boy willnot - that though his mother keep but a little village shop, he never forget she, but pension I on thirty pound a year - more than I want, for I put by out of it - only make the condition that I be to keep down in my own part, and make no boast about he, and not trouble he. and I never have, except with look at he once a year, when he have never know it. and itbe right,' say poor old Mrs. Pegler, in affectionate championship, 'that I should keep down in my own part, and I have no doubt that if I be here I should do a many unbefitte thing, and I be well content, and I can keep my pride in my Josiah to myself, and I can love for love's own sake! and I be ashamed of you, sir,' say Mrs. Pegler, lastly, 'for your slander and suspicion. and I never stand here before, nor never want to stand here when my dear son say no. and I shouldnot be here now, if it havenot be for be bring here. and for shame upon you, oh, for shame, to accuse I of be a bad mother to my son, with my son stand here to tell you so different!'
the bystander, on and off the dining-room chair, raise a murmur of sympathy with Mrs. Pegler, and Mr. Gradgrind feel himself innocently place in a very distressing predicament, when Mr. Bounderby, who have never cease walk up and down, and have every moment swell large and large, and grow redder and redder, stop short.

although Mr. Bounderby carry it off in these term, hold the door open for the company to depart, there be a bluster sheepishness upon he, at once extremely crestfallen and superlatively absurd. detect as the Bully of humility, who have build his windy reputation upon lie, and in his boastfulness have put the honest truth as far away from he as if he have advance the mean claim (there be no meaner) to tack himself on to a pedigree, he cut a most ridiculous figure. with the people file off at the door he hold, who he know would carry what have pass to the whole town, to be give to the four wind, he could not have look a Bully more shorn and forlorn, if he have have his ear crop. even that unlucky female, Mrs. Sparsit, fall from her pinnacle of exultation into the Slough of Despond, be not in so bad a plight as that remarkable man and self-make Humbug, Josiah Bounderby of Coketown.

Mr. Gradgrind join they before they have go very far, and speak with much interest of Stephen Blackpool; for whom he think this signal failure of the suspicion against Mrs. Pegler be likely to work well.
as to the whelp; throughout this scene as on all other late occasion, he have stick close to Bounderby. he seem to feel that as long as Bounderby could make no discovery without his knowledge, he be so far safe. he never visit his sister, and have only see she once since she go home: that be to say on the night when he still stick close to Bounderby, as already relate.

CHAPTER VI - THE STARLIGHT
the Sunday be a bright Sunday in autumn, clear and cool, when early in the morning Sissy and Rachael meet, to walk in the country.
as Coketown cast ash not only on its own head but on the neighbourhood's too - after the manner of those pious person who do penance for their own sin by put other people into sackcloth - it be customary for those who now and then thirst for a draught of pure air, which be not absolutely the most wicked among the vanity of life, to get a few mile away by the railroad, and then begin their walk, or their lounge in the field. Sissy and Rachael help themselves out of the smoke by the usual mean, and be put down at a station about midway between the town and Mr. Bounderby's retreat.
though the green landscape be blot here and there with heap of coal, it be green elsewhere, and there be tree to see, and there be lark singing (though it be Sunday), and there be pleasant scent in the air, and all be over-arch by a bright blue sky. in the distance one way, Coketown show as a black mist; in another distance hill begin to rise; in a third, there be a faint change in the light of the horizon where it shine upon the far-off sea. under their foot, the grass be fresh; beautiful shadow of branch flicker upon it, and speckle it; hedgerow be luxuriant; everything be at peace. engine at pit' mouth, and lean old horse that have wear the circle of their daily labour into the ground, be alike quiet; wheel have cease for a short space to turn; and the great wheel of earth seem to revolve without the shock and noise of another time.
they walk on across the field and down the shady lane, sometimes get over a fragment of a fence so rotten that it drop at a touch of the foot, sometimes pass near a wreck of brick and beam overgrow with grass, mark the site of deserted work. they follow path and track, however slight. mound where the grass be rank and high, and where bramble, dock-weed, and such-like vegetation, be confusedly heap together, they always avoid; for dismal story be tell in that country of the old pit hide beneath such indication.
the sun be high when they sit down to rest. they have see no one, near or distant, for a long time; and the solitude remain unbroken. 'it be so still here, Rachael, and the way be so untrodden, that I think we must be the first who have be here all the summer.'
as Sissy say it, her eye be attract by another of those rotten fragment of fence upon the ground. she get up to look at it. 'and yet I donot know. this have not be break very long. the wood be quite fresh where it give way. here be footstep too. - o Rachael!'
she run back, and catch she round the neck. Rachael have already start up.
'what be the matter?'
'I donot know. there be a hat lie in the grass.' they go forward together. Rachael take it up, shake from head to foot. she break into a passion of tear and lamentation: Stephen Blackpool be write in his own hand on the inside.
'o the poor lad, the poor lad! he have be make away with. he be lie murder here!'
'be there - have the hat any blood upon it?' Sissy falter.
they be afraid to look; but they do examine it, and find no mark of violence, inside or out. it have be lie there some day, for rain and dew have stain it, and the mark of its shape be on the grass where it have fall. they look fearfully about they, without move, but could see nothing more. 'Rachael,' Sissy whisper, 'I will go on a little by myself.'
she have unclasp her hand, and be in the act of step forward, when Rachael catch she in both arm with a scream that resound over the wide landscape. before they, at their very foot, be the brink of a black ragged chasm hide by the thick grass. they spring back, and fall upon their knee, each hide her face upon the other's neck.
'o, my good Lord! hebe down there! down there!' at first this, and her terrific scream, be all that could be get from Rachael, by any tear, by any prayer, by any representation, by any mean. it be impossible to hush she; and it be deadly necessary to hold she, or she would have fling herself down the shaft.
'Rachael, dear Rachael, good Rachael, for the love of Heaven, not these dreadful cry! think of Stephen, think of Stephen, think of Stephen!'
by an earnest repetition of this entreaty, pour out in all the agony of such a moment, Sissy at last bring she to be silent, and to look at she with a tearless face of stone.
'Rachael, Stephen may be live. you wouldnot leave he lie maim at the bottom of this dreadful place, a moment, if you could bring help to he?'
'no, no, no!'
'donot stir from here, for his sake! let I go and listen.'
she shudder to approach the pit; but she creep towards it on her hand and knee, and call to he as loud as she could call. she listen, but no sound reply. she call again and listen; still no answer sound. she do this, twenty, thirty time. she take a little clod of earth from the broken ground where he have stumble, and throw it in. she could not hear it fall.
the wide prospect, so beautiful in its stillness but a few minute ago, almost carry despair to her brave heart, as she rise and look all round she, see no help. 'Rachael, we must lose not a moment. we must go in different direction, seek aid. you shall go by the way we have come, and I will go forward by the path. tell any one you see, and every one what have happen. think of Stephen, think of Stephen!'
she know by Rachael's face that she might trust she now. and after stand for a moment to see she run, wring her hand as she run, she turn and go upon her own search; she stop at the hedge to tie her shawl there as a guide to the place, then throw her bonnet aside, and run as she have never run before.
Run, Sissy, run, in Heaven's name! donot stop for breath. run, run! quicken herself by carry such entreaty in her thought, she run from field to field, and lane to lane, and place to place, as she have never run before; until she come to a shed by an engine-house, where two man lie in the shade, asleep on straw.
first to wake they, and next to tell they, all so wild and breathless as she be, what have bring she there, be difficulty; but they no soon understand she than their spirit be on fire like her. one of the man be in a drunken slumber, but on his comrade's shout to he that a man have fall down the Old Hell Shaft, he start out to a pool of dirty water, put his head in it, and come back sober.
with these two man she run to another half-a-mile far, and with that one to another, while they run elsewhere. then a horse be find; and she get another man to ride for life or death to the railroad, and send a message to Louisa, which she write and give he. by this time a whole village be up: and windlass, rope, pole, candle, lantern, all thing necessary, be fast collect and be bring into one place, to be carry to the Old Hell Shaft.
it seem now hour and hour since she have leave the lost man lie in the grave where he have be bury alive. she could not bear to remain away from it any long - it be like desert he - and she hurry swiftly back, accompany by half-a-dozen labourer, include the drunken man whom the news have sober, and who be the good man of all. when they come to the Old Hell Shaft, they find it as lonely as she have leave it. the man call and listen as she have do, and examine the edge of the chasm, and settle how it have happen, and then sit down to wait until the implement they want should come up.
every sound of insect in the air, every stirring of the leaf, every whisper among these man, make sissy tremble, for she think it be a cry at the bottom of the pit. but the wind blow idly over it, and no sound arise to the surface, and they sit upon the grass, wait and wait. after they have wait some time, straggle people who have hear of the accident begin to come up; then the real help of implement begin to arrive. in the midst of this, Rachael return; and with her party there be a surgeon, who bring some wine and medicine. but, the expectation among the people that the man would be find alive be very slight indeed.
there be now people enough present to impede the work, the sobered man put himself at the head of the rest, or be put there by the general consent, and make a large ring round the Old Hell Shaft, and appoint man to keep it. besides such volunteer as be accept to work, only Sissy and Rachael be at first permit within this ring; but, later in the day, when the message bring an express from Coketown, Mr. Gradgrind and Louisa, and Mr. Bounderby, and the whelp, be also there.
the sun be four hour low than when Sissy and Rachael have first sit down upon the grass, before a means of enable two man to descend securely be rig with pole and rope. difficulty have arise in the construction of this machine, simple as it be; requisite have be find want, and message have have to go and return. it be five o'clock in the afternoon of the bright autumnal Sunday, before a candle be send down to try the air, while three or four rough face stand crowd close together, attentively watch it: the man at the windlass lower as they be tell. the candle be bring up again, feebly burn, and then some water be cast in. then the bucket be hook on; and the sobered man and another get in with light, give the word 'lower away!'
as the rope go out, tight and strained, and the windlass creak, there be not a breath among the one or two hundred man and woman look on, that come as it be willnot to come. the signal be give and the windlass stop, with abundant rope to spare. apparently so long an interval ensue with the man at the windlass stand idle, that some woman shriek that another accident have happen! but the surgeon who hold the watch, declare five minute not to have elapse yet, and sternly admonish they to keep silence. he have not well do speak, when the windlass be reverse and work again. practised eye know that it do not go as heavily as it would if both workman have be come up, and that only one be return.
the rope come in tight and strained; and ring after ring be coil upon the barrel of the windlass, and all eye be fasten on the pit. the sobered man be bring up and leap out briskly on the grass. there be an universal cry of 'alive or dead?' and then a deep, profound hush.
when he say 'alive!' a great shout arise and many eye have tear in they.
'but hebe hurt very bad,' he add, as soon as he could make himself hear again. 'where's doctor? hebe hurt so very bad, sir, that we donno how to get he up.'
they all consult together, and look anxiously at the surgeon, as he ask some question, and shake his head on receive the reply. the sun be set now; and the red light in the evening sky touch every face there, and cause it to be distinctly see in all its rapt suspense.
the consultation end in the man return to the windlass, and the pitman go down again, carry the wine and some other small matter with he. then the other man come up. in the meantime, under the surgeon's direction, some man bring a hurdle, on which other make a thick bed of spare clothe cover with loose straw, while he himself contrive some bandage and sling from shawl and handkerchief. as these be make, they be hang upon an arm of the pitman who have last come up, with instruction how to use they: and as he stand, show by the light he carry, lean his powerful loose hand upon one of the pole, and sometimes glance down the pit, and sometimes glance round upon the people, he be not the least conspicuous figure in the scene. it be dark now, and torch be kindle.
it appear from the little this man say to those about he, which be quickly repeat all over the circle, that the lost man have fall upon a mass of crumble rubbish with which the pit be half choke up, and that his fall have be far break by some jagged earth at the side. he lie upon his back with one arm double under he, and accord to his own belief have hardly stir since he fall, except that he have move his free hand to a side pocket, in which he remember to have some bread and meat (of which he have swallow crumb), and have likewise scoop up a little water in it now and then. he have come straight away from his work, on be write to, and have walk the whole journey; and be on his way to Mr. Bounderby's country house after dark, when he fall. he be cross that dangerous country at such a dangerous time, because he be innocent of what be lay to his charge, and couldnot rest from come the near way to deliver himself up. the Old Hell Shaft, the pitman say, with a curse upon it, be worthy of its bad name to the last; for though Stephen could speak now, he believe it would soon be find to have mangle the life out of he.
when all be ready, this man, still take his last hurried charge from his comrade and the surgeon after the windlass have begin to lower he, disappear into the pit. the rope go out as before, the signal be make as before, and the windlass stop. no man remove his hand from it now. every one wait with his grasp set, and his body bend down to the work, ready to reverse and wind in. at length the signal be give, and all the ring lean forward.
for, now, the rope come in, tighten and strain to its utmost as it appear, and the man turn heavily, and the windlass complain. it be scarcely endurable to look at the rope, and think of its give way. but, ring after ring be coil upon the barrel of the windlass safely, and the connect chain appear, and finally the bucket with the two man hold on at the side - a sight to make the head swim, and oppress the heart - and tenderly support between they, sling and tie within, the figure of a poor, crush, human creature.
a low murmur of pity go round the throng, and the woman weep aloud, as this form, almost without form, be move very slowly from its iron deliverance, and lay upon the bed of straw. at first, none but the surgeon go close to it. he do what he could in its adjustment on the couch, but the good that he could do be to cover it. that gently do, he call to he Rachael and Sissy. and at that time the pale, worn, patient face be see look up at the sky, with the broken right hand lie bare on the outside of the cover garment, as if wait to be take by another hand.
they give he drink, moisten his face with water, and administer some drop of cordial and wine. though he lie quite motionless look up at the sky, he smile and say, 'Rachael.' she stoop down on the grass at his side, and bend over he until her eye be between his and the sky, for he could not so much as turn they to look at she.
'Rachael, my dear.'
she take his hand. he smile again and say, 'donot let 't go.'
'Thou'rt in great pain, my own dear Stephen?'
'I ha' be, but not now. I ha' be - dreadful, and dree, and long, my dear - but 'tis ower now. ah, Rachael, aw a muddle! Fro' first to last, a muddle!'
the spectre of his old look seem to pass as he say the word.
'I ha' fall into th' pit, my dear, as have cost wi'in the knowledge o' old fok now livin, hundred and hundred o' man's life - father, son, brother, dear to thousand an' thousand, an' keep them fro' want and hunger. I ha' fall into a pit that ha' be wi' th' firedamp crueller than battle. I ha' read on 't in the public petition, as onny one may read, fro' the man that work in pit, in which they ha' pray'n and pray'n the lawmaker for Christ's sake not to let their work be murder to them, but to spare them for th' wife and child that they love as well as gentlefok love theirs. when it be in work, it kill wi'out need; when 'tis let alone, it kill wi'out need. see how we die an' no need, one way an' another - in a muddle - every day!'
he faintly say it, without any anger against any one. merely as the truth.
'thy little sister, Rachael, thou hast not forget she. Thou'rt not like to forget she now, and I so nigh she. Thou know'st - poor, patient, suff'rin, dear - how thou didst work for she, seet'n all day long in her little chair at thy winder, and how she die, young and misshapen, awlung o' sickly air as had'n no need to be, an' awlung o' work people's miserable home. a muddle! aw a muddle!'
Louisa approach he; but he could not see she, lie with his face turn up to the night sky.
'if aw th' thing that tooche we, my dear, be not so muddled, I should'n ha' had'n need to coom heer. if we be not in a muddle among ourseln, I should'n ha' be, by my own fellow weaver and workin' brother, so mistook. if Mr. Bounderby have ever know'd I right - if hewould ever know'd I at aw - he would'n ha' took'n offence wi' I. he would'n ha' suspect'n I. but look up yonder, Rachael! look aboove!'
follow his eye, she see that he be gaze at a star.
'it ha' shin upon I,' he say reverently, 'in my pain and trouble down below. it ha' shin into my mind. I ha' look'n at 't and thowt o' thee, Rachael, till the muddle in my mind have clear awa, above a bit, I hope. if soom ha' be wantin' in unnerstan'in I well, I, too, ha' be wantin' in unnerstan'in they well. when I get thy letter, I easily believen that what the yoong ledy sen and do to I, and what her brother sen and do to I, be one, and that there be a wicked plot betwixt them. when I fall, I be in anger wi' her, an' hurryin on t' be as onjust t' she as oother be t' I. but in our judgment, like as in our doin, we mun bear and forbear. in my pain an' trouble, lookin up yonder, - wi' it shinin on I - I ha' see more clear, and ha' make it my dyin prayer that aw th' world may on'y coom toogether more, an' get a well unnerstan'in o' one another, than when I be in 't my own weak seln.'
louisa hear what he say, bend over he on the opposite side to Rachael, so that he could see she.
'you ha' hear?' he say, after a few moment' silence. 'I ha' not forget you, ledy.'
'yes, Stephen, I have hear you. and your prayer be mine.'
'you ha' a father. will yo tak' a message to he?'
'he be here,' say Louisa, with dread. 'shall I bring he to you?'
'if yo please.'
Louisa return with her father. stand hand-in-hand, they both look down upon the solemn countenance.
'Sir, yo will clear I an' mak my name good wi' aw man. this I leave to yo.'
Mr. Gradgrind be troubled and ask how?
'Sir,' be the reply: 'yor son will tell yo how. ask he. I mak no charge: I leave none ahint I: not a single word. I ha' see an' spok'n wi' yor son, one night. I ask no more o' yo than that yo clear I - an' I trust to yo to do 't.'
the bearer be now ready to carry he away, and the surgeon be anxious for his removal, those who have torch or lantern, prepare to go in front of the litter. before it be raise, and while they be arrange how to go, he say to Rachael, look upward at the star:
'often as I coom to myseln, and find it shinin' on I down there in my trouble, I thowt it be the star as guide to our Saviour's home. I awmust think it be the very star!'
they lift he up, and he be overjoyed to find that they be about to take he in the direction whither the star seem to he to lead.
'Rachael, beloved lass! donot let go my hand. we may walk toogether t'night, my dear!'
'I will hold thy hand, and keep beside thee, Stephen, all the way.'
'bless thee! will soombody be pleased to coover my face!'
they carry he very gently along the field, and down the lane, and over the wide landscape; Rachael always hold the hand in her. very few whisper break the mournful silence. it be soon a funeral procession. the star have show he where to find the God of the poor; and through humility, and sorrow, and forgiveness, he have go to his Redeemer's rest.
CHAPTER VII - WHELP-hunting
before the ring form round the Old Hell Shaft be break, one figure have disappear from within it. Mr. Bounderby and his shadow have not stand near Louisa, who hold her father's arm, but in a retired place by themselves. when Mr. Gradgrind be summon to the couch, Sissy, attentive to all that happen, slip behind that wicked shadow - a sight in the horror of his face, if there have be eye there for any sight but one - and whisper in his ear. without turn his head, he confer with she a few moment, and vanish. thus the whelp have go out of the circle before the people move.
when the father reach home, he send a message to Mr. Bounderby's, desire his son to come to he directly. the reply be, that Mr. Bounderby have miss he in the crowd, and see nothing of he since, have suppose he to be at Stone Lodge.
'I believe, father,' say Louisa, 'he will not come back to town to-night.' Mr. Gradgrind turn away, and say no more.
in the morning, he go down to the Bank himself as soon as it be open, and see his son's place empty (he have not the courage to look in at first) go back along the street to meet Mr. Bounderby on his way there. to whom he say that, for reason he would soon explain, but entreat not then to be ask for, he have find it necessary to employ his son at a distance for a little while. also, that he be charge with the duty of vindicate Stephen Blackpool's memory, and declare the thief. Mr. Bounderby quite confound, stand stock-still in the street after his father-in-law have leave he, swell like an immense soap-bubble, without its beauty.
Mr. Gradgrind go home, lock himself in his room, and keep it all that day. when Sissy and Louisa tap at his door, he say, without open it, 'not now, my dear; in the evening.' on their return in the evening, he say, 'I be not able yet - to-morrow.' he eat nothing all day, and have no candle after dark; and they hear he walk to and fro late at night.
but, in the morning he appear at breakfast at the usual hour, and take his usual place at the table. aged and bent he look, and quite bow down; and yet he look a wise man, and a well man, than in the day when in this life he want nothing - but fact. before he leave the room, he appoint a time for they to come to he; and so, with his gray head droop, go away.
'dear father,' say Louisa, when they keep their appointment, 'you have three young child leave. they will be different, I will be different yet, with Heaven's help.'
she give her hand to Sissy, as if she mean with her help too.
'your wretched brother,' say Mr. Gradgrind. 'do you think he have plan this robbery, when he go with you to the lodging?'
'I fear so, father. I know he have want money very much, and have spend a great deal.'
'the poor man be about to leave the town, it come into his evil brain to cast suspicion on he?'
'I think it must have flash upon he while he sit there, father. for I ask he to go there with I. the visit do not originate with he.'
'he have some conversation with the poor man. do he take he aside?'
'he take he out of the room. I ask he afterwards, why he have do so, and he make a plausible excuse; but since last night, father, and when I remember the circumstance by its light, I be afraid I can imagine too truly what pass between they.'
'let I know,' say her father, 'if your thought present your guilty brother in the same dark view as mine.'
'I fear, father,' hesitate Louisa, 'that he must have make some representation to Stephen Blackpool - perhaps in my name, perhaps in his own - which induce he to do in good faith and honesty, what he have never do before, and to wait about the Bank those two or three night before he leave the town.'
'too plain!' return the father. 'too plain!'
he shade his face, and remain silent for some moment. recover himself, he say:
'and now, how be he to be find? how be he to be save from justice? in the few hour that I can possibly allow to elapse before I publish the truth, how be he to be find by we, and only by we? ten thousand pound could not effect it.'
'Sissy have effect it, father.'
he raise his eye to where she stand, like a good fairy in his house, and say in a tone of soften gratitude and grateful kindness, 'it be always you, my child!'
'we have our fear,' Sissy explain, glance at Louisa, 'before yesterday; and when I see you bring to the side of the litter last night, and hear what pass (be close to Rachael all the time), I go to he when no one see, and say to he, "donot look at I. see where your father be. escape at once, for his sake and your own!" he be in a tremble before I whisper to he, and he start and tremble more then, and say, "where can I go? I have very little money, and I donot know who will hide I!" I think of father's old circus. I have not forget where Mr. Sleary go at this time of year, and I read of he in a paper only the other day. I tell he to hurry there, and tell his name, and ask Mr. Sleary to hide he till I come. "Iwill get to he before the morning," he say. and I see he shrink away among the people.'
'thank Heaven!' exclaim his father. 'he may be get abroad yet.'
it be the more hopeful as the town to which Sissy have direct he be within three hour' journey of Liverpool, whence he could be swiftly dispatch to any part of the world. but, caution be necessary in communicate with he - for there be a great danger every moment of his be suspect now, and nobody could be sure at heart but that Mr. Bounderby himself, in a bullying vein of public zeal, might play a roman part - it be consent that Sissy and Louisa should repair to the place in question, by a circuitous course, alone; and that the unhappy father, set forth in an opposite direction, should get round to the same bourne by another and wide route. it be far agree that he should not present himself to Mr. Sleary, lest his intention should be mistrust, or the intelligence of his arrival should cause his son to take flight anew; but, that the communication should be leave to Sissy and Louisa to open; and that they should inform the cause of so much misery and disgrace, of his father's be at hand and of the purpose for which they have come. when these arrangement have be well consider and be fully understand by all three, it be time to begin to carry they into execution. early in the afternoon, Mr. Gradgrind walk direct from his own house into the country, to be take up on the line by which he be to travel; and at night the remain two set forth upon their different course, encourage by not see any face they know.
the two travel all night, except when they be leave, for odd number of minute, at branch-place, up illimitable flight of step, or down well - which be the only variety of those branch - and, early in the morning, be turn out on a swamp, a mile or two from the town they seek. from this dismal spot they be rescue by a savage old postilion, who happen to be up early, kick a horse in a fly: and so be smuggle into the town by all the back lane where the pig live: which, although not a magnificent or even savoury approach, be, as be usual in such case, the legitimate highway.
the first thing they see on enter the town be the skeleton of Sleary's Circus. the company have depart for another town more than twenty mile off, and have open there last night. the connection between the two place be by a hilly turnpike-road, and the travel on that road be very slow. though they take but a hasty breakfast, and no rest (which it would have be in vain to seek under such anxious circumstance), it be noon before they begin to find the bill of Sleary's horse-riding on barn and wall, and one o'clock when they stop in the market-place.
a Grand Morning Performance by the rider, commence at that very hour, be in course of announcement by the bellman as they set their foot upon the stone of the street. Sissy recommend that, to avoid make inquiry and attract attention in the town, they should present themselves to pay at the door. if Mr. Sleary be take the money, he would be sure to know she, and would proceed with discretion. if he be not, he would be sure to see they inside; and, know what he have do with the fugitive, would proceed with discretion still.
therefore, they repair, with flutter heart, to the well- remember booth. the flag with the inscription SLEARY'S horse- riding be there; and the Gothic niche be there; but Mr. Sleary be not there. Master Kidderminster, grow too maturely turfy to be receive by the wild credulity as Cupid any more, have yield to the invincible force of circumstance (and his beard), and, in the capacity of a man who make himself generally useful, preside on this occasion over the exchequer - have also a drum in reserve, on which to expend his leisure moment and superfluous force. in the extreme sharpness of his look out for base coin, Mr. Kidderminster, as at present situate, never see anything but money; so Sissy pass he unrecognised, and they go in.
the Emperor of Japan, on a steady old white horse stencil with black spot, be twirl five wash-hand basin at once, as it be the favourite recreation of that monarch to do. Sissy, though well acquaint with his royal line, have no personal knowledge of the present Emperor, and his reign be peaceful. Miss Josephine Sleary, in her celebrate graceful Equestrian Tyrolean Flower Act, be then announce by a new clown (who humorously say Cauliflower Act), and Mr. Sleary appear, lead she in.
Mr. Sleary have only make one cut at the Clown with his long whip- lash, and the Clown have only say, 'if you do it again, Iwill throw the horse at you!' when Sissy be recognise both by father and daughter. but they get through the Act with great self-possession; and Mr. Sleary, save for the first instant, convey no more expression into his locomotive eye than into his fix one. the performance seem a little long to Sissy and Louisa, particularly when it stop to afford the Clown an opportunity of tell Mr. Sleary (who say 'indeed, sir!' to all his observation in the calm way, and with his eye on the house) about two leg sit on three leg look at one leg, when in come four leg, and lay hold of one leg, and up get two leg, catch hold of three leg, and throw them at four leg, who run away with one leg. for, although an ingenious allegory relate to a butcher, a three- legged stool, a dog, and a leg of mutton, this narrative consume time; and they be in great suspense. at last, however, little fair-haired Josephine make her curtsey amid great applause; and the Clown, leave alone in the ring, have just warm himself, and say, 'now Iwill have a turn!' when Sissy be touch on the shoulder, and beckon out.
she take Louisa with she; and they be receive by Mr. Sleary in a very little private apartment, with canvas side, a grass floor, and a wooden ceiling all aslant, on which the box company stamp their approbation, as if they be come through. 'Thethilia,' say Mr. Sleary, who have brandy and water at hand, 'it doth I good to thee you. you wath alwayth a favourite with uth, and youhave do uth credith thinth the old timeth Ibe thure. you mutht thee our people, my dear, afore we thpeak of bithnith, or theywill break their hearth - ethpethially the woman. Here'th Jothphine hath be and get married to E. W. B. Childerth, and thee hath get a boy, and though he'th only three yearth old, he thtickth on to any pony you can bring againtht he. He'th name the little wonder of Thcolathtic Equitation; and if you donot hear of that boy at Athley'th, youwill hear of he at Parith. and you recollect Kidderminthter, that wath think to be rather thweet upon yourthelf? well. He'th marry too. marry a widder. old enough to be hith mother. Thee wath Tightrope, thee wath, and now thee'th nothing - on accounth of fat. theyhave get two child, tho webe thtrong in the Fairy bithnith and the Nurthery dodge. if you wath to thee our child in the Wood, with their father and mother both a dyin' on a horthe - their uncle a retheiving of them ath hith wardth, upon a horthe - themthelvth both a go a black- berryin' on a horthe - and the Robinth a come in to cover them with leavth, upon a horthe - youwould thay it wath the completetht thing ath ever you thet your eyeth on! and you remember Emma Gordon, my dear, ath wath a'motht a mother to you? of courthe you do; I neednot athk. well! Emma, thee lotht her huthband. he wath throw'd a heavy back-fall off a Elephant in a thort of a Pagoda thing ath the Thultan of the Indieth, and he never get the well of it; and thee marry a thecond time - marry a Cheethemonger ath fall in love with she from the front - and he'th a Overtheer and makin' a fortun.'
these various change, Mr. Sleary, very short of breath now, relate with great heartiness, and with a wonderful kind of innocence, consider what a bleary and brandy-and-watery old veteran he be. afterwards he bring in Josephine, and E. W. B. Childers (rather deeply line in the jaw by daylight), and the Little wonder of Scholastic Equitation, and in a word, all the company. amazing creature they be in Louisa's eye, so white and pink of complexion, so scant of dress, and so demonstrative of leg; but it be very agreeable to see they crowd about Sissy, and very natural in Sissy to be unable to refrain from tear.
'there! now Thethilia hath kithd all the child, and hug all the woman, and thaken handth all round with all the man, clear, every one of you, and ring in the band for the thecond part!'
as soon as they be go, he continue in a low tone. 'now, Thethilia, I donot athk to know any thecreth, but I thuppothe I may conthider thith to be Mith Thquire.'
'this be his sister. yes.'
'and t'other on'th daughter. That'h what I mean. Hope I thee you well, mith. and I hope the Thquire'th well?'
'my father will be here soon,' say Louisa, anxious to bring he to the point. 'be my brother safe?'
'thafe and thound!' he reply. 'I want you jutht to take a peep at the Ring, mith, through here. Thethilia, you know the dodgeth; find a thpy-hole for yourthelf.'
they each look through a chink in the board.
'That'h Jack the Giant Killer - piethe of comic infant bithnith,' say Sleary. 'there'th a property-houthe, you thee, for Jack to hide in; there'th my Clown with a thauthepan-lid and a thpit, for Jack'th thervant; there'th little Jack himthelf in a thplendid thoot of armour; there'th two comic black thervanth twithe ath big ath the houthe, to thtand by it and to bring it in and clear it; and the Giant (a very ecthpenthive bathket one), he an't on yet. now, do you thee them all?'
'yes,' they both say.
'look at them again,' say Sleary, 'look at them well. you thee em all? very good. now, mith;' he put a form for they to sit on; 'I have my opinionth, and the Thquire your father hath hith. I donot want to know what your brother'th be up to; ith well for I not to know. all I thay ith, the Thquire hath thtood by Thethilia, and Iwill thtand by the Thquire. your brother ith one they black thervanth.'
Louisa utter an exclamation, partly of distress, partly of satisfaction.
'ith a fact,' say Sleary, 'and even knowin' it, you couldnot put your finger on he. let the Thquire come. I thall keep your brother here after the performanth. I thant undreth he, nor yet wath hith paint off. let the Thquire come here after the performanth, or come here yourthelf after the performanth, and you thall find your brother, and have the whole plathe to talk to he in. never mind the lookth of he, ath long ath he'th well hide.'
Louisa, with many thank and with a lightened load, detain Mr. Sleary no long then. she leave her love for her brother, with her eye full of tear; and she and Sissy go away until later in the afternoon.
Mr. Gradgrind arrive within an hour afterwards. he too have encounter no one whom he know; and be now sanguine with Sleary's assistance, of get his disgraced son to Liverpool in the night. as neither of the three could be his companion without almost identify he under any disguise, he prepare a letter to a correspondent whom he could trust, beseech he to ship the bearer off at any cost, to North or South America, or any distant part of the world to which he could be the most speedily and privately dispatch.
this do, they walk about, wait for the Circus to be quite vacate; not only by the audience, but by the company and by the horse. after watch it a long time, they see Mr. Sleary bring out a chair and sit down by the side-door, smoking; as if that be his signal that they might approach.
'your thervant, Thquire,' be his cautious salutation as they pass in. 'if you want I youwill find I here. you muthn't mind your thon have a comic livery on.'
they all three go in; and Mr. Gradgrind sit down forlorn, on the Clown's perform chair in the middle of the ring. on one of the back bench, remote in the subdue light and the strangeness of the place, sit the villainous whelp, sulky to the last, whom he have the misery to call his son.
in a preposterous coat, like a beadle's, with cuff and flap exaggerate to an unspeakable extent; in an immense waistcoat, knee-breech, buckled shoe, and a mad cocked hat; with nothing fit he, and everything of coarse material, moth-eat and full of hole; with seam in his black face, where fear and heat have start through the greasy composition daub all over it; anything so grimly, detestably, ridiculously shameful as the whelp in his comic livery, Mr. Gradgrind never could by any other mean have believe in, weighable and measurable fact though it be. and one of his model child have come to this!
at first the whelp would not draw any nearer, but persist in remain up there by himself. yield at length, if any concession so sullenly make can be call yield, to the entreaty of Sissy - for Louisa he disown altogether - he come down, bench by bench, until he stand in the sawdust, on the verge of the circle, as far as possible, within its limit from where his father sit.
'how be this do?' ask the father.
'how be what do?' moodily answer the son.
'this robbery,' say the father, raise his voice upon the word.
'I force the safe myself over night, and shut it up ajar before I go away. I have have the key that be find, make long before. I drop it that morning, that it might be suppose to have be use. I donot take the money all at once. I pretend to put my balance away every night, but I donot. now you know all about it.'
'if a thunderbolt have fall on I,' say the father, 'it would have shock I less than this!'
'I donot see why,' grumble the son. 'so many people be employ in situation of trust; so many people, out of so many, will be dishonest. I have hear you talk, a hundred time, of its be a law. how can I help law? you have comfort other with such thing, father. Comfort yourself!'
the father bury his face in his hand, and the son stand in his disgraceful grotesqueness, bite straw: his hand, with the black partly wear away inside, look like the hand of a monkey. the evening be fast closing in; and from time to time, he turn the white of his eye restlessly and impatiently towards his father. they be the only part of his face that show any life or expression, the pigment upon it be so thick.
'you must be get to Liverpool, and send abroad.'
'I suppose I must. I cannot be more miserable anywhere,' whimper the whelp, 'than I have be here, ever since I can remember. thatbe one thing.'
Mr. Gradgrind go to the door, and return with Sleary, to whom he submit the question, how to get this deplorable object away?
'why, Ihave be think of it, Thquire. There'th not muth time to lothe, tho you muth thay yeth or no. ith over twenty mileth to the rail. There'th a coath in half an hour, that goeth to the rail, 'purpothe to cath the mail train. that train will take he right to Liverpool.'
'but look at he,' groan Mr. Gradgrind. 'will any coach - '
'I donot mean that he thould go in the comic livery,' say Sleary. 'Thay the word, and Iwill make a Jothkin of he, out of the wardrobe, in five minute.'
'I donot understand,' say Mr. Gradgrind.
'a Jothkin - a carter. make up your mind quick, Thquire. therewill be beer to feth. Ihave never meet with nothing but beer ath'll ever clean a comic blackamoor.'
Mr. Gradgrind rapidly assent; Mr. Sleary rapidly turn out from a box, a smock frock, a feel hat, and other essential; the whelp rapidly change clothe behind a screen of baize; Mr. Sleary rapidly bring beer, and wash he white again.
'now,' say Sleary, 'come along to the coath, and jump up behind; Iwill go with you there, and theywill thuppothe you one of my people. Thay farewell to your family, and tharp'th the word.' with which he delicately retire.
'here be your letter,' say Mr. Gradgrind. 'all necessary mean will be provide for you. atone, by repentance and well conduct, for the shocking action you have commit, and the dreadful consequence to which it have lead. give I your hand, my poor boy, and may God forgive you as I do!'
the culprit be move to a few abject tear by these word and their pathetic tone. but, when Louisa open her arm, he repulse her afresh.
'not you. I donot want to have anything to say to you!'
'o Tom, Tom, do we end so, after all my love!'
'after all your love!' he return, obdurately. 'pretty love! leave old Bounderby to himself, and pack my good friend Mr. Harthouse off, and go home just when I be in the great danger. pretty love that! come out with every word about our have go to that place, when you see the net be gather round I. pretty love that! you have regularly give I up. you never care for I.'
'tharp'th the word!' say Sleary, at the door.
they all confusedly go out: Louisa cry to he that she forgive he, and love he still, and that he would one day be sorry to have leave she so, and glad to think of these her last word, far away: when some one run against they. Mr. Gradgrind and Sissy, who be both before he while his sister yet clung to his shoulder, stop and recoil.
for, there be Bitzer, out of breath, his thin lip part, his thin nostril distend, his white eyelash quiver, his colourless face more colourless than ever, as if he run himself into a white heat, when other people run themselves into a glow. there he stand, pant and heaving, as if he have never stop since the night, now long ago, when he have run they down before.
'Ibe sorry to interfere with your plan,' say Bitzer, shake his head, 'but I cannot allow myself to be do by horse-rider. I must have young Mr. Tom; he mustnot be get away by horse-rider; here he be in a smock frock, and I must have he!'
by the collar, too, it seem. for, so he take possession of he.
CHAPTER VIII - PHILOSOPHICAL
they go back into the booth, Sleary shut the door to keep intruder out. Bitzer, still hold the paralysed culprit by the collar, stand in the Ring, blink at his old patron through the darkness of the twilight.
'Bitzer,' say Mr. Gradgrind, break down, and miserably submissive to he, 'have you a heart?'
'the circulation, sir,' return Bitzer, smile at the oddity of the question, 'couldnot be carry on without one. no man, sir, acquaint with the fact establish by Harvey relate to the circulation of the blood, can doubt that I have a heart.'
'be it accessible,' cry Mr. Gradgrind, 'to any compassionate influence?'
'it be accessible to Reason, sir,' return the excellent young man. 'and to nothing else.'
they stand look at each other; Mr. Gradgrind's face as white as the pursuer's.
'what motive - even what motive in reason - can you have for prevent the escape of this wretched youth,' say Mr. Gradgrind, 'and crush his miserable father? see his sister here. pity we!'
'Sir,' return Bitzer, in a very business-like and logical manner, 'since you ask I what motive I have in reason, for take young Mr. Tom back to Coketown, it be only reasonable to let you know. I have suspect young Mr. Tom of this bank-robbery from the first. I have have my eye upon he before that time, for I know his way. I have keep my observation to myself, but I have make they; and I have get ample proof against he now, besides his run away, and besides his own confession, which I be just in time to overhear. I have the pleasure of watch your house yesterday morning, and follow you here. I be go to take young Mr. Tom back to Coketown, in order to deliver he over to Mr. Bounderby. Sir, I have no doubt whatever that Mr. Bounderby will then promote I to young Mr. Tom's situation. and I wish to have his situation, sir, for it will be a rise to I, and will do I good.'
'if this be solely a question of self-interest with you - ' Mr. Gradgrind begin.
'I beg your pardon for interrupt you, sir,' return Bitzer; 'but I be sure you know that the whole social system be a question of self-interest. what you must always appeal to, be a person's self-interest. itbe your only hold. we be so constituted. I be bring up in that catechism when I be very young, sir, as you be aware.'
'what sum of money,' say Mr. Gradgrind, 'will you set against your expect promotion?'
'thank you, sir,' return Bitzer, 'for hint at the proposal; but I will not set any sum against it. know that your clear head would propose that alternative, I have go over the calculation in my mind; and I find that to compound a felony, even on very high term indeed, would not be as safe and good for I as my improved prospect in the Bank.'
'Bitzer,' say Mr. Gradgrind, stretch out his hand as though he would have say, see how miserable I be! 'Bitzer, I have but one chance leave to soften you. you be many year at my school. if, in remembrance of the pain bestow upon you there, you can persuade yourself in any degree to disregard your present interest and release my son, I entreat and pray you to give he the benefit of that remembrance.'
'I really wonder, sir,' rejoin the old pupil in an argumentative manner, 'to find you take a position so untenable. my schooling be pay for; it be a bargain; and when I come away, the bargain end.'
it be a fundamental principle of the Gradgrind philosophy that everything be to be pay for. nobody be ever on any account to give anybody anything, or render anybody help without purchase. Gratitude be to be abolish, and the virtue spring from it be not to be. every inch of the existence of mankind, from birth to death, be to be a bargain across a counter. and if we donot get to Heaven that way, it be not a politico-economical place, and we have no business there.
'I donot deny,' add Bitzer, 'that my schooling be cheap. but that come right, sir. I be make in the cheap market, and have to dispose of myself in the dearest.'
he be a little troubled here, by Louisa and Sissy cry.
'pray donot do that,' say he, 'itbe of no use do that: it only worry. you seem to think that I have some animosity against young Mr. Tom; whereas I have none at all. I be only go, on the reasonable ground I have mention, to take he back to Coketown. if he be to resist, I should set up the cry of stop thief! but, he willnot resist, you may depend upon it.'
Mr. Sleary, who with his mouth open and his rolling eye as immovably jammed in his head as his fix one, have listen to these doctrine with profound attention, here step forward.
'Thquire, you know perfectly well, and your daughter knowth perfectly well (well than you, becauthe I the it to she), that I donot know what your thon have do, and that I donot want to know - I the it wath well not, though I only think, then, it wath thome thkylarke. however, thith young man have make it know to be a robbery of a bank, why, that'h a theriouth thing; muth too theriouth a thing for I to compound, ath thith young man hath very properly call it. conthequently, Thquire, you muthn't quarrel with I if I take thith young man'th thide, and thay he'th right and there'th no help for it. but I tell you what Iwill do, Thquire; Iwill drive your thon and thith young man over to the rail, and prevent expothure here. I cannot conthent to do more, but Iwill do that.'
fresh lamentation from Louisa, and deep affliction on Mr. Gradgrind's part, follow this desertion of they by their last friend. but, Sissy glance at he with great attention; nor do she in her own breast misunderstand he. as they be all go out again, he favour she with one slight roll of his movable eye, desire she to linger behind. as he lock the door, he say excitedly:
'the Thquire thtood by you, Thethilia, and Iwill thtand by the Thquire. More than that: thith ith a prethiouth rathcal, and belongth to that bluthtere Cove that my people nearly pitht out o' winder. itwill be a dark night; Ihave get a horthe thatwill do anything but thpeak; Ihave get a pony thatwill go fifteen mile an hour with Childerth drive of he; Ihave get a dog thatwill keep a man to one plathe four-and-twenty hourth. get a word with the young Thquire. tell he, when he theeth our horthe begin to danthe, not to be afraid of be thpilt, but to look out for a pony-gig come up. tell he, when he theeth that gig clothe by, to jump down, and itwill take he off at a rattle pathe. if my dog leth thith young man thtir a peg on foot, I give he leave to go. and if my horthe ever thtirth from that thpot where he beginth a danthing, till the morning - I donot know he? - tharp'th the word!'
the word be so sharp, that in ten minute Mr. Childers, saunter about the market-place in a pair of slipper, have his cue, and Mr. Sleary's equipage be ready. it be a fine sight, to behold the learn dog bark round it, and Mr. Sleary instruct he, with his one practicable eye, that Bitzer be the object of his particular attention. soon after dark they all three get in and start; the learn dog (a formidable creature) already pin Bitzer with his eye, and stick close to the wheel on his side, that he might be ready for he in the event of his show the slight disposition to alight.
the other three sit up at the inn all night in great suspense. at eight o'clock in the morning Mr. Sleary and the dog reappear: both in high spirit.
'all right, Thquire!' say Mr. Sleary, 'your thon may be aboard-a- thip by thith time. Childerth take he off, an hour and a half after we leave there latht night. the horthe danthe the polka till he wath dead beat (he would have walthe if he havenot be in harneth), and then I give he the word and he go to thleep comfortable. when that prethiouth young Rathcal the hewould go for'ard afoot, the dog hang on to hith neck-hankercher with all four legth in the air and pull he down and roll he over. Tho he come back into the drag, and there he that, 'till I turn the horthe'th head, at half-patht thixth thith morning.'
Mr. Gradgrind overwhelm he with thank, of course; and hint as delicately as he could, at a handsome remuneration in money.
'I donot want money mythelf, Thquire; but Childerth ith a family man, and if you wath to like to offer he a five-pound note, it mightnot be unactheptable. likewithe if you wath to thtand a collar for the dog, or a thet of bellth for the horthe, I thould be very glad to take them. Brandy and water I alwayth take.' he have already call for a glass, and now call for another. 'if you wouldnot think it go too far, Thquire, to make a little thpread for the company at about three and thixth ahead, not reckon Luth, it would make them happy.'
all these little token of his gratitude, Mr. Gradgrind very willingly undertake to render. though he think they far too slight, he say, for such a service.
'very well, Thquire; then, if youwill only give a Horthe-riding, a bethpeak, whenever you can, youwill more than balanthe the account. now, Thquire, if your daughter will ethcuthe I, I thould like one parting word with you.'
Louisa and Sissy withdraw into an adjoining room; Mr. Sleary, stir and drink his brandy and water as he stand, go on:
'Thquire, - you donot need to be tell that dogth ith wonderful animalth.'
'their instinct,' say Mr. Gradgrind, 'be surprising.'
'whatever you call it - and Ibe bletht if I know what to call it' - say Sleary, 'it ith athtonithing. the way in whith a dog'll find you - the dithtanthe hewill come!'
'his scent,' say Mr. Gradgrind, 'be so fine.'
'Ibe bletht if I know what to call it,' repeat Sleary, shake his head, 'but I have have dogth find I, Thquire, in a way that make I think whether that dog havenot go to another dog, and the, "you donot happen to know a perthon of the name of Thleary, do you? Perthon of the name of Thleary, in the Horthe-Riding way - thtout man - game eye?" and whether that dog mightnot have the, "well, I cannot thay I know he mythelf, but I know a dog that I think would be likely to be acquaint with he." and whether that dog mightnot have think it over, and the, "Thleary, Thleary! o yeth, to be thure! a friend of mine menthione he to I at one time. I can get you hith addreth directly." in conthequenth of my be afore the public, and go about tho muth, you thee, there mutht be a number of dogth acquaint with I, Thquire, that I donot know!'
Mr. Gradgrind seem to be quite confound by this speculation.
'any way,' say Sleary, after put his lip to his brandy and water, 'ith fourteen month ago, Thquire, thinthe we wath at Chethter. we wath get up our child in the Wood one morning, when there cometh into our Ring, by the thtage door, a dog. he have travel a long way, he wath in a very bad condithon, he wath lame, and pretty well blind. he go round to our child, one after another, as if he wath a theeking for a child he know'd; and then he come to I, and throwd hithelf up behind, and thtood on hith two forelegth, weak ath he wath, and then he wag hith tail and die. Thquire, that dog wath Merrylegth.'
'Sissy's father's dog!'
'thethilia'th father'th old dog. now, Thquire, I can take my oath, from my knowledge of that dog, that that man wath dead - and bury - afore that dog come back to I. Joth'phine and Childerth and I talk it over a long time, whether I thould write or not. but we agree, "no. There'th nothing comfortable to tell; why unthettle her mind, and make she unhappy?" Tho, whether her father bathely detherte she; or whether he break hith own heart alone, rather than pull she down along with he; never will be know, now, Thquire, till - no, not till we know how the dogth findth uth out!'
'she keep the bottle that he send she for, to this hour; and she will believe in his affection to the last moment of her life,' say Mr. Gradgrind.
'it theemth to prethent two thingth to a perthon, donot it, Thquire?' say Mr. Sleary, muse as he look down into the depth of his brandy and water: 'one, that there ith a love in the world, not all Thelf-interetht after all, but thomethe very different; t'other, that it bath a way of ith own of calculate or not calculate, whith thomehow or another ith at leatht ath hard to give a name to, ath the wayth of the dogth ith!'
Mr. Gradgrind look out of window, and make no reply. Mr. Sleary empty his glass and recall the lady.
'Thethilia my dear, kith I and good-bye! Mith Thquire, to thee you treat of she like a thithter, and a thithter that you trutht and honour with all your heart and more, ith a very pretty thight to I. I hope your brother may live to be well detherving of you, and a great comfort to you. Thquire, thake handth, firtht and latht! donot be croth with uth poor vagabondth. People mutht be amuthe. they cannot be alwayth a learning, nor yet they cannot be alwayth a working, they an't make for it. you mutht have uth, Thquire. do the withe thing and the kind thing too, and make the betht of uth; not the wurtht!'
'and I never think before,' say Mr. Sleary, put his head in at the door again to say it, 'that I wath tho muth of a Cackler!'
CHAPTER IX - FINAL
it be a dangerous thing to see anything in the sphere of a vain blusterer, before the vain blusterer see it himself. Mr. Bounderby feel that Mrs. Sparsit have audaciously anticipate he, and presume to be wise than he. inappeasably indignant with she for her triumphant discovery of Mrs. Pegler, he turn this presumption, on the part of a woman in her dependent position, over and over in his mind, until it accumulate with turn like a great snowball. at last he make the discovery that to discharge this highly connect female - to have it in his power to say, 'she be a woman of family, and want to stick to I, but I wouldnot have it, and get rid of she' - would be to get the utmost possible amount of crown glory out of the connection, and at the same time to punish Mrs. Sparsit accord to her desert.
fill full than ever, with this great idea, Mr. Bounderby come in to lunch, and sit himself down in the dining-room of former day, where his portrait be. Mrs. Sparsit sit by the fire, with her foot in her cotton stirrup, little thinking whither she be post.
since the Pegler affair, this gentlewoman have cover her pity for Mr. Bounderby with a veil of quiet melancholy and contrition. in virtue thereof, it have become her habit to assume a woful look, which woful look she now bestow upon her patron.
'whatbe the matter now, ma'am?' say Mr. Bounderby, in a very short, rough way.
'pray, sir,' return Mrs. Sparsit, 'do not bite my nose off.'
'bite your nose off, ma'am?' repeat Mr. Bounderby. 'your nose!' meaning, as Mrs. Sparsit conceive, that it be too develop a nose for the purpose. after which offensive implication, he cut himself a crust of bread, and throw the knife down with a noise.
Mrs. Sparsit take her foot out of her stirrup, and say, 'Mr. Bounderby, sir!'
'well, ma'am?' retort Mr. Bounderby. 'what be you star at?'
'may I ask, sir,' say Mrs. Sparsit, 'have you be ruffle this morning?'
'yes, ma'am.'
'may I inquire, sir,' pursue the injure woman, 'whether I be the unfortunate cause of your have lose your temper?'
'now, Iwill tell you what, ma'am,' say Bounderby, 'I be not come here to be bully. a female may be highly connected, but she cannot be permit to bother and badger a man in my position, and I be not go to put up with it.' (Mr. Bounderby feel it necessary to get on: foresee that if he allow of detail, he would be beat.)
Mrs. Sparsit first elevate, then knit, her coriolanian eyebrow; gather up her work into its proper basket; and rise.
'Sir,' say she, majestically. 'it be apparent to I that I be in your way at present. I will retire to my own apartment.'
'allow I to open the door, ma'am.'
'thank you, sir; I can do it for myself.'
'you have well allow I, ma'am,' say Bounderby, pass she, and get his hand upon the lock; 'because I can take the opportunity of say a word to you, before you go. Mrs. Sparsit, ma'am, I rather think you be cramp here, do you know? it appear to I, that, under my humble roof, therebe hardly open enough for a lady of your genius in other people's affair.'
Mrs. Sparsit give he a look of the darkest scorn, and say with great politeness, 'really, sir?'
'I have be think it over, you see, since the late affair have happen, ma'am,' say Bounderby; 'and it appear to my poor judgment - '
'oh! pray, sir,' Mrs. Sparsit interpose, with sprightly cheerfulness, 'donot disparage your judgment. everybody know how unerring Mr. Bounderby's judgment be. everybody have have proof of it. it must be the theme of general conversation. disparage anything in yourself but your judgment, sir,' say Mrs. Sparsit, laugh.
Mr. Bounderby, very red and uncomfortable, resume:
'it appear to I, ma'am, I say, that a different sort of establishment altogether would bring out a lady of your power. such an establishment as your relation, Lady Scadgers's, now. donot you think you might find some affair there, ma'am, to interfere with?'
'it never occur to I before, sir,' return Mrs. Sparsit; 'but now you mention it, should think it highly probable.'
'then suppose you try, ma'am,' say Bounderby, lay an envelope with a cheque in it in her little basket. 'you can take your own time for going, ma'am; but perhaps in the meanwhile, it will be more agreeable to a lady of your power of mind, to eat her meal by herself, and not to be intrude upon. I really ought to apologise to you - be only Josiah Bounderby of Coketown - for have stand in your light so long.'
'pray donot name it, sir,' return Mrs. Sparsit. 'if that portrait could speak, sir - but it have the advantage over the original of not possess the power of commit itself and disgusting other, - it would testify, that a long period have elapse since I first habitually address it as the picture of a Noodle. nothing that a Noodle do, can awaken surprise or indignation; the proceeding of a Noodle can only inspire contempt.'
thus say, Mrs. Sparsit, with her roman feature like a medal strike to commemorate her scorn of Mr. Bounderby, survey he fixedly from head to foot, sweep disdainfully past he, and ascend the staircase. Mr. Bounderby close the door, and stand before the fire; project himself after his old explosive manner into his portrait - and into futurity.
into how much of futurity? he see Mrs. Sparsit fight out a daily fight at the point of all the weapon in the female armoury, with the grudging, smarting, peevish, torment Lady Scadgers, still lay up in bed with her mysterious leg, and gobble her insufficient income down by about the middle of every quarter, in a mean little airless lodge, a mere closet for one, a mere crib for two; but do he see more? do he catch any glimpse of himself make a show of Bitzer to stranger, as the rise young man, so devoted to his master's great merit, who have win young Tom's place, and have almost capture young Tom himself, in the time when by various rascal he be spirit away? do he see any faint reflection of his own image make a vain-glorious will, whereby five-and-twenty humbug, past five-and-fifty year of age, each take upon himself the name, Josiah Bounderby of Coketown, should for ever dine in Bounderby Hall, for ever lodge in Bounderby building, for ever attend a Bounderby chapel, for ever go to sleep under a Bounderby chaplain, for ever be support out of a Bounderby estate, and for ever nauseate all healthy stomach, with a vast amount of Bounderby balderdash and bluster? have he any prescience of the day, five year to come, when Josiah Bounderby of Coketown be to die of a fit in the Coketown street, and this same precious will be to begin its long career of quibble, plunder, false pretence, vile example, little service and much law? probably not. yet the portrait be to see it all out.
here be Mr. Gradgrind on the same day, and in the same hour, sit thoughtful in his own room. how much of futurity do he see? do he see himself, a white-haired decrepit man, bend his hitherto inflexible theory to appoint circumstance; make his fact and figure subservient to Faith, Hope, and Charity; and no long try to grind that heavenly trio in his dusty little mill? do he catch sight of himself, therefore much despise by his late political associate? do he see they, in the era of its being quite settle that the national dustman have only to do with one another, and owe no duty to an abstraction call a People, 'taunt the honourable gentleman' with this and with that and with what not, five night a-week, until the small hour of the morning? probably he have that much foreknowledge, know his man.
here be Louisa on the night of the same day, watch the fire as in day of yore, though with a gentler and a humbler face. how much of the future might arise before her vision? broadside in the street, sign with her father's name, exonerate the late Stephen Blackpool, weaver, from misplaced suspicion, and publish the guilt of his own son, with such extenuation as his year and temptation (he could not bring himself to add, his education) might beseech; be of the Present. so, Stephen Blackpool's tombstone, with her father's record of his death, be almost of the Present, for she know it be to be. these thing she could plainly see. but, how much of the Future?
a work woman, christen Rachael, after a long illness once again appear at the ringing of the Factory bell, and pass to and fro at the set hour, among the Coketown Hands; a woman of pensive beauty, always dress in black, but sweet-temper and serene, and even cheerful; who, of all the people in the place, alone appear to have compassion on a degrade, drunken wretch of her own sex, who be sometimes see in the town secretly beg of she, and cry to she; a woman work, ever work, but content to do it, and prefer to do it as her natural lot, until she should be too old to labour any more? do Louisa see this? such a thing be to be.
a lonely brother, many thousand of mile away, writing, on paper blot with tear, that her word have too soon come true, and that all the treasure in the world would be cheaply barter for a sight of her dear face? at length this brother come nearer home, with hope of see she, and be delay by illness; and then a letter, in a strange hand, say 'he die in hospital, of fever, such a day, and die in penitence and love of you: his last word be your name'? do Louisa see these thing? such thing be to be.
herself again a wife - a mother - lovingly watchful of her child, ever careful that they should have a childhood of the mind no less than a childhood of the body, as know it to be even a more beautiful thing, and a possession, any hoard scrap of which, be a blessing and happiness to the wise? do Louisa see this? such a thing be never to be.
but, happy Sissy's happy child love she; all child love she; she, grow learn in childish lore; think no innocent and pretty fancy ever to be despise; try hard to know her humbler fellow-creature, and to beautify their life of machinery and reality with those imaginative grace and delight, without which the heart of infancy will wither up, the sturdy physical manhood will be morally stark death, and the plainest national prosperity figure can show, will be the writing on the Wall, - she hold this course as part of no fantastic vow, or bond, or brotherhood, or sisterhood, or pledge, or covenant, or fancy dress, or fancy fair; but simply as a duty to be do, - do Louisa see these thing of herself? these thing be to be.
dear reader! it rest with you and I, whether, in our two field of action, similar thing shall be or not. let they be! we shall sit with light bosom on the hearth, to see the ash of our fire turn gray and cold.
